THE RED PAGE
PARNASSUN, M.L.
A REMARKABLY high level of
poetic excellence characterises W.
F. Alexander and A. E. Currie’s
anthology of New Zealand Verse,
which has made its appearance in The Can-
terbury Poets series, published by Walter
Scott, London—an astonishingly cheap shil-
lingsworth. Comparing this collection with
the recently published Anthology of Aus-
tralian Verse, it must be admitted that the
M.L. verse makes a more distinctive national
appeal, while its general level of poetic
power is no whit lower than the concise an-
thology of Bertram Stevens. Doubtless it is
easier to make a characteristic collection of
verse when confined within the limits of
one small State; but credit must be given
to the high level of poetry produced by that
State. Maoriland is a nation of minor
poets. It is true that it is favored By being
a compact and isolated group, with a history
of its own; it must be much easier to feel
the inspiration of an island than of a wedge
of a continent, separated from its neighbors
merely by arbitrary lines upon a map. Aus-
tralia has not yet found a poet who has felt
the inspiration of being a New South Welsh-
man ; and it will be necessary to wait till the
unification of the Commonwealth is consu-
mated before the poet of Australia appears.
Meantime a community that, seventy years
a g°, did not exist, and now does not number
a million souls, has so exuberantly flowered
into verse that the editors of New Zealand
Verse were able to cull no less than 170
poems, the blossoms of a crowded garden of
55 poets. And though, in Maoriland as in
Australia, everybody writes poetry—except
editors, whose time is fully taken up reading
and rejecting it—the level of literary excel-
lence retained in the M.L. anthology gives the
island-State pre-eminence in poetic worth.
Surely one of the unnamed peaks of the
Southern Alps might be called Parnassus.
In their naive introduction the young edi-
tors, obsessed by the scenic beauty of the
islands, unnecessarily apologise for the
anthology being an anthology. They have a
hazy idea that it ought to have been a guide-
book in verse. The sole object of an antholo-
gist should be the maintenance of the highest
possible poetic standard. The editors doubt
the existence of a distinctive school
in Maoriland verse, with the possible
exception of a school of “landscape”
poetry. Undoubtedly in this anthology
there is evidence of a difference in taste that
severs Maoriland as sharply from the Com-
monwealth as her 1200 miles of sea. The
island bards distinctly favor the more classical
forms— e.g., blank verse and sonnets, to the
looser rolling, seven-foot iambic couplets
favored by Lawson and the Australian
“ horse ” school. This preference is partly
due to the overshadowing influence of Domett,
whose work is of the time, and under the
influence, of Browning. Domett may not
be read by the modern Maoriland public,
but he is read by the Maoriland. poet.
This predisposition toward the classic forms
is also partly due to the leaven of cul-
ture that consciously characterised the early
colonising schemes. A tradition of learning
has always been in the land.
There are evidences, too, of other
“schools,” in this little island-group
seething with poetic inspiration. The scenery
of Maoriland has wrung tribute from Domett
to Reeves, the sea has its call for these sea-
dwellers, and in this most remote of all the
countries of the globe it is natural to find the
song of the exile, as in Mary Colborne-Yeel’s
verses, and of the fervid patriot, as in a host
of others. But it is in the Maorilander’s
firiceless heritage of Maori and South Sea
egend, as strangely beautiful, as richly in-
spiring as the mythology of the Greeks, that
the island-State will find its unique place in
the literature of the world. Certain of the
Maoriland writers, notably Domett, Jessie
Mackay and Arthur H. Adams, have already
done good pioneer work in this subject.
The scheme of the anthology, allowing a
varied representation of the chief writers,
most of whom have allotted to them seven or
eight poems, permits a more precise judgment
of their merits than was possible in the
smaller space necessarily given them by Ber-
tram Stevens in his Australian anthology.
The group of women writers, chiefly in
Christchurch, the literary centre of the
State, is admirably represented by the
namesof B. E. Baughan, MaryColborne-Veel,
Jessie Mackay, Mary H. Poynter, Mary E.
Richmond, Dora Wilcox, and Annie Glenny
Wilson. One other contributor, Arnold Wall,
demands further notice in a subsequent
issue.
THREE NOVELS
Following the method of zoology, we have
divided Literature, the branch of the creative
arts with which we have specially to deal, into
three classes, K. L. M. —Knowledge, Litera-
ture, and Mud. Each of these classes we may
further divide into orders. Thus, the class,
Knowledge, may be subdivided into the
orders of theology, philosophy, history, edu-
cation, science, art, music, etc. Literature
proper may be split into the three orders of
poetry, drama, and fiction. And Mud, the
remnant class, may be subdivided into novels-
that-are-not-novels, guide-books, remarks by
Mr. T. Crosland, and other ineffectivenesses.
Then each of these orders may in turn be
divided into families, and—should it be
necessary for further identification—the
classification may be continued down through
genus to species, and even to variety.
Discarding zoology, we will, for the pre-
sent, confine ourselves to one of the three
orders of Literature proper—fiction. Let us
take three recent novels and scientifically
classify them. White Fang, by Jack London
(Macmillan, Lond., per Sydney Book Club),
we may place in the order of animal stories ;
family, wolf. The House of Islam, by Mar-
maduke Pickthall (Methuen, Lond.), is in
the order of studies of a civilisation ; family,
Turkish; genus, lacking love-interest;
species, religious. The Salving of a Derelict,
by Maurice Drake (T. W. Laurie, Loud.),
would be included in the order of popular
novels; family, adventure; genus, strenu-
ous ; species, cockney; variety, happy-ending.
For further classification we may also
divide fiction into, say, four grades of lite-
rary merit. These we may label FI, F 2,
F 3, F 4. Then White Fang, considered
solely as literature, would be branded “ ani-
mal story, FI ” ; The House of Islam would
be properly pigeon-holed as “civilisation-
study, F 3 ” ; and The Salving of a Derelict
would bear the illuminative tag “popular
novel, F 4.”
Below F 4 would begin the M’s ; the Ml
class Corelli and Caine would almost have to
themselves; while in M 3 Boothby and Nat
Gould would jostle with a host of crowding
competitors. And below M 3 there would
yet be the bottom ooze of literary mud,
where, with the brand “M 4 ” upon their
foreheads, nameless poets, compilers of
“How to Behave in Society,” and the
anonymous authors of Donf’s, would for ever
writhe. It is questionable, however, where
even in M 4 the utmost depths of literary mud
have been reached, and it may be necessary to
create an even muddier and oozier class,
labelled Slush 1, for the reception of those
feebly crawling things of the deeps who
chronicle the habits of the pet poodles of
aristocracy.
It would evidently save the reading public
and the non-reading reviewers much per-
plexity if the Government were to appoint
literary graders to examine and test all
literary produce as it left the publishers,
and in big black letters to affix their brand
to each volume. The Ml class of readers
would then be in no danger of getting
Maeterlinck for Marie; librarians could
place opposite their subscribers’ names the
figures that would designate their literary
desire; and literary criticism could be left to
sub-editors. But this development of State-
socialism is not in our time, and we must
ourselves—not altogether reluctantly—do the
necessary branding.
In White Fang, animal-story, FI, London
has triumphantly done a difficult thing. He
has repeated a success; he has added a sequel
to The Gall of the Wild. His first animal-
study had an air of novelty that is necessarily
lacking in this story. Yet the facile London,
in the midst of a ceaseless literary activity—
his output, covering a short space of years,
already comprises a dozen hastily compiled
books—has been able to invest White Fang
a cub three parts wolf and one part dog
with all the interest that the reader felt
in London’s earlier canine hero, Buck. In
truth, London's dogs are much more life-like
than his men. The Gall of the Wild showed
a domesticated dog inevitably giving up his
acquired tameness for his almost forgotten
ancestral habits. White Fang might almost
be titled The Recall of the Wild. It shows
the gradual taming of a wolf, who repeats
in one short life-time the story of his race
that began as wolf and ends as dog.
The cub had never seen man, yet the instinct
concerning man was his. In dim ways he recog-
nised in man the animal that had fought itself to
primacy over the other animals of the wild. Not
alone out of his own eyes, but out of the eyes of
all his ancestors was the cub now looking upon
man—out of eyes that had circled in the darkness
around countless winter camp-fires, that had
peered from safe distances, and from the hearts of
thickets at the strange, two-legged animal that
was lord over living things. The spell of the cub’s
heritage was upon him ... too compelling for
a wolf that was only a cub. He cowered
down in a paralysis of fear, already half pre-
ferring the submission that his kind had pre-
ferred from the first time a wolf came in to sit by
man’s fire and be made warm.
The story opens dramatically with the
half-dog, half-wolf mother of the pup run-
ning down two human beings conveying a
dead man upon a sledge through the wilds
of Alaska. Then the cub is born, and his
education begins.
He began to accompany his mother on the
meat-trail, and he saw much of the killing of
meat and began to play his own part in it. And
in his own dim way he learned the law of meat...
The aim of life was meat, Life itself was meat,
Life lived on life. There were the eaters and the
eaten. The law was: Eat or be eaten. He did
not formulate the law in clear, set terms and
moralize about it. He did not even think
the law; he merely lived the law without
thinking about it at a 11.... Had the cub
thought in man-fashion, he might have epi-
tomised life as a voracious appetite, and the
world as a place wherein raged a multitude of
appetites, pursuing and being pursued, hunting
and being hunted, eating and being eaten, all in
blindness and confusion, with violence and dis-
order, a chaos of gluttony and slaughter, ruled
over by chance, merciless, planless, endless.
White Fang meets man—a band of In-
dians, instantly recognises them as gods,
and learns servitude.
He felt the justice of the man animals, and he
knew them for what they were—makers of law
and executors of law. Also, he appreciated the
power with which they administered the law.
Unlike any animals he had ever encountered,
they did not bite nor claw. They enforced their
live strength with the power of dead things.
Dead things did their bidding. Thus, sticks and
stones, directed by these strange creatures, leaped
through the air like living things, inflicting
grievous hurts. To his mind this was power un-
usual, power inconceivable and beyond the natu-
ral, power that was god-like. White Fang, in
the very nature of him, could never know any-
thing about gods;. . . but the wonder and awe
that he had of these man-animals in ways re-
sembled what would be the wonder and awe of
man at sight of some celestial creature, on a
mountain top, hurling thunderbolts from either
hand at an astonished world.
The man-animals were gods unmistakable and
unescapable.... He belonged to them as all dogs
belonged to them. His actions were theirs to
command. His body was theirs to maul, to stamp
upon, to tolerate. Such was the lesson that was
quickly borne in upon him. It came hard, going
as it did, counter to much that was strong and
dominant in his own nature ; and while he dis-
liked it in the learning of it, unknown to himself
he was learning to like it. It was a placing of his
destiny in another’s hands, a shifting of the
responsibilities of existence. This in itself was
compensation.
So from these gods he comes to greater
gods—the white men. The battles to the death
that the dog—exhibited as * ‘ the fighting wolf ”
by his depraved master—is compelled to fight,
are told with a deliberate grimness; the cul-
minating fight between White Fang and a
bull-dog is almost homeric. Then follows
his meeting with the god who was to conquer
his fierceness by love, and the story of this
last taming shows no falling-off in graphic
power or interest. Unhesitatingly we can
place White Fang with London’s earlier dog-
story in the class FI.
The House of Islam, F 3, owing to the
strangeness of its subject, is more difficult to
“ place.” The life and ideals of the Turk
are so remote from the ordinary channels of
our modern civilisation that the book in-
terests rather as a study than as a piece of
literature. It is difficult to criticise, no
matter how consistently the work is carried
out, a book dealing with a civilisation that,
for instance, regards women as trivial toys,
and allows a saintly sheykh, as a matter of
duty, to take as his wife an old and hideous
slave who had been admitted to his harem
merely to wait upon his daughter. The story
of the two brothers, one a saint and the
other a self-seeking great official, moves with
a quiet inevitability to its close. An insight
to the Turkish mind is furnished by the
letter of the great Abd-ur-Rahman Bek,
written to his brother, the sheykh, when the
latter’s son relinquishes his ambitions to re-
turn to his father’s obscurity.
Though I grieve much for the loss of a youth
whom I had come to regard as my son (a thing I
could never procure of my own body, though I
have tried many women and begotten at divers
times no less than sixteen daughters, if I count
aright; so that I have come to hate girl-children
as a deception, and utterly fail to comprehend
thy infatuation for that girl who died), yet I will
not disguise from thee that the withdrawal of the
young one has done me good in my position. It
is but policy in the Sovereign to look askance on
the posterity of men of note; and that custom
of the Turks is wise by which honors descend not
from father to son. The eyes of many in high
places regarded with disfavor my fondness for
Abd-ur-Rahman; but now, an old man without
a sequel, I arouse no hatred, only expectation.
What am I, thus lonely, but a kind of eunuch, a
natural and unenviable appanage of sovereignty.
The Salving of a Derelict, F 4, is a prize
novel, and is deliberately prize -novelish.
The offer of a prize of £lOO by a certain
notorious London half-penny paper for a
novel by a new author attracted 600 com-
petitors, and Maurice Drake’s efforts
emerged. In the 699 unsuccessful manu-
scripts there may have been a work of
genius, but against the almost shameless
excellencies of the winning manuscript that
work of genius was obviously doomed to
oblivion. If the youthful writer wants to
know the sort of novel that will invariably
find a publisher, let him study this ex-
and, if he values his art, avoid
imitating it. It is a story built to be
serialised ; every chapter closes with a clang.
She spun on her heel and struck him full m
the mouth with all her strength. “Go * ” she
oried, and choked. “ Go. I pray God I’ll never
see vour wicked, lying face again.” She burst
into hysterical tears and ran from the room.
(To be continued in our next.)
“Run up some sort of a flag half-mast,” he
called to the bridge. He jerked his thumb
towards the cabin ; the man at the wheel growled
a surly assent, and Laurence went below again to
the compftny of the shrouded bundle on his
table.
(To be continued in our next.)
But her lips were smiling, though her eyes were
full of tears. Beneath their wet and fluttering
lashes they looked bravely into his own. And—
“ And must I ask you myself, then, Laurence
Averil 1 ” she said.
(The End.)
The persons of the novel are few, and melo-
dramatically sketched. Their characteristics
are easily grasped, their actions obvious.
The plot is a simple one, early allowing the
?ractised novel-reader a guess at its ending.
'he hero is a human brute—the strong,
wicked man of youthful femininity’s auda-
cious dream; the heroine, like so many
heroines of popular fiction, merely dresses
beautifully, weeps appropriately, and is
charmingly stupid. Still, the story, with
the exception of an excrescent first chapter,
is compact and neatly rounded. There is
nothing new in theme or development;
Maurice Drake has read his popular authors
too conscientiously for that. And he has
got £lOO for his manuscript, which is more
than 99 per cent, of authors get for a first
novel. Let the youthful author read Drake
—and beware :. . . F 4.

Bards are reminded that their Rubaiyats of
Australia (prize, one guinea) must be forwarded to
this office before Feb. Ist.

Constance A. Barnicoat, in Deo. Nineteenth
Centwry, summarises the reading of the colonial
girl from answers to questions sent to schools
throughout the Empire. She finds the colonial
girl older for her years than the British girl.
When sixteen and seventeen, she tends to put
away such girlish things as Mrs. L. T. Meade,
and long before this age she scorns girls’
magazines. The colonial girl has a stronger
preference than her British cousin for boys’
stories. The South African girl is specially
devoted to books of the hair-breadth escape and
the blood-curdling adventure, which leaves her no
time for The Story of a South African Farm. With
Australian girls Ethel Turner is immensely popular
—also Boldrewood, Boothby, Gordonand Paterson.
The verses they like best are The Man from
Snowy River , The Sick Stookrider, Admirals All
(Newbolt), and The Recessional (Kipling). Barrie
is much more read by British than by colonial
girls, “ for whom he is probably both too Scotch
and too sentimental.” Of magazines the favorite
with colonial girls is (alack !) the Strand.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the Autumn Days.
Where the plovers cry at dawn,
Where the wild swans fly
Like a dark line swiftly drawn
Through a pink-flushed sky ;
Where the crumpled wavelet gleams
In the golden sweet sun rays,
There the Lone Grey Water dreams
Thro’ the Autumn days;
Shining out across the plain,
Like a sea of glass
When the daylight fails again,
When the sunsets pass.
Mystic when the silver beams
Make a shimmery starlight blaze,
There the Lone Grey Water dreams
In the Autumn days.
When the brown hills turn to green
Out beyond the plain,
And a thousand rills are seen
In a sparkling rain,
Comes a silence sweet and calm,
Falling on the winding ways,
Falling like a soothing balm
In the Autumn days.
Where the cattle-track winds down
In the twilight gloom,
Through the bracken, green and brown,
Where the dark pines loom.
All the winds are fast asleep,
All the world is wrapt in haze;
Nature has a peace to keep
In the Autumn days.
Through the golden afternoon,
While the swift hours run,
Eastward gleams a ghost-white moon,
Westward droops the sun.
Through the rippled reed-bed stream’s
Tangled, sunny, shadowy maze,
There the Lone Grey Water dreams
In the Autumn days.
Willow leaves are fluttering down,
She-oaks softly sigh;
All the earth wears green and brown,
Grey and blue the sky.
Magic there in soft light teems,
Haunting all the shining ways
Where the Lone Grey Water dreams
In the Autumn days.
VIC.
KATHLEEN WALKER.
98° in the Shade.
O to lie naked beside the sea !
Afar from the city to dream and roam,
Where wave-curls whiten and sea-mews glee
On shelving beaches a-wash with foam ;
0 to lie naked beside the sea !
Red drought is breathing a westerly,
The pavements shimmer and blaze with light,
And e’en the sparrow, a refugee
Beneath the eaves, is too limp for flight;
O to lie naked beside the sea !
White dust and smoke-drift are choking me ;
I think of a sprinkly pellucid pool,
Of dripping caverns, where one may flee,
And ocean murmurs and sea-wind cool;
0 to lie naked beside the sea !
O cool wet sand and shadowed lee !
The flies are sticky, and buzz like fun,
The dust-fiends dance a corroboree—
Ye gods ! will ever my work be done—
O to lie naked beside the sea !
I’d lock my office and fling the key
To Sheol —I speak from my inmost soul—
But who may wrangle when destiny
Pursues poor humans from pole to pole ?
O to lie naked beside the sea !
0 set, ye fiends, the south-wind free,
And freshen earth with the sweet sea-smell
(’Tis 98, by the saints that be,
And Satan’s loosing his brood from hell) ;
0 to lie naked beside the sea !
At last! Hence, inkpot! I’ve earned my fee,
O welcome chiming, ’tis half-past four !
I’ll foot it now to the busy Quay,
Where smoke-trails stream to the ocean-sfiore;
0 to lie naked beside the sea !
N-S . w .
W. M. WHITNEY.
The Tree of Stars.
One night I dreamed that all the stars
On unseen branches grew,
And thro’ the night they sparkled bright
To ev’ry wind that blew !
I dreamed upon that mystic tree
I climbed amidst the skies,
And you, my dear, were standing near
With wonder-laden eyes.
I shook the branches one by one
Till stars and planets fell
Down— gaily down on bush and town,
Where all my kindred dwell.
I shook the branches one by one
Till every twig was bare,
And “ Girl o’ Mine, those gems are thine,’
I said, “ for thee to wear ! ”
But ah, it was a dismal place
For one lone man to bide ;
I gazed below, and turned to go ;
“ Good luck !” the seraphs cried.
You stared into the naked slues,
Commingling joy and pain, _
Until I said “ Be comforted—
The stars will grow again !
N.s.w.

AN entirely new design by Mr. Morse is
here illustrated for conveying wool bales
from the mammoth new warehouse of
Dalgety & Co., Ltd., Miller’s Point, to the
wharf, by gravity, through steel tubes. The
outer tubes (one being over 200 feet long) con-
vey to points 370 feet apart. An open steel-
work column, on trachyte base, supports each
tube at centre of roadway. The whole con-
struction presents a striking and unique
appearance.
The Hydraulic Lifts and Wool Dumping
Machinery in the same warehouse were also
constructed from Mr. Morse’s specifications
and designs in conjunction with Mr. James
Kidd, the firm’s engineer.
Communications hy letter or wire will receive
prompt attention.

How to A ddress Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
qnyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persom instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.
S/ierial Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor will carefully rpad and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence” column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dialing with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
two columns'in length, or, say, 8000 ivords, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. ivhen the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed But no
MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is forwarded without stamps
for return it will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, farther, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—ivill be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under ivhich
such artistic contributions will be received are those,
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, JANUARY 24, 1907.
WANTED, AN ELECTIVE MINISTRY.
The old Australian Parliament which recently
laid its bones in the sepulchre was a Legislature
of three parties. The new one will be a Legis-
lature of at least three parties—probably four, for
a section of the Anti-Sosh crowd is bitterly
dissatisfied with the alleged leadership of a
Yes-No who is never there to lead, and there is a
fair chance that a large section of it will break
away and follow Irvine, and thus make two
Anti-Sosh parties. It is no use weeping over
these facts and crying out for a return to “ the
two-party system and true Constitutional Govern-
ment.” That sort of thing is a mere crying for
the moon, and it is all the more futile since the
Foreign-trade Tory crowd, which demands a
return to the two-party system, is itself respon-
sible, by its refusal to become Protectionist and
Democratic, for the existence of a third party.
The two-party system was only possible
so long as politicians had no principle
save the desire for office, and so long as
the only parties were the Ins and the Outs.
We have advanced beyond that unwholesome
and immoral stage of politics. Already men,
through thinking for themselves, have evolved
three distinct political factions and some frag-
ments, and when they improve and think still
more for themselves, they may constitute five
parties, or even thirteen. Unfortunately the pre-
sent system of Party Government only works
where there are two parties and no more. Now
that it is a misfit and obsolete, the great need of
the moment is for a cleaner system of government
adapted to the cleaner conditions of tne day—not
for a return to an antiquated and unprincipled
Parliamentary method so as to avoid disarranging
the antiquated and illogical Ministerial system
which fitted in with it. When the table has one
leg longer than the other, there are two alterna-
tives —either to make a hole in the floor to accom-
modate the extra-long leg, or to improve the table.
The crowd which cries hopelessly for a return to
the two-party method demands that the floor
shall be spoiled to adapt it to the diseased table;
the more progressive party would rather have a
sound table that would suit a sound floor. If the
Australian Parliament is to have a chance—any
kind of a chance—to get on with business, it needs
to do away with the superannuated policy of
Party Government, and replace it by Elective
Ministries.
• * • »
The Elective Ministry, like many other busi-
ness-like political ideas, is of Swiss origin. When
a new Parliament meets it elects, one by one, the
members of the Cabinet, and, barring such mis-
conduct as might fairly justify their expulsion
from Parliament, they remain fixed for the
duration of that Parliament. There are at least
eight different points on which this method can
claim a vast superiority over the present hap-
hazard, illogical, and unworkable policy :
(1) Under the present system an Opposition, which may
be a mere handful, elects a leader, possibly by a majority
of one, and if his party grows and becomes a majority,
that man inherits the Premiership automatically, though
in the first place he was the nominee of a very small
minority. The elective system makes the Premier the
direct choice of a majority of the House.
(2) The elective system makes his colleagues the choice
of the House instead of merely the nominees of the
Premier.
(3) Under the elective system the Ministry doesn’t re-
quire “ the confidence of the House ” in the present
absurd sense—that is to say, it doesn’t require to have
behind it a party which will back it up in almost every-
thing, whether it is right or wrong. It isn’t expected to
resign if its Bills are defeated any more than a private
member resigns when his private Bill is defeated. It
is expected to administer its departments honestly
and capably ; but with regard to the passing of new laws
the House may reject or amend out of shape the whole
Ministerial policy without thereby implying that the
Ministry should resign. Thus a strongly Protectionist
House might, in the midst of a fiscal battle, appoint a
Ministry that was almost wholly Freetrade, if it hap-
pened that half-a-dozen of the most capable adminis-
trators were Freetraders, and if it then proceeded to
double the tariff it would cast no reflection on the Minis-
try by doing so.
(4) The Ministry doesn’t need to hunt for immoral alli-
ances to keep it in office, or give concessions in return for
support. It is fixed for the term of Parliament whether
it gets support or not, and it can’t give much in the way
of concessions, because, as often as not, it has no definite
party behind it to guarantee the fulfilment of its bar-
gain.
(5) The Ministry need not be of one mind internally,
and no. Minister need sell his convictions for the sake of
unity in the Cabinet. If the Premier introduces the
Bill for Giving Everything to Everybody the Treasurer
may speak and vote against it if he pleases, and the
House decides. The Treasurer’s duty to his chief con-
sists in being a good, honest Keeper of the Public Cash,
and nothing more.
(6) The House votes on public measures according to
its conscience, if it has one. Under the present system a
member often votes against a Bill which he thinks is
good, because it is a Government Bill and it is his busi-
ness, as a party man of the niost direadful description, to
get the Government out by defeating its Bill; or else he
votes for a measure which he knows is bad because he is
a party man of the worst character, and it is his business
to keep the Government in by passing its Bill. If the
Government stayed in just the same whether its Bill
passed or not, there would be no inducement for any
member to vote against his conscience.
(7) There is little stonewalling under the Elective
Ministry system. Stonewalling is mostly an effort to get
rid of the Government by preventing the passage of its
measures, and when a Government is fixed for the life of
Parliament a great deal of the inducement to stonewall
fades away.
(8) Most important of all, the system of Elective
Ministries answers equally well whether there are two
parties or 13. There must be a majority—even if it is
only the majority of one supplied by the Speaker’s casting
vote—for or against every separate proposition laid be-
fore the House. No amount of subdivision into parties
can alter that fact. When the multiplicity of parties is
no longer allowed to block business by perpetually un-
seating the Ministry, there is really nothing that it
can do. Once the Ministry is fixed there are in practice
only two parties—the one that is for the Bill or clause or
proposition just then before the House, and the one that
is against it. There may be thirteen different parties
and different programmes at the election, but for
practical purposes there are not, and can’t be, more than
two at any one moment in the House.
• • • »
The reason why Great Britain hasn’t adopted
this simple and obvious device appears to be that
a strangely busted old tradition is in the way.
Until quite recently British Ministers were
appointed by the King to carry out, as best they
could, the King’s programme, whether they had a
majority in the House or not. (Previously they
weren’t in the House at all, and they were
appointed by the King to carry out his pro-
gramme, so far as they could do so, though
they might not have a single supporter in
Parliament.) Even William IV. believed that
he had the right to appoint Ministers and
keep them in office against the will of the
people and of Parliament, and Victoria is
said to have come to the throne with the remnant
of a vague belief in the same prerogative. Nomi-
nally the power of royal appointment survives,
and is in full bloom to this day. So the British
Parliament doesn’t elect its Ministry, because to
do so would be an inroad on the royal preroga-
tive —the prerogative that is dead, but which is
supposed to be still alive and in splendid health.
Between the fact that the British Parliament
mustn’t openly and directly appoint the Ministry,
because that is the King’s exclusive job, and
the fact that the King dare no longer do what
is supposed to be his exclusive job. Ministries
just happen or arrive or transpire. Similarly, in
Australia, Parliament has not hitherto elected
the Ministry, because the Governor is vaguely
supposed to appoint that body without any refer-
ence to Parliament at all. He never did anything
of the sort, but it is felt to be a fine old traditional,
constitutional sort of thing to go on supposing that
he does, just as the King is supposed to do in
England. Then the Ministry must have a
whole legislative programme of its own, be-
cause the King or his representative is sup-
posed to create the Cabinet in a high-
handed way, and hand it his own ready-made
programme to be shoved through willy-nilly. He
still professes, in the speech at the opening of
Parliament, that it is his programme, which is
another comic and frisky survival. And the
Ministry is supposed to resign if it is defeated on
any part of its programme, no matter if it is the
best Ministry that the House can produce,
apparently because the House, when it rejects
some Bill or other, is supposed to be asserting its
independence against the minions of the monarch
or the Viceroy; the measure is supposed to be the
King’s unpalatable measure, and the House is
supposed to be rising in wrath against the King’s
nominees. And these nominees have no fixed
term of office, partly because the King is
supposed to reserve the right to dismiss them
when he feels that way, and partly because the
House would rather go to gaol than let the King’s
Ministers, appointed by him without any refer-
ence to the popular will, have a definite lease of
their billets. And the Speaker, who is very
silent nowadays, is so called because he used to go
and speak to the sovereign about the dreadful
way his majesty went on, whereupon, as in the
case of Peter de la Mare, he was sometimes put
in a large stone receptacle. And the Speaker is
elected, though the Ministry isn’t, because the
Speaker was supposed, in the British long ago, to
assert the rights of the House against the
Ministers —the latter being the King’s minions
and quite apart from Parliament—and even, at
times, to demand that they be beheaded. He
was the House’s elected representative as dis-
tinct from Ministers, who were the potentate’s
agents. It is all a mass of curious and cumber-
some survivals and suppositions, wholly unsuited
to a state of things in which the Cabinet is
responsible to Parliament and the people, and to
no other power whatever.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Forrest and Gr. Reid Meet.
There is again a Federal political situation.
G. Reid, clutching at a straw, voyaged to Ade-
laide last week, met there a mass of sympathetic
meat in the person of John Forrest, and from
the mighty collision of Fat it is said the question
has come, “ Why not a Deakin-Reid alliance?”
It seems pretty evident, too, that the question
has been put to Mr. Deakin, and that that amiable
and extraordinary politician is “considering”
it with vast deliberation. An ordinarily
sensible man would have said, promptly
and publicly, “Rats!” to the first whisper
on the subject, and thus saved a good
deal of trouble. But Deakin is no ordinary man,
and, though in some regards almost a man of
genius, he is not always sensible. It will be quite
characteristic of him to give an amount of pon-
derous thought to the question of the possibilities
of an alliance with G. Reid, which the absurd
subject does not merit. The results of the
elections leave G. Reid practically an outcast
in the political world. In Victoria every candi-
date tainted by association with him was rejected.
In N.S. Wales his direct following was reduced
by 30 per cent. In no State was RsiD-ism
as a policy given countenance. It but requires
now another fairly strong, or only moderately
weak, candidate for the leadership of the so-
called “Anti-Sosh” party to make Reid’s
deposition certain, for those who have come back
look upon the mournful list of the lost, and blame
the bad tactics of the leader for the staggering
defeat that the party has suffered. An alliance with
Reid therefore represents a union with an Old
Man of the Sea—it means paying a big price for a
deficit, purchasing an injury at a valuation, or
something equally senseless. Only two very fat
and very vain men, like Reid and Forrest, could
have hatched the idea of a Deakin-Reid alliance.

Resuscitating the Apparently Impure.
It is an axiom of good government that its com-
ponent parts should not only be pure, but should
appear pure. Not the most intimate friends of
the Victorian Labor party can glance back at its
attitude towards John Wren and Bung, in
connection with the Gambling and Licensing
Acts in the last session of Parliament, and con-
tend that it appears spotless to the unprejudiced
eye. Every friend of Labor must admit that two
great enemies of Democracy are gambling and
drink. The individual dominated by either of
them is a potential enemy to the worker. It is
only a matter of time before he gambles or drinks
himself into such a condition that he is willing,
for money or liquid refreshment, to become the
pimp or the dupe of any Trust, or monopolist,
or Tory that comes along. Yet, instead of
lending a hand towards the regulation of
these evils, the Victorian Labor party—or rather
the majority of its members, for there were a
handful who took an independent stand—fought
as they had never fought for any principle pre-
viously, in the interests of John, of the Colling-
wood tote, and Bung, the big brewer. No
factories legislation, no measure aimed to prevent
the mutilation or the poisoning of the working
classes, ever aroused these Laborites to greater en-
thusiasm. With wild fanaticism they worshipped,
with Wren’s tote-sheet for their confession of
faith, and Bung’s beer-pump for their totem.
Worse than all that, they succeeded in
what appeared to be a deliberate endeavor
to stonewall the Gambling Bill, in order
that it might not come into force until after John
Wren had scooped the mighty profits to be derived
from the Melbourne Cup meeting. It is quite
possible that the Labor party was actuated by the
highest motives—that it aimed simply at per-
fecting two most important measures, and that it
feared lest the passage of these measures with-
out due criticism might inspire Judkins to
further excesses. If so, its methods were so ill-
guided that it is easy to comprehend why the
party should be cruelly maligned at the present
time, not only by opponents, but by a large
section of those who have voted Labor solidly in
the past. So large is this section that the Labor
party’s chances at the approaching Victorian
State elections look worse than they have been
for years. If the Political Labor League wishes
to score —and, apart from its personnel, there
is no reason why the party should not come back
25 per cent, stronger —it should completely revise
its list of candidates. Hitherto it has been an
unwritten rule with all political parties that,
under ordinary conditions, the retiring member
should be again chosen. This time, the rule
should be reversed, and the fact that a candidate
was a member of the Victorian Labor party of
1903-1906 should be a reason why he should be
passed out, unless he can prove satisfactorily by
his speeches and votes in the House that he was
an exception and not like the bulk of the
party. Speaking broadly, the present Labor
party should be sent about its business; and,
with a few exceptions, an entirely new col-
lection of candidates chosen. By giving people
an opportunity to level charges against the
party, and identify it with Wren and the
drink traffic, the sitting members have done
their cause incalculable injury. It is not sug-
gested that they were guilty of corruption, but
men who commit such shocking blunders in
tactics, and have such a capacity for looking cor-
rupt without receiving the money, are too unlucky
to call for anybody’s support. There is a point at
which ill-luck becomes a crime.
4.
N.S.W. State Politics.
It is announced that certainly two, and probably
three, members of the N.S.W. Ministry will, so
to speak, “cheat the hangman” and retire from
office and from Parliament before the State
general elections. Mr. Ashton and Mr. O’Conor
are certain to do so; Mr. Carruthers isn’t quite
so definite about his intentions, but he probably
will also refuse to face the electors again. Such a
decision shows prudence, if not courage. There
is not the least doubt that with adequate opposi-
tion the Carruthers Government and its fol-
lowing will be cleared out of political life as soon
as the people can get at them ; and even if such
half-hearted and half-sensible opposition as it has
had in the past continues for the future, the
Cabinet will be in a position of grave peril when
it comes before the electors. The Labor party in
N.S.W. State Parliament has done little to earn
the confidence of democrats. At the last election
it refused to adopt an honest financial policy. In
Parliament it refused to be bound even to the
half-honest policy it had put before the electors.
In the matter of the Lands scandals it was clear
that the party had touched pitch and been de-
filed. It was not compromised to anything
like the extent of the party which found the
chief culprits, and the present reigning party
which shielded them ; but on some of its members
the taint of scandal showed, and the party hadn’t
the courage to act sternly towards them; and
the party was lacking in either the inclination or
the capacity to put the public will into effect and
force a full inquiry and an adequate punishment
Nevertheless, as compared with the Carruthers
party—the gang of extraordinary coincidenters
who have made the name of Justice a shrieking
farce in the State—the Labor party may
the verdict of the people. And if—as may hap-
pen—that party should, before the elections, put
its affairs into order and appeal to the public on a
real Reform platform, the fate of the Carruthers
party would be that of Kingston in Jamaica.
The Ministers who have resolved to sidle out
doubtless consider that far better than waiting
to be kicked out.

The Queensland Situation.
A Queensland Laborite begs for publication of
the following “to assist the cause of reasonable
Democracy in Queensland. The moderate Labor
party has practically no press representation, and
it looks to The Bulletin as its only effective
mouthpiece in Queensland ” :
The attitude of the moderate section of the Queensland
Parliamentary Labor party and its Democratic allies is
habitually misrepresented by the Tory press and the
Worker. In the first place, the Brisbane Worker is an
utterly impossible political guide for any party saddled
with responsibility. Its clamor for an extreme Socialistic
objective scares away thousands of decent Democrats;
its foolish references to “ gods and dogs ” alienate the
religious element among the Queensland working men ;
its inane policy with regard to Protection has been a
stumbling block to the Queensland Labor movement. It
is of no use mincing matters at this juncture. The
Queensland political position is so precarious that only
one thing can save it, and that is straight-speaking to the
reasonable Labor men of Queensland. Here are a few
examples of the kind of utterances (from the Worker)
that are ruining the Queensland Labor movement:—
“ Where Labor men and women of every faith and want
of faith assemble together, the order of the day should
be, ‘No gods or dogs ’ admitted.”
“ Wanted, a Savior ; no gods need apply.”
“When the Labor movement has to turn to God for
help it will be God help it indeed.”
With singular appropriateness, all this pitiful balder-
dash was published on the first of April, 1905. Whatever
may be the ethical merits of the above utterances, there
can be only one opinion as to the utter foolishness of
printing this kind of thing in a paper which is regarded
as a representative journal of Labor. Only a few months
ago the Socialist organ regaled its readers with this
delightful pabulum:—
“ Nothing less than the shock of the bomb-shell will
set the people’s mental apparatus in motion.”
“The way of Social salvation is not the ballot-box,
where the people are fooled into a slavery worse than
any known before on earth, but the swift doom, the flung
death, spreading consternation among rulers, toppling
over thrones and altars, bursting open the Gates of
Bondage, and setting the people free.”
When the present coalition was formed the Worker
supported it, professedly because that paper saw therein a
means to “One adult, one vote.” Now, when thatgreatre-
form has been attained, the Labor organ denounces the
Government that gave it and informs the people that the
way of social salvation is “ not the way of the ballot-box,
where the people are fooled into a slavery worse than
death.” True, the editor wrote this, not in a leading
article, but over his well-known pen-name of “Touch-
stone ” ; but it passes out to the world, and is used by
the Tory press as an expression of the genuine opinions
of a Labor journal. Another most objectionable thing,
from the standpoint of an Australian Democrat, is the
following dictum on the fiscal question
“The colored alien’s goods, if of sound quality and
clean, can do us no possible harm ; the colored alien him-
self is a moral plague in our midst and a degrader of
labor. . . But, it is urged, the colored alien’s goods
undersell our own, and in that way cheapen our labor
and throw our workers out of employment, just the same
as though he were here in direct competition with them.
In reply to which the challenged Laborite, who is not a
fiscalist, has no option but to flatly say, ‘ It is not true.’ ”
Here is a plain, undisguised advocacy of the employ-
ment of colored labor as a policy for Australian Demo-
crats. If every manufacture in N.S. Wales, Queensland,
and Victoria were wiped out to-morrow and supplanted
by goods from Hongkong, Calcutta or Japan, the
Worker would smile contentedly and say, “ The colored
alien’s goods can do us no harm.” And the amazing part
of it all is this—that the vast majority of the unionists of
Western Queensland are strong Protectionists and yet
allow their funds to be spent in the propagation of
Freetrade and cheap - labor fallacies of this kind.
It is the kind of stuff quoted above, and the utter lack of
political nous, that allows its publication that has
resulted in the present precarious condition of Labor in
Queensland. Queensland has at present to face this posi-
tion. The question is not will it have a pure Labor
Government or a capitalistic administration. The choice
is simply this : Will it take the Kidston regime, with its
record of successful finance and democratic legislation, or
go back to the old Philp party with its Tory prejudices,
its unspeakable finance, its policy of drift, its hatred of
reform, and its undying enmity of progress. 'Hie
Worker, with the full knowledge of all that Philp-isdi
means, savs deliberately “ Give us Philp.” With Philp
back in power the Worker can lie back comfortably, be
saddled with no responsibility, and fill its columns week
by week with the cheap diatribes its soul loveth. But
the Queensland Laborites will suffer.

The Hard Mozzle of the Freetrade Journalist.
The obvious instruction to the men who write
the Freetrade articles in the Australian papers
nowadays is to shut their eyes to all that appears
in their own news columns, and rely wholly upon
imagination for their facts. On its page 4 Sydney
Evening News still professes to see virtue and
salvation in free imports, yet on one day last
week three columns of its other pages were busy
shouting at the top of their voice that page 4 is a
blithering idiot. First there was an article cn
“The Iron and Steel Boom.” Half a century
ago, it was pointed out, Great Britain produced
half the world’s iron ; this year it will produce
about 17 per cent. ‘‘ As iron producers, we held
first place until 1895, when the United States
got ahead of us ; in 1903 we lost second posi-
tion, being beaten by Germany. In other
words, under Protection Britain produced halt the
world’s iron ; then it decided to become a great
Freetrade nation, like Turkey the stagnant, and
it has dropped back to third place m iron, while
the two countries of High Protection have got for
their own people the work that Britain large y
used to have under Protection. True, British
iron-masters are busy just now; but (except on
page 4) the Evening News knows wh 7 • Frote . c

line thing. The t i>e day the Evening News
dug up another b ohority on the same point—
this time the N...vs’own “Special Correspon-
dent ’’ in Argentina. Said he :
McKay’s Sunshine strippers, from Ballarat, are getting
a splendid run in this part of the world. The majority
of the agricultural machinery, however, sold here is
American —and don’t they know how to charge, as com-
pared with their Australian prices ! These gigantic
Yankee concerns seem to make their profits here and to
spend them fighting the manufacturers of such machinery
in other parts. In all Argentina one will not meet with
an agricultural machinery works. They could not exist.
Machinery for agricultural purposes comes in on a very
low duty, or if imported in certain ways absolutely free,
while mechanics can command very high wages. Under
these conditions, no one is foolhardy enough to open out
in the above line. Australians should take the greatest
care to see that the Yankee manufacturer is not allowed
to cut the Australian manufacturer out of the market.
Such, if it did occur, would be a calamity in the end for
the Australian farmer. No sooner would the factories be
closed than the price of machinery would go up 5 per
cent., then 5 per cent, more, and so on, until probably
the prices charged here were reached.
Heaven and the Bennett family only know why
the Evening News should go to all this trouble
to prove that its own page 4 is a doddering absur-
dity ; the fact remains that it does so. Meantime
—and more or less by the way—it is interesting
to observe that Sydney Daily Telegraph, also
last week, had another of its imaginative articles
on “Mr. Deakin and the Australian Future,”
in which it remarked that “with similar
wrong-headedness Mr. Deakin’s Governments
have clogged internal production by a Protective
tariff that increases the cost of the tools of
primary industry.” The Australian tariff has
established McKay ; but “ in all Argentina one
will not meet with an agricultural machinery
works ” —and, consequently, in the words of the
Evening News’ Argentine correspondent, “ don’t
the American machinery importers know how to
charge compared with their Australian prices !”
That experience would take some squaring with
the Telegraph’s theory—that is, if the Free-
trade journalist attempted the impossible job of
squaring the world’s experience and his boss’s
theory.

The Perpetuation of Degenerates.
Dr. Norris, chief of Victoria’s Board of Public
Health, recently touched lightly on the fringe of
a very large question when he wrote the Chief
Secretary, urging that, before children treated in
the proposed school for the mentally defective
were allowed to marry, a certificate should be
furnished showing that they were mentally
effective. Of course, the school is not yet, and
the defectives who will be treated there will not
reach marriageable age for a decade or so
yet—but that is only the fringe. The question
itself is whether the State should not take in
hand the whole question of the propagation
of the mentally deficient, or defective or
abnormal. The State, or society generally,
has in the long run to support practically all
defectives and degenerates, and therefore has, it
is argued, the right to insist that they should not
be produced if such can be avoided. Defectives
retort that it is not their fault they are so, and
that, being here, they have a right to all the
pleasures of matrimony or “housekeeping,” and
that if they can’t support a wife, she can, and
very often does, support them. Thirdly, there is
no certainty that degenerate does produce de-
generate. Genius certainly does not produce
genius, though mediocrity often does. Shak-
speare’s parents were, so far as is known, neither
abnormally brilliant nor the reverse; and there
is no evidence that the progenitors of, say,
Charles Peace or Deeming were criminals
or degenerates of any sort. And is there
any proof that mental deficiency is more
perpetuative than physical infirmity? A man
born blind does not usually have blind children ;
nor does a woman with a weak heart or bandy
legs necessarily bear children similarly afflicted.
Finally, who is to decide who is mentally effec-
tive and who is deficient or degenerate ? Nearly
all the great men of the world have been declared
by some to be mad. And yet—and yet—as a
Board of Health member put it, “ in the breeding
of stock all care is taken to prevent unsoundness,
but nothing is done at all to keep the race of
human beings free from hereditary taint.”

Privy Council and High Court.
“Constitutionalist” throws some further light
on the big constitutional law issue arising out of
the Outtrim case :
The question as to whether Federal officers are liable
to a State income-tax in respect of their salaries earned
as Federal officers is the specific matter involved, but the
question decided was an infinitely wider one. Most
people who have watched this controversy have been
influenced by what lawyers call “ the merits ” involved in
the particular question; but the real matter—the
matter of vital importance to Australia as a whole —
is the underlying legal point: whether or not there
are in the Constitution implied restrictions on
the powers given by it to the Commonwealth
and the States in terms which are, on the face of them,
unrestricted. The High Court decided that there were
restrictions upon those apparently unrestricted powers,
and in the case of D’Emden v. Pkdder laid down the law
in these words : “ When a State attempts to give to its
legislative or executive authority an operation which, if
valid, would fetter, control, or interfere with the free
exercise of the legislative or executive power of the
Commonwealth, the attempt, unless expressly authorised
by the Constitution, is to that extent invalid and inopera-
tive.” In the Income-tax cases the Court repeated this
statement of the law, and applying the principle so laid
down in D’Emden v. Pedder, decided that the State of
Victoria had no power to tax Federal officers in respect
of their salaries as such. So that the decision in the
Income-tax cases was but an application of a very wide
and important principle, a fact which the man at the
corner has never really appreciated.
The Council did not decide that the principle
laid down by the High Court did not apply in the Income-
tax cases, but denied altogether the existence of any
such principle, saying that no such implied restrictions
were to be found in the Constitution Act as were found
there by the High Court; or in other words, that the
powers conferred on the States in general and unre-
stricted words were unrestricted in fact, and any
exercise of those powers was valid except in so far as it
was expressly forbidden by the Constitution Act. To
both Courts it is plain that there may be many cases of
clashing sovereignty between State and Commonwealth
which are not provided for in the Constitution Act, be-
cause they are not of the open and direct kind which are
expressly guarded against there. Of such instances of
clashing sovereignty the United States law reports are
full. The High Court says in effect: " If these clashes of
power are allowed to occur there will be nothing but con-
fusion. In such a state of affairs the Government of
a country by a Federal arrangement is impossible.
There must be some restriction on these powers which at
first sight are unrestricted. What, then, is that re-
striction?” They found the answer in the case of
M'Odlloch v. Maryland, decided long ago in the Supreme
Court of the United States in circumstances which ap-
peared to the High Court analogous to those with which
it was then faced. The Privy Council, on the other
hand, says: “ True, these clashes of power may occur,
but such was the intention of the British Legislature as
expressed by the Constitution Act.” The High Court
says such could never have been the intention ; the Privy
Council replies, “It was.” The High Court wished to
construe the Constitution in such a way as to make it a
workable one, and looked not only at the words but at
the subject matter. The Privy Council looked only at
the words.
In dismissing the notion of any analogy between the
Australian and American Constitutions, as suggested by
the Australian Chief Justice, the Privy Council says that
the analogy failed in every matter which was under de-
bate. In what respects? Their Lordships do not deign
to state, but content themselves with pointing out that
“no state of the Australian Commonwealth had the
power of independent legislation possessed by the States
of the American Union. Every Act of the Victorian Coun-
cil and Assembly required the assent of the Crown. . . .”
Then this is why the analogy fails 1 In the case of
the American States, there is no power above them but the
Constitution, and therefore it may well be that some im-
plied restriction should be called in aid to prevent an
indirect invasion of Federal authority on their part, but
in the case of the Australian States there is a power above
them whose consent is required to any proposed legisla-
tion before it can become law. And this is our security
against clashing sovereignty. Hence, it is unnecessary
in our case to call in aid any implied restriction, since re-
trictions of this sort can only be implied of necessity, and
here there is no necessity. This must be their Lord-
ships’ argument ; otherwise the distinction is of no
force and is merely irrelevant. The answer is that the
power of royal veto was exercised at the time of the
granting of the Constitution for entirely different pur-
poses, and that it was never contemplated by the British
Legislature when it granted us our Constitution that it
should be put to such a use ; that as a security in fact it
is miserably inadequate. The Governor of a State, in
giving the King’s assent, acts on the advice of his Minis-
ters, who will never advise against the power of the
State. In disallowing an enactment that has already
become law, the King acts on the advice of his
British Ministers, who cannot be expected to
know whether it invades Australian Federal authority.
But that is the reason why, according to the Privy
Council, the ground for implying restrictions as in the
case of the Constitution of the United States, does not
exist in our Constitution ; a reason narrow and technical
to a degree, based on the existence of a power of veto
which, though real enough as a legal power of the Crown,
is, when looked upon as a security against clashing
sovereignty between States and a Federation, a mockery.
What is the High Court now to do ? In which way lies
its duty? It seems to Australian Federalists that their
Honors should prove themsel res faithful to the opinions
they expressed m such unequivocal language when refus-
ing leave to appeal from their judg'ment to the Privy
Council. They should fol ow their own decision regard-
less of the consequences and deaf to the clamor of the
anti-Australian, anti-everything crowd. In such matters
it was intended that they should be supreme, and that
theirs should be the only voice. By the merest mistake
another voice is heard. The High Court’s voice should ring
out strong and clear, and not attempt any discreditable
unison. Australians, true Australians, will listen toit. The
effective remedy, however, lies with the Federal Parlia-
ment. There are three ways out of this difficulty. One
is to remove the Federal jurisdiction from the State Courts.
The second is the amendment of the Constitution Act so
as to prevent such appeals. The third, and, if it can be
managed, the most convenient, is to secure the alteration
of the Orders-in-Council dealing with appeals from the
State Courts to the Privy Council. The present British
Liberal Government might possibly be brought to do
this if it could be made to understand the greatness of
the difficulty that has arisen.

£lOOO a Tear.
A pboblem which Australia will have to face before
long is the necessity for a very large increase
in the salaries of Federal members. The figure
should be raised to at least £lOOO a year. The
present amount (£4OO a year) represents abject
poverty, and is equal in most cases to only about
£l5O a year in any other pursuit. There are cer-
tain travelling expenses to be deducted, in the
first place. For a member with a wife and family
—unless he be one of the very few who live handy
to the door of Parliament House —there is the
expense of keeping up his home while paying
hotel or boarding-house or other hash charges at
the seat of Government during the session.
There are periodical election expenses, which eat
up much inoney. Then, between elections, the
member is expected to hire a hall periodically
and address his more or less enlightened sup-
porters. He has to wander about his elec-
torate and shake the more or less horny paw of
his constituent. Also, the member is the mark of
all the beggars and charity-mongers of his dis-
trict, and between the calls he can’t avoid, and
the calls he can only avoid with difficulty, his
£4OO a year dwindles to very little. Then the
position is more precarious than almost any other,
and when it goes there is no pension or retiring
allowance. The member’s business or practice or
connection or job has vanished during his legisla-
tive period, and he is simply a very poor man—
sometimes an old man as well—starting life
afresh. The visible, glaring, threadbare poverty
of many of the ex-members who gathered the
other day to celebrate the Victorian Parliamen-
tary jubilee with food and drink—of which several
seemed much in need—was a deafening com-
mentary on the fact that politics don’t pay;
and Federal politics, which may involve thou-
sands of miles of travel, pay worst of all.
Of course there is a Tory theory or tradition that
members should “serve their country” without
reward (except steals), and from motives of pure
patriotism, but the great bulk of them can’t, for
patriotism isn’t food, nor is it lodging. In any
case the theory was only invented by the rich in
order to restrict Parliamentary membership to
the rich ; if there had been anything more than
that motive of brute selfishness in it, there would
have been a corresponding theory in favor of un-
paid judges, civil servants, military officers,
governors, kings, and the like, who also serve
their country, and who have an equal right to do
it without payment. The proposition about the
glow of patriotic fervor being sufficient reward is
only applied where it came in handy to shut out
competitors among the proletariat; there being
no working-man competition for the job of
monarch or governor, it has always been held in
his case tljat the satisfaction of serving the
community and the empire is no reward at
all, and that a large, sordid wage is re-
quired. Such a wage is equally required in the case
of members of Parliament—in fact, it is more
urgently required, Parliament being of vastly
more importance than either king or governor.
The Parliamentary wage should be sufficient to
enable any man to afford to be in politics. The
only bar to membership should be lack of charac-
ter or lack of intellect. An increase of the
Federal Parliamentary salary to £lOOO a year
represents the lowest figure which is likely to
meet the case. The public might elect some in-
capables at £lOOO a year, just as they do at £4OO,
but at least they wouldn’t be compelled to elect
incapables because wiser men couldn’t afford to
come forward. The proverb about fools rushing
in where angels fear to tread has an application
hereabouts. It is only a very great fool who
thinks it is possible to live in any comfort under
the circumstances with the Parliamentary £4OO a
year as his sola support; and he rushes in. Some-
times he rushes in by a pretty large majority too.
Anti-Sosh Rails Against Itself.
Melbourne importers who lord it over Flinders
Lane are the apostles of Anti-Sosh, so long as Social-
ism is going to benefit somebody else. As soon as
they perceive any possible benefit to themselves
in Socialism they cry out for it. Melbourne
Harbor Trust is an Anti-Sosh body. That is why
it refuses to engage in the work of discharg-
ing, stacking, and delivering cargo. It has
handed all this work over to tne Victorian
Stevedoring and General Contracting Co.,
Ltd., which represents Private Enterprise.
This huge monopoly is making money out of the
importers, who have suddenly dropped their
Anti-Sosh convictions and are crying out for
Socialism. They want the Trust to wipe out
the stevedoring company altogether, and itself
enter into business and handle cargo. “We
want Borne responsible body to take charge of
cargo between the ships’ slings and the con-
signee.” They are quite right, too. They have
discovered that in this case—because it benefits
them —Socialism is a good and proper thing. The
fact is that the Melbourne port accommodation is
wretched. Cargo is pitched out of a ship by
the stevedores, stacked, and left on the wharf or
in the shed, with nobody to look after it.
At present, on most of the Melbourne wharves,
the cargo is left so absolutely unprotected that
there is quite a class of people who make a
living by pillaging, and a good living too.
Withal, stevedoring charges are high. All
this the Harbor Trust can do away with by
Socialism. The port of Melbourne is far behind
most other ports of the same size, and the reason
is that most other ports are run on Socialistic
lines, whereas Melbourne still labors along in the
slough of Private Enterprise. Perhaps the pro-
testations of such a fine body of Anti-Socialists as
the Melbourne importers may mean Socialistic
reform.
The Cow and N.S.W. North Coast Scenery.
“ X. Y.” WRITES from beside a Northern river
Years ago the North Coast district of N.S. Wales was
pronounced by the scarce and accidental tourist to be
beautiful almost beyond praise. The luxuriant growth
of primeval subtropical forest and jungle, impervious to
the sun’s rays, enriched with prodigal wealth of orchids,
tree-ferns, staghorns, bungalow palms and creepers,
stretched almost unbrokenly from the Richmond to the
Queensland border over the undulating volcanic slopes
of the famous Big Scrub. You might have travelled
from Lismore to the Tweed with scarcely a glimpse of
the sun, along avenues cut by cedar getters, suffused
with soft green light filtering through the feathery fronds
of ferns and palms and the higher dense foliage of giant
softwoods and creepers interlaced overhead. To men
from the Western and other districts the beauty of the
leafy profusion was incredible, and had to be seen to be
believed. The Tweed, especially—surrounded by ranges
of mountains dark with dense forests, dominated by the
peak of Wollumbin (Mt. Warning), and intersected in all
directions by arms of the river—was a Paradise.
But now you can travel this same route beneath a
blazing sun with never a break. The Big Scrub has been,
oris being, wholly destroyed. The people are obsessed
by the übiquitous Cow. Not even shade trees for the
cattle in the paddocks seem to be deemed necessary.
And, as for flowers, fruit trees or ornamental trees, well,
with few exceptions, the money-crazed Cow-cockies
prefer to be, and in wet weather often are, mid-thigh
deep in manure and slush. Their children’s lives are
exploited morning and evening, wet and dry, at the cow-
bails, pigstyes and separators. One State school that I
saw is hemmed in by pigstyes and cowyards. Close to
one corner is a pigstye, close to another a cowyard,
then another pigstye, and yet another cowyard. So that,
no matter from which quarter the gentle wind of heaven
may blow, the children constantly inhale the spicy
breezes wandering over beds of assorted manures ! This
school is known to the Public Instruction Department
as Dungay school I
Originally the banks of all the arms of the Tweed
River were overhung by dark, towering walls of virgin
forest, from which hung festoons of flowering creepers,
beautiful beyond description, occasionally lightened by
the flash of brilliant, golden-winged regent birds. Now
one island represents almost all that is left of this sub-
tropical wealth of vegetation. The Government, a short
time back, was mysteriously moved to reserve a few
small areas for the preservation of native flora. But
it has weakened to the cry of the Cow-men,
and to its own greed of revenue, and now pro-
poses to hand these reserves over to destruction—to
be levelled with the dust and strewn with cows, cowdung,
and myriad flies. Near Murwillumbah, on the north arm
of the Tweed, is one reserve of about 50 or 60 acres of
subtropical jungle which it is proposed to alienate, all
except about 15 acres of the part least fitted for cow-
pasture. A protest was attempted at a sparsely-attended
public meeting, and a committee was appointed to see if
it was advisable to retain this reserve ! The committee
viewed the matter according to its personnel. The
mayor, postmaster, a doctor and a publican were for re-
taining the reserve intact, but the other three, all Cow-
men, cried out for destruction and more Cows. Probably
the uprising of the legions of Cow and the apathy of the
townspeople will result in no protest being made. These
men should have no say in this matter. It is a question
for the whole State, or the Ministry as representatives
thereof. The reserves, if left sufficiently large to withstand
the unaccustomed exposure, would afford an object lesson
hundreds of years hence on the beauty of the perished
subtropical forests, and would be things of beauty in a
land where all else is utter barrenness and Cow. If the
trees, many centuries old, are destroyed, the British
Empire will be dead of old age before they can be re-
placed. This letter is an appeal, while there is yet a little
time, to the people of Sydney and the State, and to the
Ministry, to secure from ruthless destruction the few re-
maining flora reserves in this North Coast district, so
that it may not be utterly, completely and hopelessly Cow.
Much sympathy is now being worked up on
behalf of some few Australian-born Kanaka
children who haye gone back with their parents
to the Isles, though the children have, in all
instances, been returned to the islands at the
express wish of the parents themselves. The
drum has been flogged with extra fury for the girl
children, who, it is implied, will have to lead
lives of shocking iniquity in the archipelago from
which their parents came. No explanation is
given of the alleged iniquity, and, as far as
anybody can see, the girl will do nothing worse
than become the consort of a male nigger, who
will make her toil about half as hard as she would
have had to do in the old days in Queensland.
If there is anything worse than that before her,
then the missionary push, with its stories about
how, for fifty years and more, it has been
shedding light on the darkness and shovelling
away the gloom of Polynesia, is about the
biggest liar and the most scoundrelly con-
fidence trick now above ground. Mean-
while, there is another side to the picture—
one that is never exhibited. Consider the
fate of the Kanaka girl who is not deported.
There is only one opening for her. She will be-
come the plaything of the Chow and the Jap and
the other Asiatic scum which infest the north-
east corner of Australia, and her life, under the
beneficent influences of civilisation, will be a
short and unmerry one. There is practically no
escape for the female Kanaka child left in Queens-
land. Prostitution of the most repulsive sort is
her almost inevitable end, and the good people,
now talking so loudly about the injury which will
be done her by returning to the home of her
ancestors, shouid consider whether the life that
lies before her here is preferable to a monogamous
existence of semi-nakedness and affable loafing
under the sheltering palms of Malaita.
Randolph Bedford, in pursuance of a contro-
versy which threatens to be tiresome :
In B. of 3rd Jan. you say of my recent and much-dis-
respected opponent, J. N. H. H. Coo <—“ He consistently
supported the Watson Government when to do that was
to cut absolutely away from the more conservative sec-
tion of the Protectionists ; had practically a pledge of
Labor support at the 1906 election, and should not have
been opposed by Labor.”
This statement of yours emphasises my statement that
youdidn’tknow what you weretalkingaboutin this matter.
Cook wasaLaborite in the Victorian State Parliament,
and was defeated at the 1900 elections for ratting. As
sworn to by Mr. P. Keane in a statutory declaration
published in the Argus two months ago, the same highly-
disrespected Cook tried to get the Pedi ral Labor nomi-
nation for Bourke at the 1901 election, and his relations
packed the local Labor leagues for the purposes of selec-
tion, and didn’t get it after all. From this it is apparent
that fear dictated his truckling to Watson. But neither
Watson, nor you, nor anybody could guarantee immu-
nity from opposition in return for votes. That would
mean the practical disfranchisement of an electorate.
And if it were right to grant immunity for one term of
3 years, why not for 6 ; and if for 6, why not for 9 ; and
if for 9, why not for life ; and if for life, why not make
the job hereditary ?
The facts —at the risk of weariness—may be
repeated. The Labor Government when in office
could not exist without outside support. To the
men who gave that support, the Labor Govern-
ment promised to use all its influence to secure
them immunity from Labor opposition (Note :
Labor opposition, not any opposition, as our
correspondent would try to twist the yarn to) at the
next elections. The inter-State Labor Confer-
ence endorsed that alliance, though deprecating
such agreements in future. Thus Labor was
pledged in honor not to oppose Isaacs, Higgins,
Cook, Mauger, and others. The “motives” of
the men who gave Labor support in a crisis
could not be honorably questioned after their
support was accepted, when it came to a ques-
tion of paying the price agreed for it.

The study of Australian politics, as presented to
the British public, has a special charm of its own.
Novelty and romance meet the student at every
turn. For example, a few choice jets of light
from the gas-bag of “a well-known Australian
politician in London,” which illuminate the
columns of the Standard (15/10/’OG): —
Within the next five years the State Customs revenues
will be absorbed by the Commonwealth in their entirety.
A pleasing picture of the Commonwealth, with
insatiable maw plotting the absolute starvation of
the poor State Treasurers, all in the interests of
“ Sosh ”:—
The financial situation is the great weapon in a fight
between non-Socialism, the States, and Socialism, the
Commonwealth.
Clearly our “ well-known politician ” is not in love
with Federation, the source from which all curses
flow, including “Sosh.”
The Senate is Labor to a man.
Shades of Symon, Gould, Clemons, and the
Mao Walker party.
New South Wales is discontented owing to the pledge
on the subject of the new Federal capital never having
been carried out. No steps towards this end have 3 T et
been taken.
Poor Federal Parliament! Its festive picnics,
the throes of its capital sites debates, the intri-
cacies of its divisions, its choice of Dalgety, and
the rejection of its choice by N.S.W., all sunk
already in political oblivion ! And so history is
made, and so the British Public acquires know-
ledge. The lean finger of suspicion points towards
our old friend, Horace Tozer, as the “well-
known politician.” The Bulletin’s columns are
open to his denial.

The loud applause with which Sydney Telegraph
greets Premier Carruthers’ proposal to force the
Peel River Co. in New South Wales to cut up the
vast estate which it monopolises against agri-
cultural settlement, shows either a vacant mind
and a to-let understanding, or, what is more
probable, a smug hypocrisy. For what Joseph
Carruthers proposes to do in regard to this one
estate, by a slow, cumbrous and expensive pro-
cess, and with grave risk of scandal, is exactly
what J. C. Watson proposed to do in regard to
all the vast estates in Australia, which, like the
Peel River, are held by Monopoly against agri-
cultural settlement, and to do without expense
and without risk of scandal. Since the Tele-
graph so cordially approves of forcing one big
estate into use, why not others, why not all ? If
it is a good thing in the public interest to drive
the Peel River Co. to open up its lands to settle-
ment, why isn’t it a good thing to coerce the
A. A. Co., the Y.D.L. Co., the Victorian Western
District, and the Queensland Darling Downs
monopolists in the same way ?

The frantic denunciation of everything Austra
lian, and the glorification of the imported article,
is sometimes checked by the imported expert
who, like Balaam, the son of Beor, blesses where,
he is expected to curse. At a recent dinner in
Sydney
Captain Stokes Bees, who will shortly leave the Aus-
tralian station, after spending two years here, remarked
that there was magnificent material in Australia from
which to evolve naval men, if they could only get at it,
but no material, however excellent in itself, could be of
any practical use until it was trained.
It was the simple truth. In Australia you can
get any sort of capacity you like to ask and pay
for. The capacity often wants training and de-
veloping, because there is nothing in the com-
mon daily life of the people to call it into action.
When the Australian navy comes the Australian
naval men will not be wanting.
