The Englishman (Calcutta), on the Yellow Peril
in Australia :
Japanese ask for equality of treatment with Europeans,
and America is forced to answer “ yes ” or “ no.” If Presi-
dent Roosevelt wins, it means that all immigration barriers
that differentiate the Japanese from the European must
be pulled down, and the brown man accepted as an
industrial and social equal. The same demand will come
from China, and the same answer will have to he given.
Although the affair only affects America at present,
at any moment it may become of very great concern to
the British Empire. Should America grant equality of
treatment, Japan will naturally turn to Australia. Here
she will find humiliating barriers set against her people,
and a prejudice, that has developed into a natural senti-
ment, against the admittance of Asiatics. Australia is par-
ticularly desirable from the point of view of the Japanese.
It is only natural that they should look longingly
towards Australia. At present the question is shelved,
but obviously only for a little time. When the demand
for Japanese equality does come, as come it must, Great
Britain will be faced with an extremely difficult problem.
On the one hand, there will be the logical position of
her ally, Japan, and on the other the loyalty of the
Commonwealth, which would not stand the strain of
being coerced in this matter. The Australian attitude,
selfish as it may seem, is easily explained. Experience
has shown Australians that the Asiatic is a good and
thrifty worker, who labors long hours for small wages.
If freely admitted into Australia, he would quickly dis-
place the white worker, and Australia would surely cease to
be a white man’s country. Full political rights would
follow free admittance, and then the destiny of Aus-
tralia would pass into the hands of Asia. The four
millions of white people in Australia would rapidly be
absorbed by the stream of unrestricted immigration
that would flow in from Japan and China once the
barriers were removed.

-♦
Bland Holt’s new production at Sydney
Royal is patterned after “The Midnight Wed-
ding” and “The Face at the Window” rather
than the good old stock melodrama, in which
virtue wades toits happy conclusion through aseries
of events which, if not in the least probable, are
at any rate possible. “Revenge” is divorced
from all possibility; it gives the loud “Ha-ha,”
which betokens the contemptuous mind, to
reason, sense, and sequence; it frankly gives the
longarm of coincidence thego-byand welters in the
impossible. The wicked man and the wicked
women are candidates for no less than the throne
of Austria, and their machinations to foil virtue—
represented by the Queen of Austria and
her faithful and true lover, a Captain—
include an explosive ink-pot which goes off
pop when the hero puts his pen into it, and
blinds him with tears and ink. Virtue is cham-
pioned by this gentleman and a British naval
officer who has come up the Danube to Vienna on
his Dreadnought, and runs things generally with
a company of bluejackets. V hen vice has finally
to be foiled, these bluejackets capture the throne
of Austria, seemingly for the lawful Queen
and the Captain who is going to be her
lawful husband No. 2, the revolutionaries
having obligingly killed No. 1. All this
balderdash is illustrated by charming scenes
and brilliant costumes, and goes with a snap
which shows that the melodrama public can
swallow any crudity so long as the pill is well
gilded.
* ■* * •
As to the acting of “ Revenge,” the scene-
painter is excellent, the costumier a genuine artist,
and the stage-carpenter was not called before the
curtain to receive his due simply because the audi-
ence hadn’t enough sense of humor. The rest of
Bland Holt’s co. took subordinate parts.
** * *
“The New Clown” will not remark
“Here we are again” after Friday night. To
close the short Sydney season the Willoughby-
Ward Co. will stage on Saturday “ The Talk of
the Town ” —a farce comedy which Melbourne
preferred to “The Man from Mexico.” The
comedy will be ushered in by “ The Vagabond,”
a one-act drama in which Miss Grace Palotta and
Hugh Ward have “pathetic” parts, and coax
the tear from the eye with gentle sadness, instead
of forcing it out with the head-splitting laugh.
* * *
When the last figure has been added into
the total it will probably be found that “ Parsi-
fal ” has brought more money to Her Majesty’s,
Sydney, than any other piece since J. C. William-
son included Tallis and Ramaciotti; and that will
be the best sort of encouragement to the trinity to
plunge on their own and not wait for London to
first pronounce judgment. Certainly it isn’t every
piece that can hit the public in so many places.
First, in holiday mood, the people turned up to
see it as a spectacle. Then they proceeded to con-
sider it as a play ; then the everlasting question
as to the merits of Tittell Brune had to be
weighed ; now, the holidays over, and the dog
days heavy on the land, a community that is
mostly not feeling very well is debating whether
the play isn’t irreverential and all that. One way
and another it keeps the theatre full, and has
easily outlasted the panto., “ Alice in Wonder-
land.” Alice is now, so to speak, on her last legs,
and the matinees will end with this week.
Chanti’s quick changes hit Tivoli patrons
hard, and set them off on bursts of loud applause
before they have had time to think that Chanti
wouldn’t be anything special as a performer
without all the mechanical extras. As a sample
of ingenious stage management the turn deserves
the large splatters of ink on the bills ; except for
that, a small splatter would do. Otherwise and
generally speaking, the Tivoli performance con-
sists of a large quantity of good, honest variety
food—it has nothing that takes your breath, or,
when it is over, makes you go on forgetting that
you put your hat under the seat.
** * *
On Saturday night—Anniversary date —
if the citizens of this by no means mean city hear
reverberations, sounds as of explosions, and feel
tremors, and see glares, they need not run out to
camp in the open, or call upon their gods. Not
a great natural force, but Mr. William Anderson
will be the cause of the magnificent noise. The
bombardment of Port Arthur will be re-enacted
at Wonderland City. Also the Death Loop and
the Circus will help to keep things exciting.

“Jane,” at the Palace, helps along the
gaiety of Sydney, and by way of an olive, so to
speak, it is prefaced by “ Nan, tho Good-for-
Nothing.” Jane will dust her master down, and
be his official wife till further notice.

Miss Florence Archer will lift her con-
tralto voice at the Pianola Hall in George-street
to-night, and the lastest royal roads to playing
the piano like Paderewski will be exhibited. The
Henry M. Birge Co. gives these complimentary
recitals at short intervals, and up to the seating
accommodation of the Showroom.
In prose —this is the fifth week of the
panto, at Her Majesty’s (Melb.), and the prosper-
ous concern is still in its golden prime.
* * * *-
A revival of “When London Sleeps ” is
threatened for Saturday (26th) at the Theatre
Royal. The retiring drama—“ The Village Black-
smith ” —belongs to the least amusing order of
Andersonian bellowdrama, but the things that
happen “When London Sleeps” have given The
Bulletin periodical bursts of astonishment for
many years. Saturday’s burst will be rather
more “grand and elaborate” than usual, it is
said.
** * #
At the Opera House, where for some time
past the principal turns have been frivolously
disposed, the appearance of Charles Leonard
Fletcher gives a touch of sombre relief to the bill.
Fletcher is an old man character-actor of fair
ability, who makes up his face in view of the
audience, and calls considerable darkness to his
aid when he is acting. His sketch of Mark Twain
as an after-dinner speaker is an impersonation of
the man —not a very faithful one, but sufficiently
clever. His Fagin (from “Oliver Twist”) and
Little Nell’s Grandfather (“The Old Curiosity
Shop ”) are more acceptable than his imitation of
William Gillette’s Sherlock Holmes, who was more
impressively imitated by Cuyler Hastings. The
new comer is not equipped with a fine voice, any-
how, and his “ impersonation ” of Charles Warner
in the second scene from “At the Telephone,”
gives no suggestion of mimicry. It is not even as
forcible as Julius Knight’s noisy treatment of the
same scene, but it will interest the large public
that was mostly not present when Julius in-
troduced the thrilling French playlet at Her
Majesty’s.
** * *
Owing to the fact that the Athenaeum
Hall is about to receive a much-needed wash and
a general redecoration, the season of “ The Kelly
Gang ” biograph will be cut short on Saturday.
Since the beginning of that brisk pictorial drama
the most popular incidents have been “padded”
with many yards of new film and bits of explana-
tory dialogue uttered by an unseen voice at the
rear. There are two voices concerned in the
Taits’ show. The other one is the splendidly
robust tenor organ of Sydney Monk, who warbles
popular ballads during the comic picture pauses
in the first part. Sydney Monk sounds like a
vocalist of great possibilities and limited train-
ing. At present he is in no respect the artist he
should strive to be. His walk is deplorable, his
appearance needs careful cultivation, his singing
method is crude. But his Voice—well, God gave
it to him, and he’s an unworthy Monk if he doesn’t
make the most of it.
* * *
“ Snell ” : The Tait brothers have a pro-
lific oof bird in their grand national cinema-
tograph drama, “ The Kelly Gang,” now showing
at Melb. Athenaeum ; but the gang is presented
in the pure spirit of twopenny-colored melodrama,
and the moral of the story is that it is nobler and
more righteous in the eyes of the gods to be a tall
bushranger and thieve and kill than to be the
despicable policeman who loses his life in
endeavoring to trap outlaws. These splendid’
bushrangers never come within a hundred yards
of a woman without taking off their hats,
and on occasions they remove their hats
as often as nine times to one woman. This is held
to be a glorious characteristic, and justifies all
Ned Kelly’s viciousness and villainies. They are
very respectful to corpses, too, these double-dyed
murderers, and when they have shot a wounded
constable in cold blood they all take off their hats
to his body, and say he was a brave man, and the
audience applauds feelingly, satisfied that Ned
Kelly was a gentleman of exquisite sensibilities.
There is a deal too much racket in connection
with the show—sometimes you can’t see the pic-
tures for the noise of horses, trains, gunshots, and
wild cries; but,"all the same, it is the sort of
bellowdrama that the lower disorders crave for,
and two-thirds of Australia will want to see it—
the two-thirds that believes Ned Kelly was a
greater man than George Washington.
** * *
Melbourne’s outdoor places where six-
pence buys admission and subsequent silver coins
purchase various extra joys, are doing a warm
summer business of late. Also the Australian
Exhibition has made a promising start. Inciden-
tal to the display in the big building there are
open-air concerts and other occurrences in the
quadrangle, especially on Saturday nights. As
regards opportunities for Edwin and Angelina to
tell their love beneath the stars whilst the band
is playing, Melbourne appears to be advancing in
its civilisation.
** * *
At Adelaide Tivoli, Fred Bluett has had
the support of Leonard Nelson, “ comedian,” and
Maud Faning, who can put a little colored coon to
bed with all the frenzied gestures of Lady
Macbeth. The Hippodrome relies largely on a
Scottish strong-man, Lex McLean. And every
evening, under the stars, “Professor” Fenton
encourages the public to step up and get its
immortal soul bucked out of it by his competent
horses and asses, bullocks and mules.
0 ! Pantomime, O ! Pantomime,
Your tracks upon the sands of time
Are indications, gleaming bright,
Of profits earned by day or night.
Some forty footprints up to date
Denote the gay and easy gait
Of one who, free from doubt and fears,
Rejoices in the public’s cheers,
Nor heeds the captious critic’s sneers.
O ! Pantomime, O ! Pantomime,
Not strong in reason or in rhyme,
You fill a want, you play the fool,
You entertain the kids from school
And please their elders, that is plain—
Men see you once and come again.
The public High Court judgment stirs
The bard to speak, so he “concurs,”
Like Barton, when the pleadings deep
Have lulled his Honor off to sleep.
If other tongues in praise run loose,
The bard concurs, for what’s the use
Of finding fault with “ Mother Goose” ?
AT POVERTY POINT.
“ The public is always anxious to hear actors abused.
It likes to spend its days buying- their picture postcards,
its evenings hearing how they beat their wives.” — Tub
Globe.
“ E.”: The Australian stage nowadays is seemingly a
dumping place for ladies who are threatened with fashion-
able divorce-court proceedings in their own country. A
third-rate actress is imported on her merits as a first-rate
co-respondent.
Harry Leston, well known amongst us as a teacher of
elocution and stage-business, was 56 last week. Harry is
very young for his age—juvenescent, in fact, his recent
illness notwithstanding. It is now 38 years since he
joined “the profesh."
Whilst tenor Caruso was in that little trouble in
America, the German Kaiser’s second son, Prince Eitel
Fritz, telegraphed to him that he didn’t believe he
pinched the lady. The wire doesn’t seem to have done
Caruso any good, though it will probably serve him as an
advt. in the future, but it has got the German Prince
into much hot water. The press unkindly hints that his
father represents about all the meddlesome omniscience
that Germany can stand just now. As one (Conservative)
journal politely puts it: “The universal conviction is
that the demand for oratorical and telegraphical mani-
festations is adequately covered.”
A new Australian inedodrama has been perpetrated in
Melbourne, by Bert Bailey, William Anderson’s breezy
comedy man, and Ned Duggan, brother of Miss Eugenie
Duggan and stage manager to the Andersonian shows.
It is understood that Bailey, who is the most irresponsible
of comedians, has constructed the serious part of the
drama—the aerated sentiment and the love and the
strong situations. Ned Duggan supplies the humor.
Anderson stages the play shortly.
Tom Dawson, who shares with Fred Bluett (his deadly
rival and firm friend) the topmost position among Aus-
tralian variety comedians, has been three consecutive years
with Rickards. Formerly he was on Adelaide Advertiser
staff, and then managed a photo establishment in the
Holy City. Rickards allowed him to show' what he could
do at Melbourne Opera House one night. He wasn’t
advertised, and no one knew' w'ho he was. The notice
boards at the sides of the stage read, “ Extra Turn.” He
hit 'em, and signed a contract with Rickards to open in
Sydney. Going across in the express he saw himself
described as “Just arrived from London, the eminent
English comedian and grotesque dancer,” or something
of the sort. Some day Dawson hopes to make London
laugh like mad, and, if he does, he w»on’t forget to let it
know that it’s an Australian that is raising the laugh.
Herold Bassett writes re par. in “Poverty Point”
(B. 10/3 /’O7) : I wish to call attention to quite a few
inaccuracies in Mr. Lincoln’s letter :
(1) I have never made, or attempted to make, Mr. Lin-
coln a scapegoat, and had he not written his letters,
should have made no intimation that he was liable for any
part of loss.
(2) Amount of printing ordered by me up to time Mr.
Lincoln took charge of the printing was £127 odd, with
which I thought to finish the tour of Australia.
(3) Mr. Lincoln ordered up to £283 odd in addition.
(4) I thought to guard against any extravagance by
Mr. Lincoln by making over to him an interest in the
tour, and is teas his own suggestion that he be liable in
case of a loss to the extent of 30 p.c.
(5) The printing account had nothing to do with my
insolvency, which was caused by Mr. Lincoln refusing to
pay a share of the losses. Investigation of the facts will
show that any extravagance was not mine.
(6) Never did Mr. Lincoln keep within his estimate of
expenses, and they ran from half again to double the sum
given me. I have his own figures.
(7) Mr. Lincoln’s idea of a “quick tour” is that of a
barnstorming company playing every night at every tin-
pot town through the country. That W'asn’t mine.
According to paragraphs in Paris papers of last month,
Carrie Swain hasn’t done with Frank Gardner yet.
Carrie, after the rupture, wanted half of Gardner’s for-
tune “ by reason of partnership,” but the question of
domicile was raised, and the interesting legal scrap was
postponed till it was settled whether Frank, of the Sitting
Bull countenance, was a Frenchman or a mere casual
tripper from England. The Paris American Register
(Dec. 9) alleges that Carrie threw at the court an affidavit
that Sitting Bull had sworn before the British Vice-
Consul, to the effect that he had resided in France for the
past 10 years ; and it was such a heavy affidavit that
Gardner’s counsel threw up his brief. The case will go
on, therefore, with the great Labori holding up Carrie’s
end of the argument.
A remarkable feature about Sydney and its suburbs is
the continuous increase of minor shows. “Passing”
they may be, but their successors crowd on. The effort
appears to be in showland what Tit-Bits and Snips and
Barks periodicals are in literature. There is still a
creditable support given to drama and opera of a good
school, but the development of taste is not towards that
class of stage performance As a body the public, instead
of paying ss. for one good performance, prefers to pay
ten sixpences for ten separate whiffs of excitement, con-
taining no information of value and leaving no memory
behind them.

A Sydney Herald reporter who went out to see
Katzenjammer Castle at Wonderland City found it all
strangely familiar—especially the racket, and the draughts.
Indeed, he said that if a few barrels of decayed herrings,
to represent the Stinking Fish crv, were scattered round
Katzenjammer might well be his office.
“Slavin’’: I have been song-writing- for music hall
artists and publishers for the past nine years, and dufing
that time have written and composed over 300 songs, yet
I. am practically unknown to the Australian public,
simply because I am an Australian. Publishers inform
me that they cannot attach my name to songs, as the
public will not buy songs unless they are written and
composed by some well-known American or English author.
Scores of my songs have been published as written and
composed by well-known foreigners. One publisher
in Sydney has attached his own name to several of my
songs. lam thinking seriously of journeying to Ameru a
or some other foreign place, and committing bigamy, or
manslaughter, or embalmed beefery, and exporting my-
self back to Australia, in the hope that this foreign-cursed
land will take me to its unmaternal heart.
“R.” : He was a plump, fatuous, smiling man, and as
he sat in the tram, was lost in obvious admiration
of actor Hugh Ward. That curly-headed come-
dian didn’t seem to appreciate the honor of the
situation, and moved to another seat. But his
admirer wouldn’t hold off. He got up and came up smil-
ing for a second round. “Do you know, old man, I
can’t help saying it. I don’t know you, old man.
But I admire you. Shake-—.” Then the fatuous
man tripped over his own large feet and fell
out of the tram—which fortunately had stopped
—and a passing cab nearly ran over him, and
the cabby completely swore at him ; and women shrieked
as he fell; and then everybody laughed ; and the comedian
looked as if he were wishing he could be as funny.
Christchurch is surely becoming demoralised by its
Exhibition. Hitherto it has been a staid city which led
the simple family life in all its purity. Now it is cultivat-
ing the tastes of degenerate Rome in the days when it
walked home from the wild beast show in the cheerful
glow made by the burning of an Early Christian. One of
the sensations at the Exhibition consists of the rescue of
a living woman from the top of a burning building.
Some night the arrangements will perhaps go wrong, and
the lady will not be rescued, and then Christchurch will
have enjoyed itself thoroughly.
“ Snell ” : The jaw of the actor man has more iron in it
than that of the policeman. Came upon an actor man in
Bourke-street the other day, who should have been out
playing leads with the Blood-thunder, Barnstorm, and
Guttersplud Dramatic Co. on its rounds in Gippsland.
He had his right hand in a sling. “ Resting,” he said.
“ Got a hurt in my fight with the villain. You know, I
have to punch him on the jaw in our big quarrel scene.
At least I hit at him, and they clap two planks together
in the wings. The other night I lunged for him as usual,
but miscalculated and got right on to his jaw. This is
the result.” He pointed to the broken limb! “ And the
villain ?” I asked, anxiously. “Oh, he’s playing leads
now,” said the actor man.
“A.E.M.”: The glamor surrounding the chorus lady’s
life fades rapidly when one is in the know. They don’t
get paid unless they’re in the current production. But
they are not allowed to get employment elsewhere
for the period during which they get no salary. When
rehearsing all day they buy their own food, except in the
case of dress rehearsals, when they get coffee and sand-
wiches, but nothing more substantial or more expensive.
In reputable companies the chorus girl may get up to
£2 10s. per week when on tour; when not touring,
she is lucky if she gets 30s.
J. C. Williamson is not the only earthquake realist
Chicago is responsible for a melodrama, “While ’Frisco
Burns.” Earthquake, lire, famine, and the ten plagues
of Egypt are written round the adventures of the blonde,
manly lover, and the grass-green maid is pursued for
four acts by the ruffian who plunders the dead and is
otherwise corrupt and dark-haired.
“ The Ugliest Woman on Earth ” is the high-sounding
title of the latest London melodrama. Ugly women have
been converted into walking posters to advertise the
show, and have drawn great crowds. In some cases their
lack of comeliness has even stopped the traffic.
La Belle Otero had better luck with the diamonds she
received from King Edward (B. 3/l/’O7) than those pre-
sented to her as a mark of esteem by Leopold the
Awful, King of the Belgians. When Leo was going
strong in his admiration of the Spanish dancer, he
handed her over a mass of glittering hardware, presum-
ably paid for out of his ill-gotten Congo profits.
La Belle wore them for some months, when Leo’s ardor
began to cool, and, one day, he suggested that they might
be given a more artistic setting. The lady thought the
same, and, after an interval, Leo brought them back,
looking better than ever. A little later, he kissed Otero
good-bye, and at a subsequent date the gems seemed to
be getting tired of scintillating, and wore such a worried
look that their owner took them to the nearest jeweller’s.
“ Paste,” remarked the man behind the counter, and he
brutally valued the lot at one lonesome quid.
We don’t have the comic-opera law-suit in Australia.
In London, last month, there was a real “Trial by Jury”
case between a tailor and an actress. Plaintiff pulled de-
fendant’s gown down to show how well it fitted, and she
walked “ majestically ” round the court to show how it
rucked up again at once ; and the titled Judge pl a y?*l U P
to them with “ I’m looking for all I’m worth,” and Come
along, pull away,” and even (straight out of the °P era )
“ Come up here by me.” And after a morning of loud
laughter ” the claim of £3 9s. was reduced to £1 75., and
they all went home happy.


“ Locust ” writes
Now that the elections are really over—the last Senate
return has just come in—one can see plainly that the
overdose of Anti-Sosh administered by the daily press of
N.S.W. acted like an overdose of poison, and caused a
beneficent nausea in the electorates. Not only did the
dailies alternately thunder and scold in their leading
columns, and suppress their opponents and puff their
own men in the ordinary and special political columns,
but the war of supplication and objurgation was carried
into other regions which usually don’t meddle with
politics. For example, the “ Land ” and the “ Soil ’’ men
spread themselves to intimidate the farmer by telling
him in parallel columns all he wants to know about
butter-fat and codlin moths, and also the dangers of an
equal division of all his goods under Socialism. The dog
column barked Anti Sosh, the hen column cackled it,
and that other hen column, the “ Ladies Page,” devoted
much space to it between advice on “ How to remove
freckles’’and “How to dress and look smart on small
incomes of £lOOO a year.” Nor was the egg-laying com-
petition column voiceless. There were exceptions—the
shipping column was strangely silent; nor did the births,
marriages and deaths column make any sign ; but these
were the only exceptions.

and Sons, at their City Horse Bazaar, Sydney-road,
Melbourne.*

The latest British ohampion of American Henry
Arthur Jones, whose new play, “The Hypocrite,” was
recently produced at the Hudson Theatre, New York.
Speaking of the American language the other day to Yale
University students, he said, “It has more bite and
sting, and swarms with lusty young idioms, struck off
red hot with vitality. I hear them as I walk your streets.
I meet them as I read your journals, and try to under-
stand your politics. Your language is better fitted than
ours for the modern living drama.”
London Savoy, the home of Gilbert and Sullivan operas*
started a series of revivals last month with “ The Yeomen
of the artistically the best of the lot. It was a
startling success. Gallery enthusiasts began to gather
at noon, the doors were opened at four, tea was served
by the management, and the crowd then sang all the
best-known Sullivan airs till starting-time. At the end,
there was a wild scene of enthusiasm, especially when W.
S. Gilbert took a call. And the co. was rather a scratch
one, though all the papers agree that the Jack Point—
one Workman, with only a provincial reputation before
—is a real artist. In the original production, in 1889,
Wallace Brownlow and Courtice Pounds both had leading
parts.
Ada Ward, who once pleased Australian playgoers as a
handsome Rosalind, is about to revisit these parts with
an address entitled “ From the Stage to Christ.” Miss
Ward made her first Australian appearance in 1877, as
leading actress in Dumas and Nowski’s “Danicheff,”
under W. S. Lyster’s management. She again acted in
Australia about a decade later, and soon after gave up
the stage to turn “ evangelist.”
Genevieve Ward’s age is more or less settled by dates
given by herself in one of the year-books. She says she was
born in New 7 York, U.S.A., March 27, 1838 ; married Count
deGuerbel in Warsaw at 17 ; appeared asLucrezia Borgia
(under name of Ginevra Guerrabella), at La Scala, Milan,
in 1856. After some years of opera, her voice was in-
jured through over-exertion, but she taught singing in
New York for a long period, and did not make her dbbut
in drama till 1873, when she appeared at Manchester
Theatre Royal as Lady Macbeth. First appeared in
•‘Forget-Me-Not” at London Lyceum, 1879.
“E.F.”: I can speak of Miss Genevieve Ward’s age
from my own youthful impressions of her performance at
the Adelphi, early in 1874. She played a girl part in
“ The Prayer in the Storm,” and I remember to have
been struck by the absurdity of her impersonation. Her
remarkable face, her voice, figure, bearing, and O, her
legs, which showed under a short skirt, were, to my
boyish eyes, undoubtedly elderly. She reminded me of
Madame Celeste, an aged French actress, who had been
playing youthful heroines at the same theatre in the
previous year, and whom I, as a boy, had regarded with
horror. Madame Celeste, by the way, admitted to being
born in 1814. My next recollection of Miss Genevibve
Ward dates back to March, 1884 —nearly 23 years ago.
She was playing the name part in “Forget-Me-Not,” at
the old Princess’s Theatre, Meb.ourne, and she looked
almost as old on the stage as off it. Any Australians who
met her at that time will, I think, support me in the sup-
position that she was at least a woman of 50. “J.A.C.,”
in last Bulletin, suggests that Miss Ward “ must surely
have a good way to go before she reaches 70.” I am
practically convinced that she would have to go back-
wards to arrive at 70.
“0.K.”: A “Poverty Pointer” mentions Miss Edie
Stack, a well-known swimmeress, as “ a striking addition
to the Melb. panto, chorus.” There’s another damsel of
the same name—Miss Freda Stack—who cuts a charming
little figure as a dancer (with a sweet little voice) in the
same panto. She is one of Miss Brenan’s young pupils,
and this is only her first attempt as a professional
“ auxiliary,” but she shows all the style of one born for
the stage.
“Moff”: I went to “an Australian performance by
Australian artists for Australian people ” the other
night. There was a nigger on the stage, and he seemed
to dominate it. Presently, when two white women
stood up to do their turn they “ chucked ” the large nig.
under the chin, with a remark to the effect that they’d
“be happy with a boy like” the nig. aforesaid. An Austra-
lian performance 1 Heaven help us! And “ for Austra-
lian people ” 1 My next-door neighbor was a yellow pagan
from Hankow.
“ Hamer” : The success of the entertainments at which
prizes are given away to attract the otherwise indifferent
crowd is an evidence of the preponderance of the love of
gambling over the love of the theatre ; and yet Australia
is a theatre-loving country. The bare chance of winning
a small prize from a houseful of other hopeful people is
sufficient to attract a big audience. Writer was with a
show at one time which, after playing to houses averaging
£l5 a night, and going as low as £5, gave a bicycle away,
and that evening it scooped a cheque running into
three figures. The bike (given below cost as an
advertisement for its make) cost £5; the fine for
running a lottery, and the cost of proceedings took
£8 ; and a couple of pounds covered the extra advertis-
ing. There is a town in Australia, however, that turns a
cold shoulder to prize nights. A stranded mummer, who
was running dramatic classes in that locality, drew a big
house to one of his shows by the offer of “ a prize worth
three guineas.” When a stout, elderly, countrywoman
in the shilling seats had been adjudged the winner, the
prize was announced as a quarter’s tuition in the dramatic
art, under the artist who gave the show. When that
stout cockyess heard that she was expected, as a prize ,
to trudge three miles into town twice a week and learn
to be Juliet on a balcony, or Mrs. Macbeth, walking in
her sleep with a night-dress on, her rage was terrible.
“Snell”: A taste for the more extravagant forms of
melodrama is not necessarily an indication of mental
weakness in the young or of senile decay in the old,
although, speaking generally, it does go with an artistic
development on a plane with that of the Bosjes man or
the Westralian gin. At one metropolitan theatre, the
other night, I sat behind a very learned professor, who
was as much wrought upon by the strident acting and
the frantic situations of an amazingly bad bellowdrama
as the most susceptible factory girl, hanging with dis-
ordered hair over the gallery rail. Nothing in the way of
butchers’ heroics was too steep for the intellectual giant.
He forgot his surroundings in his excitement, and cried:
“Yes, yes !” “ God bless my soul 1 ” “ She is not guilty 1 ”
“Ah, ah, they will catch him!” “Thank heaven!”
And when the curtain came down, for an interval he sat
back, panting as if he had just run half-a-mile, and
mopped the perspiration from his dome-like brow.
The Inky Way.
“Onawe”: The dishonest methods of the Australian
daily press are apparent, not merely through the medium
of their own unprincipled rags, but by the outside in-
fluences Which they can exert. Maoriland seems to be
at their mercy for its cable news. A glaring example was
the recent Commonwealth elections. It was cabled over
the day after the contest that, “Judging by latest re-
turns, Mr. Reid’s majority in the House will not be
more than two. ” This distinctly implied that anti-Sosh had
wiped the ground with its opponents. Of course, a few
of us know how to interpret this bosh, but the great
majority receive a first impression which isn’t easily dis-
lodged. There are strong evidences, by the way, in this
State, as in Australia, that the daily press is losing its
influence in politics, and what wonder? When Seddon
wiped the Opposition out, here in Maoriland, at his first
personal appeal to the country, he had the whole press of
the country against him, with the exception of about three
journals, and the majority of the papers were against him
to the end, except during his wild jingoistic jag in the
farm-burning days. And Richard seemed to find it a
great deal better to have the daily press for his enemy
than his friend.
* • * *
“ Hamer Par (B. 20/12/’O6) about M.L. papers re-
fusing to comment on exhibits at the Christchurch
show except as advertisements reminds writer that the
M.L. papers—especially those of Wellington and Christ-
church—are, more than any other journals, purely adver-
tising affairs. They wouldn’t announce the second
coming of the Lord, if it happened in those parts, unless
the manager guaranteed an advertisement; and if it
wasn’t a good, solid ad. the notice would only be a small
par. I once collected some information about a per-
sonage regarding whom M.L. was rather curious, and
tried to get rid of it; but there wasn’t a paper that would
take it as news ; everyone wanted to collect something
from the personage for advertisement.
* * « *
A good deal of the country journalism of Maoriland
still consists of the dull vituperation of the Potts and
Slurk era, and clamors around in the dark ages of litera-
ture with such gems as the following on its reptile con-
temporary :
“ A poor weak-headed starveling, a half human smirch
on a decent profession, who grossly maltreats and
butchers the King’s English, and whose daily jabbering-
on politics and other things proclaims him to be as near
an approach to the congenital idiot or the mental par-
alytic as it is possible to be, and yet be permitted to go
at large. He is in every sense a human superfluity,
something that is not wanted, that serves no known use
ful service in the plan of creation but that must be pro-
vided for, a moral excrescence, a mental wart, an ugly
protuberance on the intellectual face of Nature.”
And the reply :
“ We have our faults, and many though they be our
captious critics cannot truthfully say that they ever saw
the journalistic ‘we’ under the table of the sanctum
sanctorum with an empty whisky bottle clasped to his
breast and the editorial chair in the occupancy of the
printer’s devil.” .
England is supposed to be slow in some things, but it
dropped this dreary kind of abuse thirty years ago. Even
the most backblocky paper in Queensland has got
beyond the Slurk stage these days, and in Queensland
journalism has struggled further out among the wombats
than in any other State.
* * » *
“ The Colonel ” : The death of William Carrington re-
vives memories of Melbourne Herald when it was owned
by Sam Winter and James Halfey, and (on latter’s death)
by Winter alone. These were the Little Collins-street
days, when the Herald was something approaching an
evening paper, giving all the news of the day sanely, with
leaders from the pen of George Walstab, instead of
ragged items by boys and ill-paid casuals, with slipshod
and yapping leaderettes by the printer’s-devil-knows-
wliom. Carrington was leader of the staff then. He
was helpful in the kindliest way to newcomers; I
ought to know, for I beg - an regular journalism
under Winter, James Middleton (the daddy sub. and all-
round journalist) and Carrington. “ Sweet William’s ”
innate affability ran into his prettily descriptive pen now
and then with rather gushy effect, but his occasional
“specials” were readable and grammatical, anyhow.
He took a live interest in the Newsboys’ Try Ex-
celsior Class, which the Herald fathered spaciously.
The class met in a music-firm’s lumber-room, higher
up the street, close to the old city lock-up, behind
the Town Hall, and my first regular night engage-
ment was its Friday entertainment, previously at-
tended by Carrington. Between us we ran that
highly-interesting and deserving little show into
more space than such things ever get nowadays.
When the Herald shifted through many proprietaries
into its present faked up bonded store, they made Car-
rington editor of the Weekly Times. He worked at high
pressure, and did a lot towards hurting the Leader (Age
weekly) in the country. But his mental equilibrium
suffered, and for a long time before his death the good-
hearted William was in retirement. He was ever a gen-
tlemanly, genial journalist, who neither drank nor
smoked. He came originally from Tasmania.
THE CRITIC.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When I was very youthful—also fresh and full of mirth
I yearned to know exactly what the critics thought me
worth,
So I seized a treasured poem, and I hurried down the
street,
Where I found a carping critic, and I squatted at his
feet.
He was honored, he was mighty, and his lightest word
was law,
And he said my limpid lyric was the worst he ever saw.
So I thanked him most politely—l was very calm and
cool,
But I whispered to my conscience, “ He’s a most tremen-
dous fool 1 ”
Then I sought another writer, and I said, “ I want to
know
What you think about this poem ; ” and his voice was
soft and low.
He read it, and advised me to proceed upon the land,
And I prayed that he would perish as I shook him by the
hand.
Then I had a fit of thinking, and I muttered, “It is
Fate 1
Up to now my choice of critics has been most unfortunate-
They were evidently biassed—they were jealous—they
were dense.
I will search until I find one with a trace of common
sense 1 ”
Then I hit upon a simple and delightful little plan
With a formula as follows : “ Er—l say ! I knew a man
Who has got a knack of writing—now and then he has
a fit
Of composing little verses. Just see what you think of
it.”
Then I gather round the critic and I hand the beast my
screed,
And I curse him in my whiskers as I watch him grimly
read,
For he never fails to shudder, though he says, “You
ought to send
This effusion to a paper—or at least advise your friend ! ”
I have tried a many critics, and I’ll try a many more
If I have to keep on searching when I reach the golden
shore,
For I want appreciation, and there must be one I know
Who will like my little verses—p’raps he perished long
ago.
I will wander in the Spaces with the stars and planets
tipped,
Hunting always for my critic with my ghostly manu-
script.
And I’ll seize him when I see him and I’ll drag him to a
cloud,
And I’ll seat him down upon it whilst I read my works
aloud.
I am writing—always writing—and the odes mount one
by one,
And I think I’ll have a thousand by the time my race is
run ;
But it really doesn’t matter—it is all the same to me,
Once I find the proper critic, I can use Eternity.
Whilst the surplus stock of aeons rust within the store of
Time
We’ll admire the fair construction and the beauty of each
rhyme :
And there’ll be no interruptions save the songs of joy
afar,
When the Blessed Ones foregather in the radiance of a
star.

KODAK.
THE DIFFERENCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At the 66,000 th celestial conversazione David the king
chanced to meet Aaron the priest in the refreshment-
room, And behold Aaron wept bitterly. And he spake
and said:
“ No, I never got a fair deal from editors. As a literary
man yourself, you know what they are. ’Cept to some.
Now, there’s that sister Miriam of mine. When Moses
occupied the editorial divan, there was no blue-pencilling
for Mirry, or for others who could not write for sour
citrons. But as for me, his own brother ”
And Aaron resumed his weep.
Now David was a subtil man. He strolled out into the
main reception-room and sat him down beside Miriam.
And he lifted up his voice and said :
“ Fine thing that timbrel song of yours, Miss Miriam."
And Miriam answered and said :
“ Ah, but you should have seen it before Moses got to
work on it with his blue pencil. I was never fairly
treated by that man—never. He had his favorites—
Aaron and a host of others who didn’t know a rondeau
from a cabbage. But as for me, his own sister ”
And David smiled.
vie. '
N.L.
Trachseotomy in the Never-
Never.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A BLAZING day, with the sun streaming
down on several tents and a scattered
pile of buildings—the latter a veritable
rookery. The tents are inches deep with red
dust. Beds are jammed together, and patients
everywhere.
Tired and footsore, the few white-capped ones
bravely struggle to supply the never-ending wants
of their charges, some feeling bitter and others
heart-sick, yet trying to hide their feelings, as if
ashamed to admit the work too much.
Then, in through the hospital gates, as only
one drives who knows what a race for life means,
comes a man holding a girl-mite of three in his
arms. “Accident?” questions a patient whose
bed is in view of the window. He receives no
answer. Nurse is wondering where to put the
case.
Outside in the hall the man stands holding a
choking child in the agonies of diphtheria. For
one instant the matron pauses. There is no
operating theatre, no isolation ward, and few
nurses—certainly none to spare for special duty.
Then, in her usual tone, she speaks to the senior
nurse : “ Prepare the large tent for an immediate
operation—trachseotom y. ’ 1
A hasty note to the two honorary doctors sends
the wardsman flying across the paddocks—on foot,
of course. There is no horse, but the wards-
man has done that run often, and is in good
training.
The nurses fly, rather than run, to their work.
All else stands still for the time being. The
typhoids have to wait for their drink or sponge.
One peevish convalescent looks up as nurse passes
with her arms full. He says : “My arrowroot is
not quite sweet enough, nurse.” “Must wait,”
says nurse, promptly.
“The doctors are here,” announces a nurse,
“ and everything we have is in the tent ” The
old thin medico and the young fat one have both
made good time. The little patient is on the
table not a minute too soon. The father waits
anxiously outside the tent, trying to get a glimpse
of what is going on. There are other patients in
the tent, but they are screened off, and all are
wondering what is about to happen. ‘‘ Feathers! ”
orders the surgeon. There are none. The matron
speaks in a tragic whisper to the little probationer.
“Catch the rooster, nurse. Feathers we must
have.” The wardsman has just returned, panting,
from his message. The nurse is panting too,
having helped to carry tables, etc., down to the
tent. Despite their weariness they overtake the
rooster, and do a deed on him that outrages his
finer feelings.
Then there is joy all round. The operation
has succeeded. At sunset the hospital has re-
sumed the tenor of its crowded, dusty way. A
tailless rooster stands outside demanding the
return of his caudal appendage, and threatening
legal proceedings.

No notice (except of a hostile character) will he taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MSS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.

In one thing worms have the advantage of
some men—they don’t have to learn to crawl.
• • * *
Too much fuss has been made about the
New Year Banquet at which New York’s Smart
Set sat down to dinner with pigs. Can’t people
choose the company that suits them ? And didn’t
those people choose well ?
* * * •
In a Sydney daily paper, the other day,
“ choice ” apples were quoted at 9s. to 10s. a case;
“jam lots” at Is. 6d. to 2s. N.S.W. Board of
Health could do some good by vigorous inspec-
tion of the fruit at Is. 6d. a case that is to be
boiled into jam for human consumption.
• • * *
The Rev. Harry Worral, the Yic. parson
who libelled Parliament, says that no hotel-
keepers will go to Heaven. Perhaps, but we
never heard of the clergymen of Heaven; yet
we’ve often heard of the hosts of Heaven.
* * * *
“ Did you give your husband any provoca-
tion ? ” was lately asked of a slightly deaf Mel-
bourne wife whose husband had rebuked her in
the strenuous fashion customary on Yarra-side.
“Yes,” she answered, “we have eleven children.”

A (Vic.) shire, the other day, increased
its rate from Is. to Is. 6d. in the £1 in order to
start making up the shortage of its late secretary
—now in Pentridge. Then it appointed a new
secretary at £5O per annum. And it probably
expects that man to be honest, too.
FROM HIS POINT.
HER: “ There, now; What do you think that hat's like?” Him: “ Like a week's screw.”
The enthusiastic Imperialism of the late
Louis Spitzel, millionaire, presents a very
humorous appearance in the light of the claim
made against his British estate by lawyer Woolf,
on behalf of Melbourne creditors. The creditors
have got a quittance in full from the British
executors of the Hempire-loving millionaire. To
have raised a legal question in the British Court
would have been to give publicity to the
Imperialist’s shady Australian past. The execu-
tors, therefore, agreed to pay all the liabilities of
the uncertificated insolvent, together with 23
years’ interest on the money, and legal expenses
appertaining to this remarkable claim. The
ghost of Spitzel should rejoice to think how he
afforded his creditors a strong incentive to
Imperialism. They will get their long-owed money
(with interest) because they belong to an Empire
on whose Insolvency Courts the sun never sets.
In making the startling assertion that
Adam and Eve didn’t “fall,” Rev. Reggie
Campbell, of London City Temple, overlooks the
fact that he is robbing many sanctimonious
dames of a dearly-cherished privilege. Those
ladies find a certain delight in being able to
regard another member of the sex as a “ fallen
woman,” even if she is, like Mrs. Adam, a distant
relative.
» * * *
There is something quite German about the
story which comes from Queenscliff (Vie.) fort con-
cerning a gunner who saw his children threatened
by a 10ft. shark, and in rescuing them, gave the
alarm to his mates. One of the latter rushed out
with a rifle, and, in defiance of military regula-
tions, fired a couple of shots at the shark, which
had just lost its dinner, whereupon “he was
placed under arrest and ordered to be confined to
barracks.” The military authorities indignantly
“deny ” the story, explaining that the man who
had violated the regulations by trying to kill a man-
eating shark had not been confined to barracks at
all. He had only been reprimanded.

Here is a hotch-potch of divorce and
divorcees. The papers have already told the yarn
of how American millionairess, Anna Gould (of
the old Jay family) divorced her count, de
Castellane. Now, if neither party changes its
alleged mind, the divorced de Castellane is going
to marry the widow of M. Menier, the chocolate
man, who was also married once to Baron
Hirscli’s adopted son, but divorced him, where-
upon Baron Hirsch’s adopted son married the
sister of Lord Gerard. So far as the Goulds,
Meniers, and Hirschs, all millionaires, are con-
cerned, motor-cars seem to have been invented to
let them get quickly between churches and divorce
courts.

Another woman has turned up in Adelaide
with the information that she “has lost her
memory.” Where do all these lost memories get
to? Do the police ever drag the harbors for
them, or search the scrub, or advertise ? There’s
quite a number of lost memories in Australia just
now ; it’s time some of them were found.

An American journalist toured Maoriland
last year, and an article entitled “Where Women
Rule,” in the Woman’s Magazine, is the startling
result. According to him, the curfew tolls at 10
p.m. in Maoriland, and all the youths go home at
the first clang, for, “thanks to the purity of the
lives of Maoriland women, and their upholding
the morality of the nation, there is not a single
immoral house in the entire colony of a million
people.” From these sublime moral heights he
drops to the quality of the tea —“ tea which must
have received awards from the gods of mythology
in days of old.” William Bruce Leffingwell is the
name of the gentleman responsible for this guff.
Perhaps he spells his name at times Laughing-
well.
* * * •
A Caliban, ostensibly hawking peaches,
threw himself upon the lawn of a N.S.W. Judge’s
house, one afternoon lately, and howled till
Ermine toddled down for a look. Caliban
declined to move till an ambulance was sent for,
and, as the Judge expected company, a sovereign
was offered as a bribe to go away. Caliban took the
sovereign, but, as the police informed the Judge
next day, the alternative had varied considerably.
The sovereign was top-notch, Caliban having, on
the same plea, accepted 10s., 7s. 6d., 55., and
half-a-crown from the owners of other lawns on
the same afternoon.
“Bargain sales,” or something of the
kind, seem to produce strange ideas of value in
the feminine mind. At St. Kilda beach (Melb.),
one woman remarked to a man in charge that she
would take two of the penny chairs. She and her
small son were laboriously conveying the chairs
towards the tram, when they were hauled back
and informed that the modest “brown” merely
represented the hire value of the furniture.

The little Melbourne ’Erald has done a
dreadful thing. Learning by cable that the
Duke of Connaught was going, with his wife and
his daughter (the Princess Patricia, who wanted
to marry the King of Spain, but Alfonso was too
fond of Patricia’s plump cousin), on a tour to the
Straits Settlements, the ’Erald vowed that the
gallant Duke must come to Australia also, and
rushed the Federal and State Ministers with thesug-
gestion. They looked at the ’Erald reporter with
a glazed, imperial eye, and said, parrot-rotically
(that’s the nearest we can get to the spelling)—“ A
splendid idea.” Minister Sam Mauger (Defence),
instead of falling heavily on the reporter, and
pushing him down Parliament-house steps, cried
“By Jingo !” and said that he had just thought
of the same thing himself. (Which suggests that
besides wrecking the ’Erald office for this pre-
cious suggestion, there’s a call to wreck Mauger’s
hat-shop too.) The bill for the Duke of York
was about a quarter of a million-enough to build
a mail steamer or a warship. Ducal visits are an
expensive luxury; and all they do is to add a
little to the good-natured contempt which the
man in the street has for royalty.
* « • 9
Most of the Australian Ministers who have
spoken on the question of inviting the Duke of
Connaught to visit Australia show a feeling like
that of a worker in a big Australian factory who
is asked by a busybody to contribute to a “pre-
sentation ” to some unheard-of person in the firm’s
London house. The dunned toiler remarks:
“ I don’t want to seem mean, but who is he, any-
way ? Oh, well, if I must ” —— And accordingly
the nonentity who doesn’t want a testimonial gets
it from those who certainly don’t want to give it,
all because of the inevitable busybody—in this
case represented by the newspapers.
* * * *
The United States, it is reported, will cut
—or attempt to cut—the Panama Canal with
Chinese coolie labor. The decision flies in, the
face of reason and experience. Now that American
science has solved the first great difficulty in
Panama by exterminating the mosquitoes, which
disseminated yellow fever and malaria, white
men can work there; and in the wake of white
labor, there is no doubt, would come machinery
reducing the actual manual labor to a minimum.
The Yellow Man will work cheaply, but slowly
and inefficiently, and his importation will add
another race problem to America. The United
States, where the population has to roast a nigger
or two every week to keep one racial difficulty in
check, should be the last country to venture on
another experiment in slave labor.
“Philo”: Re
effective life-
saving appara-
tus for the
dangerous
and much-
frequented
beaches
about Svd-
ne y. Un-
doubtedly the
best, and, in-
cidentally, the
cheapest, life-
saver is the
canvas-cased
cork belt that ties
around the body.
With this device
an indifferent swim-
mer may go to a res-
cue with safety, and
by taking out another
belt for the victim he
can easily support his
patient. This saves the
great expenditure of
strength required in
keeping afloat a help-
less, water - weighted
body, at the same time
retaining a hold on the
bobbing, wriggling, cir-
cular devil of a buoy.
To force one’s way
through chokingbreak-
ers, pushing a large,
unwieldy buoy on the
end of a long, heavy,
water-soaked ro.'e, at
which the sand seems
to drag as though it
were possessed of a
cuttle-fish, takes all
the strength out of a
man before he reaches
the drowner. Also,
the rope is too easily
tangled, and it. gets
“bellied” —that is, it
curves between the man on
the end and the beach —which
makes it still more unmanage-
able. Instead of rope, pliable
wire, wound around a reel set on wheels, should be
supplied. It is less unwieldy than rope, and far
more easily run out, and does not tangle or
“belly.” Also it cleaves its way through the
water and sand instead of sagging along the bot-
tom like a drugged whale.
The Judkins cry to abolish the military
canteens still continues in Australia; and the
present Defence Administration seems inclined to
accede to the clamor and effect, by underhand
means, what Parliament refused to sanction.
Worth noting therefore that—
Resolutions favoring the restoration of the American
Army canteen for the sale of beer and light wines to
soldiers, under proper restrictions, have been unani-
mously adopted by Advance Tent, Independent Order of
Rechabites, of Washington, U.S.A., said to be the oldest
temperance organisation in the world.
Practical result of abolishing the military can-
teens in U.S.A. has been an increase of drunken-
ness among soldiers.
A Melbourne undertaker has an arrange-
ment with the Government to convey all found-
deads to the morgue, free of cost, on the off-chance
of getting the burial. When a body is fished out
of the river or bay, the police telephone for Mould,
who conveys it to the slabs, and then hunts round
for whatever bereaved there may be. When he
informs them that “ we took the deceased to the
morgue, you know,” he usually gets the planting
job, and then departs to put his plumes in curl-
papers for the morrow.
* * * •
Two of the well-found and splendidly-
equipped vessels of the Sydney Ferries became
temporarily disabled last week. One lost her
propeller up the Parramatta River, while • the
crack boat of the fleet broke her steering gear at
Circular Quay jetty. The locality of the accident
in each case prevented danger to passengers. But
the Sydney Ferries and the Balmain Ferry Co.,
in their search for profits, run their vessels across
the Heads to Middle Harbor in practically all
weathers, and, had either of these accidents
occurred while rounding Middle Head in a lumpy
north-east swell, the result to a crowded ferry
boat would have been awful. These ferry boats
are ferry boats, and not excursion steamers, and
though admittedly more than well-found, acci-
dents must sometimes happen. On a fine, bright
afternoon it is quite common to see a heavy roll
coming in through the Heads. Should a
ferry steamer suddenly become disabled and
helpless when staggering across the entrance, the
loss of life will be counted in hundreds.
UPRIGHT ADAM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Adam did not fall." —Cabled statement of London
parson.
Oh, Adam noble, Adam good and brave,
Father of men who yarned with angels haughty,
What slanders have been uttered o’er your grave,
Declaring you were naughty !
We’ve been accustomed, with a squinting eye,
To thank you for your sin—although we chid it;
And, after all (so holy critics cry),
You never did it 1
The groves of Eden never saw you grin
That knowing grin we’ve thought of you as grinning ;
You never sinned enough to sin the sin
Which justifies our sinning.
Adam, we’re disappointed folk to-day,
No longer can we blame while we adore you ;
But if you didn’t do the thing they say—
We’ll do it for you.
VIC.
P.
A BARK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dull dogs 1 dull dogs ! they plot and plod alon ,
While we poor careless hounds are making merry.
They grow inanely rich —we sing a song,
And so, by divers roads, we reach the Ferry,
Where all our bones —e’en those we wear —we throw
To avaricious relatives a-prowling,
And they, in turn, must do the same and go
The road we went. So, friends, continue howling !

• • * *
Sydney Health Officer Ashburton Thomp-
son strikes the right note in an interview with
Sydney Telegraph on phthisis
Compulsory reporting of cases, with proper medical
action following, associated with the segregation of the
more serious cases amongst the poorest branches of the
population, and the segregation of others for the sake of
treatment, with a view to recovery, would certainly lead
to the practical extinction of the disease.
The “extinction” of the disease in Australia—
that’s the ideal to keep in mind. Phthisis is as
foreign to Australia’s eucalyptic air as plague,
titles, small-pox, snobbery, or phylloxera; and a
campaign for its extinction, if conducted on right
lines, is almost sure of success.
* * * •
The newer school of Australian farmers
is right on top amongst the world’s agriculturists.
The revolution now developing in the rural
industries sees the youth of the country bringing
brains on to the land. For a long time the
successful farmer, or breeder of stock, filled the
boys with ideas that land occupation was some-
how a poor, demeaning game. The clerk’s desk,
the city counter, or the professional ranks were
set up as the proper aims of the ambitious. The
boys, in the starched collars and boiled shirt
equipment, flooded the cities and towns. Now
the stream flows countrywards. The youth of
the country is proving his adaptability to the
soil, and he is, aided by good seasons, making
things hum. That there is money in wel 1-directed
effort is proved by the case of two brothers who
are handling less than 300 acres in the Central
Division. Last year they netted no less than
£2OOO.

Once more Premier Bent talks of removing
the big Melbourne Hospital, the Melbourne Gaol,
and the Public Library, and selling the land for
business purposes. A deal in land is Bent’s weak-
ness. Certainly, the hospital and gaol should be
removed —the former put away from the city, and
the latter done away with altogether, Pentridge
being large enough for all purposes ; but the idea
of shifting the library, museum, etc., is absurd.

N.S.W. also has its disease-spreading slate
in the infant classes of the public schools, although
the iniquitous thing is not so common as in

are gathered up to be indiscriminately distributed
next morning. And then folk express wonder
at the rapidity with which diseases spread amongst
little children.

“ A. 8.”: Surf-bathing should be absolutely
prohibited at Tamarama Beach (Bondi, N.S.W.),
where the last fatality occurred. I entered the
water there for the first time a fortnight ago, and
got a scare that spoiled my beauty for two full
days. The beach shelves so suddenly that there
is an undertow like a traction engine. To venture
twenty feet from the dry land was suicide, and,
even in a spot of comparative safety, it was neces-
sary to go down on all fours and dig your fingers
into the wet sand to avoid being carried out. For
the ignorant or venturesome, bathing there is like
fooling with live wires. Tamarama is hungrier
for prey than a tiger, and has more clawing and
dragging power than a herd of lions.

It frequently happens that old people who
live alonedie suddenly, and are not missed for days.
In an Australian suburb, last week, some of the
neighbors went to the police station and reported
that an elderly lady, who led a hatter’s existence,
had not been seen about for three or four days. A
constable was told off to investigate. The con-
stable, from past experience, guessed what he
would find, so, to avoid two trips, he took an under-
taker with him. Undertaker and bobby reached
the house and knocked loudly at the door. All
was silent as the grave. So Robert procured an
axe and battered the door open. No sooner was
it burst in than the elderly corpse, in a very much
alivestateand boiling with rage, appeared, demand-
ing, in shrill Scotch accents, what they meant by
axeing their way into her house. Robert and the
undertaker did not stay to argue. They dropped
their axe and scuttled out of the place without
even asking why the lady had played ’possum for
half a week. Now they are awaiting an action
for damages.
•'• • *
Newcastle (N.S.W.) police have a silently
humorous way of “ moving on ” the local
assignation house. There is no fuss or publicity
or law costs. A policeman in uniform is sent
around, and takes up his stand in front of
the premises. He is relieved at intervals, but
there is always a policeman there, once the cop
eventuates. He stays all day, and he lingers all
night. He is stolid like an image, and he gazes
hard at the house all the time. The rising and
the going down of the sun alike find the peeler
looking hard at the premises, and digesting
the identity of those who go in and those who
come out. The first day is followed by unutter-
able silence on the front step and fearful lan-
guage within. The second day the language
inside is worse, and grass begins to grow on the
front step. Third day the language inside is
terrible, and the furniture van comes for the
sticks of ill-fame. The John only goes home
after he has seen that van safely round the corner.

“N.L.”: In my suburb the “musical
and elocutionary competitions” mania seems to
have fairly gone to the dogs. The dog
from the corner house usually starts
proceedings. Yesterday he managed
to force his way into the street about
midnight, and after remarking in a
neat speech that only prize-winners
were taking part that night, he em-
barked on the “ Pagliacci ” prologue.
I had no programme, but the next num-
ber appeared to be a setting for two
voices of “ Ocean, Thou Mighty
Monster,” in which the street dog
combined forces with a St. Bernard in
an adjacent back-yard. Though there
was a tendency to indiscriminate ap-
plause from all the yards within a wide
radius, this number went off really well.
Then the sweet soprano of the Daudie
Dinmont next door was heard in a
magnificent rendering of “ Bid Me
Discourse,” with throat-wind obligato
by the street dog. As an encore
she favored with Tosti’s “ Good-baye,”
which was also accompanied by the in-
defatigable street dog. Then about
40 dogs gave an admirable vocal version
of the “ Gotterdammerung ” overture,
which was specially notable for the
street dog’s brilliant staccato passages.
I am sorry to say that the close of this
number was marred by an undignified
wrangle, apparently caused by the
street dog’s allegation that some non-
prize-winners had dared to chip in. At
the height of the argument the baker
opposite gave his opinion in the form
of a well-chosen lump of dough, which
struck the chief disputant (the street
dog) fairly between the eyes. He
swallowed the dough and opened the
elocutionary section by reciting the
“ Seven Ages,” which he followed with
“The Dream of Eugene Aram.” He
took every sound from the other dogs,
hostile or otherwise, as an inducement
to further effort, and, after grappling
dogfully with “ The Yarn of the Nancy
Bell ” and “ Curfew Shall Not,” he
was half-way through “Paul Revere’s
Ride ” when I dropped off to sleep.
Victorian trade unionists make a mistake
in keeping their annual art union addendum to the
Eight-hourscelebration. Attorney-GeneralDavies,
it seems, has given his consent to it this year, con-
ditionally on the expenditure being kept within
reasonable limits, and the net proceeds being
handed over to the charities, and the alleged
prizes being genuine works of art. The A.N.A.
abolished its gamble, and not only lost nothing,
but gained by doing so. Are not the trade-
unionists game to follow suit ?
* * * *
Sir Evelyn Ruggles-Brise, Chairman of
the Prison Commission, London, lately gave an
account of the “Borstal” system of dealing with
prisoners between the ages of 16 and 21. The
Borstal system, he said, had reclaimed 50 per
cent, of the cases it had dealt with since its
introduction at Bedford Prison. The main idea
is the division of a term of imprisonment into
three progressively improving stages, with atten-
dant privileges. Other features are educational
lectures, choir practices, and physical drill, in-
stead of the monotonous “exercise” in the prison
yard.

Maoriland has a dark suspicion that all its
policemen are criminals. Whether the force is
recruited from the gaols, or merely becomes de-
moralised through donning a blue uniform, is not
known, but it seems a bad, wicked force. Its
recent record includes four Dunedin constables
gaoled for burglary; another charged with
assault; two Wellington cops tried for perjury ;
and an Auckland probationer-bobby leading a
blue-metal-throwing riot. The latest incident
was that of an active and intelligent officer
who was charged with assault and robbery.
Two juries disagreed over the matter, and the
case was dropped. Some years ago, Tommy Taylor,
M.H.R., had a Commission set up to inves-
tigate the wickedness of the Maoriland “ thrap.”
The Commission sat long and late, and heard
much queer evidence. In the end it made a
lengthy statement, and the force was supposed to
be cured of all its evil habits. But its habits
seem to be worse since it was cured than they
were before.

Beer-drinkers at Rochester (Tic.) are on
strike, local publicans having put the popular
pint up to 6d., to recompense themselves for the
losses sustained by the curtailment of Sunday
trading. ‘‘For freedom and 3d. beers” is the
motto of the strikers, and as a means of ter-
rorising the poor publican, they threaten to have
Judkins and Woodful up to the town to conduct
an anti-beering campaign and assist on picket
duty.
* • • •
It is the little things that count. An easily
cleanable jug that has just come on the market
will probably be the means of saving many
infantile lives. The old, narrow jug was difficult
to clean, and often in careless hands became en-
crusted inside with a deposit of thickened milk.
This soured, deeayed, and the resultant micro-
organisms poisoned the clean milk put into the
jug. The new wide jug is so easily cleaned that
it can only be left dirty by intent.

“ The Chiel ” : How is this for a small
funeral—a man, a bike, and a brown-papered

“A. 8.5.” : Why are not our German
colonists becoming Australianised ? Although the
inhabitants of the Fatherland are not very
numerous, their influence is becoming more and
more weighty every day, and too many of them
hold aloof from Australian sentiment. The sons
of the original German settlers of S. Australia are
still encouraged to speak their mother tongue,
and the topics discussed in the German clubs
throughout Australia would make a good backing
to a coming-war novel. In the United States the
Rhinelanders quickly become Americanised, for
Uncle Sam will not tolerate anti-American
citizens.
Dear Bulletin, —My theory to account for
the objection of the average Hebrew to manual labor
is that the Jew did so much hard work building
the Pyramids that he reckons it is up to him to
have a rest. The adage that it’s only fools and
horses that work was brought home to the Jew
before the rest of humanity bestowed a thought
upon anything beyond keeping the bears out of
its cave at night time. —H.

Occasionally Sydney gives the detectives a
mystery. The latest is the absolutely unaccount-
able disappearance of Robert Amos, past 80, a
clean-living, robust, intelligent Clarence River
(N.S.W.) farmer, respecting whom there attaches
no suspicion of levanting, and whose mental
faculties were remarkably sound up to the time
he was missed, two months ago. The old chap
was in Sydney last November staying with some
relatives at Erskineville, and started out one
morning with less than a pound in his pocket to
purchase some toys for his grandchildren. Since
that time not the faintest clue has been obtained as
to his whereabouts, dead or alive. If he had the
mind to, he had no means at hand to take a
steamer or train journey of any distance ; for the
same reason, there was little temptation for the
murderer (since the days of Butler murders aren’t
committed for less than £1), or a siren. He did
not drink, or gamble, or associate with any char-
acters likely to hold him for a ransom. Only one
suspicious circumstance—which may have no
value—points to foul play or to some adventurer’s
trick. Some weeks after his disappearance a
stranger, riding a bicycle, called at his dairy farm
on the Clarence, and asked strange questions,
and curiously looked around the property, and
rode off. But that is all; otherwise, there is no
clue to the old man’s fate.

OBIT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Lo ! the boarder, sung of rhymesters, came a-whisp’ring
at my ear;
Told of lodgings, bourgeois lodgings, and of hashes, grey
and drear;
Raised his voice to heights fantastic,
Lilting glad refrains of stews—
Stews of mutton, weird and plastic,
And the odors they diffuse.
“I’m the boarder, famed of rhymesters, I am great
beyond the seas !
I’m the hero of versed hash-fights, and of lobster
thr nodies 1 ”
Weak his voice grew—weak and wistful,
As a limpid tear he dried.
Solemnly he spake, and tristful—
“ I’ve been overworked.” Then died.
vie.
JEAN SIBI.

The Bank of Victoria has had a very good half-year, and
admits it; and, on the strength of that, ordinary share-
holders for the first time get the same 5 per cent, as
preference shareholders. In any further increase of
dividend the latter will have to share ; but after they
have had 7 per cent, in 10 half-years the preference ends.
This may mean that there will not be any further in-
crease-till the bank can pay 7 percent, all round; and
that will call for £51,730 a half-year instead of £36,950.
Anyway, the bank might go on paying 6 per cent, for
20 years and the preference would still be in full working
order ; whereas if it pays 5 per cent, steadily, and builds
up its reserves, and then with their help pays 7 per cent,
for five years, the preference will end for all time ; and
as on account of the preference the ordinaries didn’t get a
cent from 1895 to 1900, they will probably feel inclined to
make a sacrifice to clear the preference out. Up to now
they certainly haven’t shown much signs of having the
sacrificing spirit in any large quantity.
The Bank of Victoria’s figures show rapid pro-
gress :
Dec., 'O5. Dec., ’O6.
Notes in circulation.. £121,426 £141,404 Inc. £19,978
Bills, &c. .. .. 504,619 557,532 „ 52,913
Deposits at interest.. 3,507,801 3,753,000 ~ 245,199
Free deposits.. .. 1,856,458 1,903,361 „ 46,903
Liquid assets .. .. 2,105,592 2,210,504 „ 104,912
Advances .. .. 5,143,194 5,412,606 „ 269,412
Premises, &c., .. 427,995 432,848 ~ 4,853
There is money in that. The increase in advances has
exceeded the increase in deposits at interest, which
hasn’t been a common Australian banking experience
lately. Of course it means risk ; for the values of many
Australian securities are based on boom markets at pre-
sent. , But in many directions Australian banking profits
have been unusually high lately, and there is no doubt
that some banks are making large provision against pos-
sible trouble by and bye. ' Whether the Victoria is one
of them Tub Bulletin doesn’t know. That bank, with its
large new advances, is certainly taking the risks ; and if it
can make special internal provision for them, apart from
the very much increased profit which it shows, then its
recovery must have been amazingly rapid. Certainly, its
profits on the basis of capital and reserves are still com-
paratively small; but note the jump :
June, 1903 £3 12 2 p.c. June, 1905 £4 3 1 p.c.
Dec., 1903 4 4 2 „ Dec., 1905 4 3 7 „
June, 1904 4' 4 4 „ June, 1906 4 911 „
Dec., 1904 4 4 2 „ Dec., 1906 5 3 6 „
The public’s money in the business is not quite four-
times more than the shareholders’ money; and that
partly explains the comparatively low earnings. The
Bank of Australasia, in which the proportion is about 7
to 1, shows a return of about 10 per cent. True, the
Commercial of Sydney (7£ to 1) shows a return of only
about 6J per cent. But, then, whereas the Commercial
of Sydney had, at June 30 last, 54 per cent, of its debts
to the public in liquid assets, the Victoria has less than
34 per cent. Even allowing that the Victoria has, on its
published figures, made an excellent recovery, it looks as
if many of its assets were still only very moderately profit-
able at present book-values, and that the bank ought
now take a long spell before it adds again to its dividend.

In 1893 the swollen Commerqial Bank burst. Its lia-
bilities at December 31st, 1392, were £12,635,066. The
scheme of reconstruction finally adopted involved the
issue of preference shares, which now represent
£2,117,350 of the total capital of £2,212,636. The capital
at the time of the smash was £1,200,000, in 300,000
shares, on which 12J per cent, was paid in ’92.
After the smash calls were made, and in all £9
10s. per share was written off. Heavy forfeitures
of capital also took place, so that now all that is
left of the ordinary capital is £95,286 in 190,573 shares of
10s. each. It represents something heroic in the way of
a cleaning-up ; and when any bank depositor, drawing
his 3 per cent., thinks jealous thoughts of the share-
holder’s 12J per cent., it will be like pouring oil on his
troubled brain if he also calls to mind what happened
respectively to depositors and shareholders in this con-
cern. Depositors not only got almost the whole busi-
ness, but were subsidised to the extent of about £1,100,000
of solid cash, put up by the shareholders, to take it.
Certainly the ordinary shareholders are not
quite done with it yet. True, they own only £95,286 of
the total capital of £2,212,636. But the preference share-
holders are only entitled to receive 4 p.c., of which 3 p.c.
is now cumulative. Four p.c. would call for under
£85,000 a year ; and after that has been paid, ordinaries
.can have a good time at very little expense. Twenty p.c.
would call for only £19,057 per half-year—a total for pref.
(4 p.c.) and ordinary shares of £61,404. That is in the
far future, no doubt; but it explains why Commercial
ordinary shares, which were nearly waste-paper a while
ago, and a little before that were a horror and a night-
mare, are now being picked up at 165., and put away in a
long stocking. It is all right so long as people remember
that, though the preference stops at 4 per cent., the pre-
ference doesn’t disappear after a certain number of 4 per
cent, payments ; also that ordinaries can get nothing till
another 1 per cent., absorbing £10,587 per half-year, is
paid to preference holders.
And there is a lot of the old trouble still left in
the Commercial. An Assets Trust was registered to
realise the worst-looking business ; and this concern gets
aid from the bank’s profits in the liquidation process. In
the past half-year £28,510 is applied to this purpose.
Bunching the Bank and Trust so far as relates to the old
trouble and the effort to get rid of it, we get this re-
sult :
Dec.,’ol. June,’o6. Dec.,’o6.
Liabilities of Trust £3,334,903 £2,919,788 £2,719,044
Assets of Trust .. 1,800,000 1,530,000 1,330,000
Deficit of Trust .. £1,534,903 £1,389,788 £1,389,044
Less Bank Reserves 27,619 225,234 237,825
Net deficit.. .. £1.507,284 £1,164,554 £1,151,219
“ Assets of Trust, £1,330,000,” is the official valuation ;
and a great deal depends upon the sort of valuation it
is. Recent conditions should have helped the Trust
(and, of course, the Bank) enormously. In the past
two. years £290,000 (valuation) of assets and £289,552 of
liabilities have disappeared. Meantime an annual
£57,000 or thereabouts of Bank profits have been ap-
plied to aid the Trust. But how much of that has
been applied to square any deficit on current trans-
actions, and how much to write down assets
in hand (by using the money to pay off lia-
bilities), it is impossible to say. And in the absence
of any accounts of the Trust, it is impossible to even
guess at the present value of its securities. This much
is pretty certain : if there were a Trust deficit of no more
than £1,534,903 in 1901, when the new general manager
was going through values, there is not a deficit of
£1,389,044 now, seeing that meantime something like
£280,000 of Bank profits have been put into the Trust.
Five years have elapsed since the business was last gone
into ; and it seems about time for it to be gone into
officially again. That is due to the holders of the deposit
receipts, and is all the more due to them because the
Bank lately announced that it would buy this paper.
The Bank cannot with any self-respect buy it at a penny
of discount without letting depositors know exactly how
the affairs of the Trust stand.
By way of a side issue, the management
announced last year that it would buy £300,000 worth of
old deposit receipts at the best price offering. The time
fixed was September. It was pledged to buy £300,000, no
matter what the price, if that much was offered. In the
past six months the liabilities of the Trust have been re-
duced by only £200,744. Did they, then, not get offers
of £300,000? Or if they did, what did they do with
them ? The situation calls for explanation.
The new business of the Bank shows very
notable expansion (and the change in the figures points
to altered conditions, which call for new information
about the Assets Trust): —

The most striking thing here is the great increase in
advances—partly, no doubt, the result of opening
numerous new branches. Even now, though, its liquid
assets represent 49 per cent, of its new debts to the
public ; and, even though it holds a very large proportion
of current-account money, which requires a large coin
reserve, it is in a position to still further extend advance
business when that business offers. As for earnings,
including as profit the £28,510 paid over to the Trust, the
funds (capital and reserves) show £5 19s. 6d. per cent. ;
excluding the aid to the Trust, £3 12s. lOd.
