A patriarch at the half-yearly meeting last week asked a
rude question of that other patriarch, chairman Hugh
Dixson. He remarked that the chairman had referred to
the State’s great prosperity, and had quoted figures to
show that the City Bank had shared in it, and he wanted
to know how it was that, under such circumstances, the
profits were always the same, within a pound or two. In
reply, the chairman, full of years and wisdom, talked
right round the compass, and said that he had increased
his holding. It wasn’t an answer, but it was the only
answer the rude, inquisitive shareholder got; and then
the usual votes of thanks were carried. The City Bank is
a mystery, and the chairman wouldn’t lift the veil. The
present figures may be worthy of confidence ; but, if they
are right, it is quite certain those of a few years ago were
absurd. Is the bank earning more than £6OOO a half-year
now, or were the former earnings of £BOOO or £9OOO a
half-year to some extent fictitious, in which case were
the dividends paid out of capital? Compare the figures
for the past half-year with those of the latter half of 1903 :
Dec., ’O3. Dec., ’O6.
Notes .. .. £76,355 £85,839 Inc. £9,484
Deposits .. 1,222,331 1,125,813 Dec. 96,518
Liquid Assets .. 433,004 322,116 Dec. 110,888
Advances .. 1,135,954 1,160,805 Inc. 24,851
Premises, &c. .. 142,821 144,596 Inc. 1,775
A decrease in deposits, which in the ordinary way would
mean less interest to pay; a substantial increase in
advances, which in the ordinary course would mean more
interest to receive; and yet the directors reported a
profit of £9052 in the earlier half-year and £6049 in the
later 1 Under such circumstances, what confidence can be
felt in the figures ? So far as “ investment ”in the shares
is concerned, it is really a gamble. The shares, £8 paid,
are worth, one lot 725. 6d., the other lot (witli a £2
precedent liability) 675. 6d. Of course, these quota-
tions scorn the balance-sheet figures. Some of the
capital has been lost: how much? It is impos-
sible to judge, because of the apparent unre-
liability of the profit figures; they being under
suspicion, there is no basis to work upon. The matter
can, however, be probed with a little more success from
the depositor’s point of view. The bank owes the public
£1,211,653. It has £322,116 of liquid assets, leaving
£889,537 of the public’s money represented by non-
liquid assets ; and the book value of nondiquid assets is
£1,305,401. In other words, depositors are all right, even
if the £1,305,401 of securities are only worth £889,537 ; and
it is incredible that there is a deficit of that size. As to
shareholders, if they accept the present stated profits as
fairly representing the position, then, capitalising them
at 5 per cent., shareholders’ capital intact is £242,000, or
£4 16s. per share. That is, we repeat, assuming the true
profits are £6050 a half-year. On capital and reserves, as
shown in the balance-sheet, the profits are at the rate of
£2 19s. 2d. per cent, per annum.
There are two lots of City Bank shares; and
though both are paid to £B, one lot is quoted at 725. 6d.
and the other at 675. 6d. This is something like the his-
tory of the bank. It was established in 1863, arid £lO
was paid on the shares; but in 1871 £2 per share was
written off. For many years it had then a paid capital of
£240,000, in 30,000 £JO shares paid to £B. Twenty years
to the day previous to the date of the last meeting
(January 18) the shareholders decided to issue another
20,000 £lO shares to be paid up to £8 at £4 premium.
(They were the days of 12J per cent, divs.) Only £3 per
share had been got in by 1893 ; then it was hurriedly
decided to get in the other £9. Later in the same year
it was proposed to transfer from reserves enough to
restore the 30,000 shares to £lO paid and to pay up the
20,000 £8 shares to £lo—in other words, to reduce the
premium on the new shares to £2. But things happened
in 1893, and this benevolent scheme, though agreed to,
had to be abandoned in 1894; and, to give the public
confidence, the shareholders were at the same time loaded
with a reserve liability of £lO per share, so that the
present liability is this £lO per share on the original
30,000 shares and the £lO and the unpaid £2 on the new
20,000 shares.

A few years ago Q.N. shares, with their £2 liability, could
not be given away. Three years back it was suggested
that they might be worth taking at a few pence by way
of old-age pension. Eighteen months ago (July, 1905)
they were on sale at 3s. 3d. Now they can’t be got for
15s. (The shares are £3 paid.) The chief cause of all
this rapidity is no doubt the Kidston rearrangement,
under which the old Government money (locked-up
loans) carries £2 12s. 6d. per cent.; the bank gave 16
promissory notes for the amount, dated at half-yearly
intervals from June, 1907, to June, 1921. The first three
of these have been prepaid, and there is little doubt others
will also be prepaid. As soon as half the pro. notes have
been got rid of the bank can pay 3J per cent, dividend.
But three-fourths of the net profits have to go towards
repaying private depositors, to whom (apart from the
deposits now represented by interminable stock) £912,974
is still owing. As shown above, of the half-year’s dis-
closed profit £14,420 was used to write down old boom
assets, and of the balance of net profit £12,000 goes to
the depositors and £4OOO to reserve. Assuming that the
bank would go on writing down assets to the same
extent, then in such a term as the past half-year only
£4OOO would be available for dividend ; and it would
take £2066 to pay a half-year’s div. at the rate of 1 per
cent, per annum. After five more pro. notes have been
met, a dividend depends upon the view taken of the
necessity for continuous writing-down. It is pretty
certain, however, that, so that private depositors may
have their half-yearly “ cut,”the whole amount of earn-
ings over outgoings will not be written off ; and if the
depositors get their “ cut,” a quarter of the latter amount
must also be available, either for dividend or for reserve.
Still, in any case, until the depositors are paid, the
dividend cannot go beyond 3J per cent. ; and at double
the average rate of repayment hitherto, it will take 32
years to pay off the depositors. Many things may, how-
ever, happen in much less than 32 years.
Since the 1897 reconstruction the following pro-
gress has been made in connection with the old trouble: —

The Government deposits carry £2 12s. 6d. p.c. interest,
the interminable stock costs 3£ p.c., and the old private
deposits in the third item (representing 6s. in the £ of
the private deposits) are free. The amount payable on
the whole £5,234,963 is therefore £140,721, or at the
average rate of £2 13s. lOd. per cent. ; and that is a
pretty large swag of interest to carry. The fact that
the bank has practically 5J millions of fixed deposits,
costing on an average £2 13s. lOd per cent.—apart from
new deposits altogether—is a considerable handicap.
Of the new deposits (£2,605,941), probably no more than
£1,000,000 is free money ; and £1,000,000 of free money
alongside £7,000,000 of money at interest would mean
poor profits for any bank that didn’t have a remarkably
good and consistent borrowing connection. The Q.N.
certainly hasn’t got much idle money ; but against its
£5,650,184 of advances there are a good many securities
that it wouldn’t take now for choice.
The growth of new business in the Q.N. is sub-
stantial :

But the increase of new advances was probably very much
greater ; old assets have been realised and new advances
have taken their place, and the above shows only the net
increase. A rush of prosperity to Queensland would in
this way bring a double blessing into the £40,000 palace
of the Q.N. —it would allow the bank to get out (witness
the recent Mt. Morgan deal), and it might restore to
Queenslanders a little of that confidence in their own
State which many of them have lost, and that would
mean more new business for the Q.N. and the rest.

The £l5OO shown as written off last half-year is trans-
ferred to contingency account, and, if that is a reserve,
it is presumably an internal reserve, and will not appear
again in the accounts. In one respept it is as good as
if it were added to reserves; and so the fact that the
formal reserves have to be reduced in order to make the
appropriations isn’t so disgraceful r s it would otherwise
be. All the same, if the real prof.-s are no more than
the published accounts show, then the increased dividend
was not justified. Looking at the improvement that has
taken place in Queensland (apart, that is, from the gold-
fields), it seems highly probable that the Royal is now
using some undisclosed profits to write down securities
(which would mean that some of the reserves formerly
shown were very doubtful), or is making very proper pro-
vision against a turn in the tide by-and-bye. It isn’t
much use discussing how far a bank is justified in keep-
ing its disclosed profits about uniform, and putting aside
the extra profits of a good year to counterbalance the
extra losses of a bad year, without letting the public
know that there were either extra profits or extra losses.
If the public was a cool, calm public, and didn’t flutter
over either large banking losses or profits, the case in
favor of bankers doing what most people know that they
do, wouldn’t be so strong as it is. The only thing to do
under the circumstances is to note the trend of a bank’s
disclosed profits, &c.—a single half-year’s statement is of
little value in most cases.
And there is no doubt about the trend of things
with the Royal of Queensland. Nor is there any doubt
that it has the foundations for very much larger figures.
One way and another Queensland got into such a state
that either the public wouldn’t lend money to the Queens-
land banks or the banks wouldn’t take it. At December
31st, 1896, this bank had barely more than 30s. of the pub-
lic’s money to every 20s. of its own. Even now it has less
than £2 Is. of the public’s to £1 of its own—as com-
pared with a proportion of £7 to £1 in some southern
banks. Under such conditions large earnings are im-
possible. The rate on capital and reserves lately has
been :

Dec., 1904 313 5 „ Dec., 1906 4211 „
As for the past year’s progress in some other respects

There is here the usual accumulation of money—deposits
have outstripped advances. One result is that liquid
assets now represent just upon 40 per cent, of the lia-
bilities to the public. The bank’s deposits, compared
with capital, are small, and 40 per cent, of what it has
got is earning little or nothing. So the trouble now
seems to be, not that Queensland banks can’t get money,
but that Queensland hasn’t got a great deal of confidence
in itself. Under the circumstances, £4 2s. lid. percent
on funds are pretty fair earnings; they couldn’t be even
that high with much dead business on the books.

This is Australia’s one and only “ provincial ” bank, with
a head-office and no branches, and a business that puts a
good profit into shareholders’ pockets and reflects a good
deal of credit upon the people who run it. Even in those
sad days when a ribald youngster chalked “ Bung ” on the
door of the Commercial Bank’s new palace in Melbourne,
and when other youngsters might have scrawled the
same rude word on a good many other doors, the Bal-
larat Bank hadn’t to do more than reduce its dividend to
6J per cent. Then, as now, it traded largely with itsown
capital; at present its own funds amount to £155,674,
and its debts to the public (including deposits) are only
£242,515 ; and its non-liquid assets might slump 10s. Id.
in the £ below their book values, and the depositors
would still be safe. This means small profits, compara-
tively speaking; but it means small risks for depositors
and shareholders alike. On the basis of capital (£85,000)
and reserves combined, profits in recent terms have
been : , (
June, 1903 ..£4 13 11 p.c. June, 1905 ..£5 6 1 p,e.
Dec., 1903 .. 5 7 9 „ Dec., 1905 .. 6 6 8 „
June, 1904 .. 5 6 2 „ June, 1906 .. 6 6 2 „
Dec., 1904 .. 5 6 4 „ Dec., 1906 .. 5 6 1 „
Sticking to Ballarat, which is to a great extent shut in
and smothered by the rich landlord of both agricultural
and mining holdings, the Ballarat Bank thus makes very
slow progress. Five years ago it had £222,562 on deposit
and £289,350 out in advances ; its deposits are now
£242,515 and its advances £301,678. In the previous five
years it showed a shade more progress. For, of course,
it is progress, though it isn’t anything like it should be
in a district of Ballarat’s natural richness.

The Sulphide Corporation, Ltd. —mine at
Broken Hill, smelting works near Newcastle, office in

1904 65,512 7J „ „ „ 41,250 22,005 96,613
1905 102,422 10 „ „ „ 65,000 50,030 94,005
1906 136,364 10 „ „ „ 55,000 41,904 133,465
But, besides the amount shown as written off last year,
capital expenditure was written down by £14,115 out of
profits, that amount being charged direct to working
account, and £16,275 special expenditure due to creeps
in the mine was also charged to working account. Apart
from the latter item, the amounts written off for the
year are equivalent to the whole of the additions
to capital account plus some special writing
down (£1726) of stores, &c. The .total provided
for depreciation is thus £56,019. The capital is £550,00(1
preference and £412,500 ordinary (originally the issues
were the same, but, on the failure of the Ashcroft sulphide
process, ss. per share was written off the ordinaries). In
1897, £lOO,OOO worth of 5 per cent, debenture stock was
issued ; the co. only got 98J per cent, for this issue, and
must redeem at 105 at the end of December, 1907 ; but
only £79,600 is now outstanding. At June 30, 1905,
the liquid assets were £344,800; the liabilities,
including debentures, were £308,379, showing a
surplus of £36,421. Twelve months later, liquid
assets were £208,228, and liabilities £132,326,
showing a surplus of £75,902. So in 12 months the cash
position had improved £39,481 after paying a dividend of
£55,000. Outside the depreciation of stores, the amount
written off during the year was £54,293. The book-
value of mine and works was £1,129,041 ; so the amount
written off was a shade under 5 per cent. Though
the amount looks tremendous, 5 per cent, certainly
isn’t extravagant. What is extravagant is the £1,129,041
book value. As previously mentioned, on the failure of
the process which was to solve the sulphide problem out-
of-hand, ss. per £1 was written off ordinary shares ; but
the capital still stood at £962,500. In other words, only
£137,500 out of £1,100,000 was wiped out—which was
ridiculous, seeing that the process on which the co. was
formed (the Central mine being a mere secondary con-
sideration) had not only collapsed utterly, but dragged
about £150,000 of hard cash with it. Practically, there-
fore, the co. is now writing down out of profits what
ought to have been written down out of capital long ago.
The trouble is that the writing-down is being done at the
expense of ordinary shareholders; they can’t get a divi-
dend because the 5 p.c. required for writing-down pur-
poses takes so vast a bite. Reckoning all in, it has been-
enough in the past two years to have given ordinary
holders about 15 per cent, per annum. Certainly,
ordinary shares in the first place were issued against the
process that failed ; but it still is a question whether
pref. shareholders, who were supposed to have made the
most elaborate inquiries and tests, shouldn’t have also
suffered in the writing-down. The ordinary shareholders’
one crumb of comfort is that, when capital expenditure
has been sufficiently written down, they will still hold a
full three-seventh interest in the whole concern. Whether
they won’t be too dead to appreciate it is another
matter.
Meantime Sulphide Corporation ordinary share-
holders have to mourn over the tact that the book value
of the concern really isn’t being reduced, after all. The
mine has had a bad time in the way of creeps, &c., and
the plant has suffered so much that additions and re-
newals have to be made just as fast as values are written
down. Also, the co. tried to pioneer zinc-smelting in
Australia, and put up expensive works, and, though no
figures are available on the subject, it is certain that the
results have not given a fractional return on the total
expenditure. The co. has, in fact, had to grope its way,
do a vast amount of experimental work, and bear a cost
which the State might fairly have shared with it; or, at
any rate, the State might have held out something in the
way of reward for this special outlay. Still, with anything
like fair prices for metals, the situation is not absolutely
hopeless for ordinaries. But for the mine-smash, they
would certainly have got a mouthful this Christmas.
What that smash did is shown in the output of ore;
this and some other details follow:

than with the small, the smash took at least £lOO,OOO off
the year’s profits. And the ordinary shareholders wanted
the extra profit badly. With the 1905 output and the
1906 prices, the co. could easily have written off £60,000,
and then paid la per cent, all round.

The Dorset Bucket Dredging Company Is
building a complete plant for its claim in Tasmania.
The dredge is to be capable of treating 3000 cubic yards
of material per day ; the buckets have each a capacity of
6 cubic feet; and the tables have a surface of over 2880
square feet; the ladder is 66 feet in length between the
centres, and can dredge to a depth of 38 feet; al7 inch
centrifugal pump supplies the water to treat the material
in the sluice boxes, and in addition there is a 6 inch high
lift pump to operate on the nozzles : the material is dis-
integrated in a revolving screen, which is 33 feet long and
5 feet in diameter ; the machinery is supplied by power
from two 60 H.H.P. horizontal engines which are supplied
by steam from an externally fired multitubular boiler,
working under a pressure of 1501bs. to the square inch.
The p ntoons to carry the plant are constructed of
Tasmanian hardwood, and are 124 feet long by 40 feet
wide. To facilitate working at night the dredge is fitted
up with an efficient electric lighting plant. The whole
plant is the largest of its kind in the Southern Hemis-
phere, and is in the main being constructed by the Austral
Otis Engineering Company, of South Melbourne, under
the supervision of Bobert McLiutock, Engineer, of
Dunedin.*

The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluming,refrigerators, bolts, nuts,
and every discretion of cast or wrought iron work.*

The Reavell Air Compressor is an entirely new
departure in compressor design, and by its numerous
good points is already finding considerable favor in Au -
tralian mining circles. As there are no suction valves,
the volumetric efficiency is at least 5 per cent higher than
any other compressor. It is unrivalled for driving by
means of an electric motor, and is the only machine in
which driving gears can be altogether dispensed with.
The sole licensees for Australia are Babcock and Wilcox,
makers of the well-known water tube boiler, whose Aus-
tralasian office is at the Haymarket, Sydney.*

The Sulphide Corporation expected to have its
granulation plant ready to work on Block 10 slimes this
O)ontb> but Oaq’t manage it. The Corporation is, how-
ewer, having a bite at the slimes, sending a few hundred
tons to Cockle Creek direct. About 56,000 tons alto-
gether were purchased.
The Gillies zinc-extraction plant on B.H. Block
10 wks to have started at the beginning of last October,
but it is still struggling against wilful and perverse
circumstances. After spasmodic activity for several
weeks, during which many adjustments and a few con-
centrates were made, the plant is again idle, undergoing
alterations. Also, when the recently-enlarged Potter
plant on the British mine was started, all the machinery
was set going, but only one little pan was put into
operation, and it got a rather poor flotation. There has
been some talk of the British mine taking over this
plant.
r * * * *
Manager Palmer, of the Junction North,
Broken Hill, is protesting too much. He has made a
public statement that his shaft never at any time col-
lapsed through a creep in the Junction or his own mine.
Call it what you like; something happened that blew the
collar of the shaft into the air, and set the ground boom-
ing and the stopes smashing together in the Junction.
The Junction North is a good little mine, and has plenty
of ore to go on with, though there is still some doubt as
to how that ore will pan out in the mill. But, especially
if they were satisfied that they could mill their ore with-
out trouble, it would have been a wise thing had the
directors decided 12 months ago to sink another shaft.
The old shaft is solid enough at present, but it will
always be at the mercy of a further movement in the
Junction, next door. As for the mill, it is doing as well
as can be expected of a new plant on its probation.
* * * X
When the British, Broken Hill, had proved its
rich carbonate body at the 200 ft. level by crosscutting
16ft. into it. the work was stopped for no visible reason.
There was great cackling on ’Change over the laying of
this remarkable egg. But, after all, important as the
find is, all these carbonate bodies are irregular, unreli-
able, and always shallow.
* * « *
The new lode at the 600 ft. level of Block 14,
Broken Hill, carries good ore, but it is narrow, so far as
has been ascertained. The diamond drill, which months
ago found both walls—they were then about 20ft. apart
—probably told the truth about the matter.
* * * •
The Australian Smelting Corporation, which
followed on after the Zinc Corporation, begins to look a
trifle more like business. It is, anyhow, asking for ten-
ders for its railway embankment and for an excavation
for smelting-works at Port Kembla (N.S.W.), the site
recommended by metallurgist Savage. But there seems
very little likelihood that the Zinc Corporation will go
in for zinc smelting in Australia. For the present Ger-
many will get that work to do —thanks to the induce-
ments which its Government offers, and the apparent
unconcern of Australia whether it ships dirt (worth £2 a
ton) or spelter (worked up to £25 a ton).
• • * *
Budgery, the parent, is looking round for a
manager at £650 a year. If it doesn’t want a metallur-
gist-copper metallurgists in Australia are scarce and
dear—it ought to be able to get a miner who knows
enough about his work to straighten up the show. The
directorate, like that of Queen Bee, is local, flabby, and
indefinite. Queen Bee, which has been developing very
well (and development was badly wanted) could get an
option at £2 per share ; yet the market sticks at a shade
under 30s.—partly because of the management, partly
because the show isn’t as far away as N. Queensland is.
As to Budgery, the most important work is the winze,
which has crept down about 30ft., with good black ore in
the bottom. That proves, anyway, that the rich stuff is
more than a flake.
• * * *
Though the parent Budgery has had a shake-up
on the strength of satisfactory work in the winze, Cobar
(N.S.W.) district excitement has mostly turned towards
Canbelego. About three months ago, a syndicate,
mainly officials of the Boppy mines and their friends,
picked up the Canbelego copper mine dirt cheap. For
some time past the owner had kept the pot boiling by
gouging a few tons of rich ore out of three or four
shallow shafts and some costeens ; there are records of
70 tons averaging 28 per cent. The lode, which has
been proved for over 200 ft. by these openings, is to be
tapped by a new working shaft; and the syndicate
seems now to have got on to the top of it. A neigh-
boring show, the Shango, has also got good ore—quan-
tity unspecified. The lode in the Canbelego isn’t very
big, though in one of the shallow shafts there was a
gossan formation 6ft. wide. The Canbelego and Shango
syndicates are in 100 shares each.
True Blue is now the bonanza of Wyalong
(N.S.W.). Gold-bearing stone has been proved to a depth
of 1200 ft., and levels are being driven on a 2ft. reef
carrying rich sulphide. The number of employees (120)
will shortly be doubled, as a lot of backs are ready to be
operated on. The Homeward Bound has been floated
into a co. of 25,000 shares. Golden Fleece starts the year
with 116 tons treated at Cockle Creek for 3670 z. ; and
Shamrock, from 45 tons, got 1420 z. The White Reef
line is showing out well, but what is wanted, instead
of indiscriminate prospecting on four separate properties,
is co-operation in sinking one good, serviceable shaft.
* • • •
Mt. Lyells have been to above 605., giving the
show a value of over £3,300,000. Any way the mine is
looked at, this is a pretty tall valuation. Rise is moie
due to the bears in London and Australia covering up than
to genuine investment. Position is that the co. has at last
got its sink at the North Lyell mine from 700 ft. to 850 ft.
finished in both the main shaft and the engine winze,
so a drive is going out from both to connect the workings.
The object of this drive, too, is to test the country ; but
if the story told be true, that the ore makes east, then
there cannot be much use for the drive save for ventila-
tion and mine purposes. Buying seemed to be prompted
because a little ore was got near the winze, and Queens-
town folk talked about a second formation. Work so far
does not prove much this way. The country is minera-
lised schist, but that is the nature of the North Lyell
formation, the fact being that rich patches sweeten up
everything. All the same, they have to be searched for ;
and that is the reason why the drill is always poking
about directly a level is opened up. Judging by appear-
ances, the ore body is still making east, and it is that
direction that has to be watched.
* * • *

* * * *
The recent joy in the Day Dawn camp, Charters
Towers, has faded. Early last year a drill was started at
a vertical depth of 1757 ft., and went down at an angle of
70 degrees east for 500 ft. The Block and Wyndham, on
the eastern side, shared the cost. At 425 ft. a 4ft. reef of
whitish stone was reported. The drill went on for
another 70ft. in footwall country. Now, a 4ft. reef should
be a very difficult thing to miss with a monkey-shaft; yet
that is what has happened. Even allowing for the erratic
nature of Towers reefs, anything up to 400 ft. of sinking
should have met the reef. Last reports show the monkey-
shaft down 580 ft., and only a couple of leaders have been
seen. The Block and Wyndham is working the Talisman
reef with satisfactory and fairly consistent results. This
Talisman reef was supposed to have got in front of the
drill that the Day Dawn used ; but the wretched thing
left before the monkey-shaft got there.
• i
The shaping of Brilliant Stockholm, outside
Charters Towers, was the field’s greatest disappointment
last year. Everything promised well for the co., which
was sorely in need of a rise. A crushing was torn out
from the straight shaft, and proved the reef to be large,
but low grade. Unfortunately, on opening out, it was
discovered that the shaft had passed through about all
there was of it; little could be found by crosscutting, or
in levels. At 714 ft. v.d. a second crosscut is going in to
hunt for another patch.
* * * *
Brilliant Freehold, Charters Towers, after
much marking time, has decided to advance—upon the
market. The wire-pullers point out that the No. 7 level
in Mills’ is looking lovely, and heading straight for the
Freehold. But they don’t ment on any of the other levels
in Mills’, though they happen to be closer to the Free-
hold’s boundary. The top ground in Mills’ was going to do
wonders for the cats that hung on to the eastern side.
There was a rush to pick up School Reserve scrip ; then
the crushings panned out ldwt. East Mexican went sky-
high, and the co. took advantage of the favorable market
to issue fresh shares. In place of the expected 4ft. reef,
they got a 2-in. leader. Yet both these shows are in a
more favorable position to locate and work the reef than
the Freehold is.
* * * *
Golden Alexandra, Charters Towers, carries a
capital of £25,000, in 100,000 shares divided into 40,000
paids and 60,000 contribs. Its underlay is getting close
to the boundary, and the ground in front is known as the
Alexandra Block, It was taken up as a spec. The
Alexandra wanted that block, or someone persuaded it
that it did ; so a meeting was called to get permission to
amalgamate. The voting was 41,000 for and 25,000
against. The motion was therefore lost, as a three-
fourths vote was required to increase the capital. Matters
were soon rectified, however, for when the amalgamation
scheme fell through, the Block was purchased for £3OOO
cash, without increasing the capital. The £3OOO may not
be too much for the land, as there is excellent grass
growing on it.
Wee Macgregor, Cloneurry (Q.), is really one of
the cheekiest floats of this boom. It is in 300,000 shares
of 10s. ; £35,000 went to working capital; vendors got
£15,000 cash, and are to get 125,000 fully paid shares
(when the London gang decides to let the vendors’ shares
loose), for a property which hadn’t a pick in it at time of
flotation. Warden Linedale, in his November report,
says: “The Wee Macgregor has also been slightly pros-
pected, but appearances are not too favorable.” Other
people who have seen the group say they are fine surface
shows. In his December report Warden Linedale says:
“At the Wee Macgregor prospecting has just been com-
menced... but the work is not sufficiently advanced to
enable me to speak with any certainty of these holdings.”
For all that, shares have been boomed up to 18s. Fact
is, Tolhurst’s firm got an option on 25,000 shares at par
for underwriting the first issue ; and the directorate—
Harvey Patterson, Weinberg, Saddler, and Tolhurst—did
the rest.
Glassford Creek (Queensland) will have to do
better or be reckoned up as another skite mine ; 333 tons
yielded 8£ tons copper, 3020 z. silver, and 18oz. gold.
Previous return was 32 tons copper, 10790 z. silver, and
620 z. gold. Shareholders who live handier to the mine
than Sydney is are shedding shares at 4s. or so.
* • * •
A Queensland paper on some alleged great
copper mines in Central Queensland : “ The Mt. Warmin-
ster Lead and Copper Co. is still in the development
stage, the big main ore body being as yet undiscovered.”
That does seem to be a bit of a drawback.
• • • •
Mt. Molloy (N.Q.) has driven 195 ft. at the
270 ft. level, and has discovered some dense iron pyrites
assaying about 3 per cent, copper. The manager con-
siders this a most important development. And so it is,
if there is enough pyrites ore to smelt the large accumula-
tion of silicious seconds, for it would relieve the co. of the
expense of putting up a concentrating plant. But it is
folly to rush at conclusions in front of development; Mt.
Garnet was an instance of that.
* « * •
As the Mitchell River Gold and Antimony Co.
(N.Q.) has been successfully got off—shares were doubly
subscribed in Australia, London and Glasgow—it won’t
be long now before a practical test is made of the new
M’Arthur antimony-gold extraction process. If it fails,
it won’t be for want of ore at the Mitchell River claims,
though it is doubtful whether the gold contents come up
to the reports.
•» * * *
The Dead Finish, the N.Q. tin show that has
come into the southern markets lately, was sold by the
men who found it, and who worked it for a while, for
£4000; and most of them are good tin-men, too. As
they are supposed to be still very well satisfied with the
price, the public can get a line as to what value the men
who know it best put on it. Still, this can be said for the
Dead Finish : if it is only a little thing, it is likely to last.
It is a narrow lode—as far as can be seen, it will not
average 3ft. —but it is strong and compact at over 100 ft.
deep. It is within about six miles of a battery.
• • * *
“M.C.”: Re “ Yarranderie’s ” inquiry about
smelting mixed copper and lead ore. There are no great
metallurgical difficulties ; it is mainly a question of quan-
tity. Chillagoe is completing furnaces for the special
treatment of ore assaying about 3 p.c. copper, 15 p.c.
lead, and lOoz. silver. The copper is recovered in the
form of matte from the lead furnaces, and is subject to
further treatment in the copper furnace. The trouble is
to find a smelter near enough that handles this special
kind of ore. Each smelter tries to standardise charges
of ore, flux, and fuel, in definite proportions; conse-
quently ore of a mixed nature can only be treated in a
furnace that deals particularly with that class of material.
Mixed lead-copper ore is very extensively treated in
America; also, as before remarked, in Chillagoe (Q.).
Metal deductions to cover loss are heavy, and in most
cases freight and smelting tariff eat up the return if the
ore has to be shipped a long distance. It doesn’t follow
that smelting cos. are robbers, as an agitation going on in
Queensland would imply.
* » * *
Maybe as the result of the Xmas holiday rush
of visitors to Daylesford (Vic.) and the adjacent
Hepburn mineral springs, there was a fleeting inflation of
Daylesford stock on the Melbourne market. Sarah
Bernhardt’s “ golden drone,” at its bygone best, was not
half so auriferous as the whisperings of Daylesford
tipsters in Collins-street, For a few days the little boom
looked vigorous; then its cheek took a sickly pallor, and
it seemingly went into a precarious state of health.
Thus far there has been nothing save the Ajax to
justify a revival of faith in the quartz of the volcanic
Daylesford district. The Ajax, which was started on
deceptive indications, struck an unexpected reef some
months ago, and now looks like a durable property.
Three or four 28. divs. have raised the shares
from Bs. last Easter to £6 ss. buyers at time of
writing-, and the price has been higher. But it takes
a lot of solid proof below ground to make an early 2s.
dividend mine a good investment at 1255. per share. As
for the specs, that emanated from the Ajax surprise—the
North Ajax, Nuggetty Ajax, the Florence, etc. —they
appear to be cats of the wildest nature. Local pre-
dictions, though naturally unanimous re the Ajax, are
stubbornly conflicting as regards the prospects of the
lesser claims. In the beginning of the New Year the
Ajax people tipped the Argyle as a certain winner ; but,
as soon as shares had risen a shilling or two, the insiders
took their profit, and the stock fell clean out of the quota-
tions. This much may be said for mountainous Dayles-
ford, with its extinct volcanoes and its rich a luvial past.
For 30 years i t was one of the drowsiest and most neglected
of old Yic. gold countries. Its long-standing reputation
for poverty in the deep ground—“ the Daylesford reefs
never go down ” —has not yet been fully proved to any
wide extent. What happened in the Ajax property may
happen again in one or other of the claims that are now
being- worked, or are yet to be taken up. The great
public of mining share buyers has not nearly exhausted
all temptations to lose money in looking for Daylesford’s
hidden possibilities.
* * * *
A long neglected reefing field, Twist Creek,
nearYackandandah (Vic.), is waking up. A local syndicate,
the Go Ahead, previously mentioned as raising ounce
stone, put through 56 tons for Xmas, which realised 180
oz. and a div. of £B4 a share. Lease comprises 48 acres,
and includes the Scandinavian, Shamrock, and Keep-it-
Dark reefs, parallel and only a few yards apart. All of
them paid, till the water got too heavy. Records avail-
able show that 1557 cons gave 1288 oz. The Polar Star (an
English co.’s) is west of the Go Ahead, and has cleaned
out an old tunnel and prepared to go further to get the
reef. South is Olingen’s Little Tunnel, which is putting
300 tons through the English co.’s new battery. There
are now three batteries on the Creek, and every squib is
being investigated.
* * # •
Lord Nelson, St. Arnaud (Vic.), has cut the reef
at 1800 ft. not a day too soon. Co. went on for years
under old Zebina Lane—the Westralian Zeb’s father —
totting up divs. until the Board got sleepy and indif-
ferent as to the future. Zeb plre firmly believed
the lode would not underlie out of the lease. Directors
hadn’t the sense to have an opinion of their own.
So they let the next-door block slip away from them
when it could have been.got for next to nothing. Their
crowning act lately was to put up a big battery at a cose
of thousands. They had by that time indisputable
evidence as to the underlay of the reef; still
there was a doubt whether the pitch of the
shoot north might not carry it into a block
held by the company away from the Lord Nelson North.
Ordinary horse-sense would have suggested proving this
block before building a big battery. Co. now has a pretty
solid debt; it isn’t at all sure about the quantity of stone it
will get in its mine ; and it is less certain as to the quality.
Kecent crushings got down to about sdwt. Winze stone
from 1700 ft. to 1800 ft. certainly averages above that; but
the winze may have gone down in a good part of the
shoot, and as the lode has been disturbed by a fault it is
wise not to build on what’s ahead at No. 18 until the pick
proves the extent of the gold.
* * * *
The old Betha.nga mine, over which the late
J. A. Wallace first spent scores of thousands without a
win, and where J. Bull in late years was not able to do
any better, has slipped into other hands. Agar Wynne
and David Syme still have their bit in the show, but are
joined by a well-to-do crowd who think that there is a
turn-over, if nothing else, in the claim. Wallace mined all
the time, but the ore was too refractory. Chlorination,
cyaniding, ordinary battery treatment, all failed, so that
about all that remains to try is smelting. Ore carries a fair
percentage of copper as well as gold, with arsenic and
zinc to make things pleasant. Now that the water is out,
estimates of the ore in sight are made. These are all very
well, but there is a good deal of difference between having
metals in an ore and getting them out. There is very
little chance of cheap work at the Bethanga unless big
capital is forthcoming. What present co. could do would
be to prove the mine ; then it might get the irresistible
Baillieus and modest Tolhurst to pass it on to Bull per
Lionel Robinson.
• * * *
During 1906, 16 Bendigo mines paid only £4OOO
less than, a quarter of a million of dividends. Besides
numerous private mines, there were also 151 non-dividend
paying mines searching for gold. From 1851 to 1906—55
years —Bendigo sent 73 millions sterling of gold to Mel-
bourne. No other goldfield on earth holds such a record.
Miles of shallow ground still remains to be prospected,
and numerous neglected mines await re-opening ; and it
seems certain that the chief work during 1907 will be
carried on in comparatively shallow ground, for which
class of enterprise an enthusiastic and well-equipped bar-
racker has lately turned up in the person of George D.
Meudell.
The December return from Golden Pole (W.A.)
was £3742 from 1500 tons, or only 495. lOd. per ton,
although the reserves on October 30 were said to be
worth 645. per ton. The reason given for closing' down
the mill is that the Government dam is dry, and there is
no fresh water for the boilers. As all the mines in the
State had once to use condensed water for their boilers,
the excuse is rather lame.
The Kalgurli (W.A.) has sunk its main shaft to
1410 ft., and has stopped sinking, and commenced opening
out at 1250 ft. and 1350 ft. The lode was in the shaft for
the full distance from 1200 ft., and ranged from 2dwt. to
13dwt. The main ore shoots are on the east and west of
the shaft, and, if they prove as consistent as ever, the ore
reserves will be enormously increased. During the past
year the main shaft has been sunk 300 ft., and levels
pushed ahead at 1050 ft. and 1150 ft. During this period
112,800 tons of ore have been treated, yet the reserves
have gained on the mill to the extent of 102,000 tons.
During the current year it is expected that 132,000 tons
will be treated, but the ore reserves should continue to
gain on the mill, as two fresh levels are already started,
whereas last year the 1150 ft. level was only started in
March. The ore in sight, taking costs at 275. lOd. per
ton, inclusive of all expenses except depreciation, shows a
profit of 325. lOd. per ton, or £1,067,000. This works out
at 178 s. per share.
* * * *
The main shaft at the Hainault, Kalgoorlie, is
down 800 ft., and is to be continued to 1000 ft., and fresh
levels driven at 850 ft. and 950 ft. When the new
plant is running, the output will be increased to 7000
tons, and the profit to about £3500 monthly. At present
this seems the very best the co. is likely to do, as the ore
reserves are only valued at 315., all in, and costs aTtr
hardly likely to be under a guinea. Some optimists talk
the scrip much higher, but there seems no reason for this.
A fine body of ore is being opened up at the 750 ft. level,
and, as ore in sight is estimated at 241,000 tons, holders
of scrip have no cause for anxiety for some time to come.
* * * »
Brown Hill Extended, Kalgoorlie, which has
been in the dumps for some time, has hired the North
Kalgurli 10-head mill, and will crush about 1200 tons
monthly in future. Manager Johns, who is one of the
most conservative managers on the field, reckons the
result will be about 40s. a ton ; but a better figure is
looked for by many people who have gone into the
matter. This mine has long been under a cloud, but
may continue a producer for a long time to come.
• » * «
Great Opliir, Davyhurst (W.A.), is a good
mine likely to go wrong through bad finance. The mine
possesses a wide schist formation averaging about 365.
per ton, and, in the oxidised zone, already 40,000 tons of
ore are exposed. Some 1500 ft. away the same ore body
has been exposed fully 12ft. wide and worth over 40s. per
ton. A strong financial crowd will some day get hold of
this and adjoining propositions, and then Davyhurst will
emerge from the obscurity brought about by the collapse
of the Golden Pole, Waihi, Eileen and other patchy
ventures.
* . * * *
Red Hill (W.A.), which has been silent for
months, is again being nibbled at. Go. has acquired the
Sons of Erin and adjoining blocks at Higginsville, to
which the plant is being, transferred. The reef on the
new property, though averaging only 2ft., is consistent,
and has been proved for a length of 3000 ft., though it
has barren patches. Manager Campion has great faith
in its future, and sees small divs. ahead. The long-
threatened Norseman railway, if ever built, will pass
close to the mine and materially reduce costs.
• * * *
Sons of Gwalia, Leonora (W.A.), continues to
open out well at the seventh and fourteenth levels, where
most exploratory work is being done. In the seventh
level, the lode at 1270 ft. south is' Bft. wide, and worth
80s. per ton : while in a rise at 1210 ft. south, values are
60s. over a width of 6ft. At the fourteenth level, at
830 ft. south, the lode carries values of 09s. per ton over a
width of 9ft. The importance of these discoveries may
be gauged when it is remembered that the eo.has bean
earning a profit of £5000.t0 £6OOO a month on ore averag-
ing less than 30s. per ton.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
.Routine is sometimes good medicine for
ennui or neurasthenia. An old circus horse,
which had for years carried the lady with the
abbreviated gauze skirts over hurdles and through
fire-hoops, and had steadily galloped its jog gallop
round the sawdust ring to the sound of the band,
while the gentleman jockey rider vaulted and
pirouetted and somersaulted on it 3 reliable and
unshrinking back, was, in the fullness of time, re-
tired to the old age pension of a nice grass paddock
in Maoriland. Instead of getting plump and fat,
and capering in wild freedom round the enclosure,
it grew sad and hung its under-lip in the grass.
The farmer filled it up with horse medicines, with
no result. Long, hairy neighboring farmers came
and looked at the horse from afar off, as Eliphaz
the Temanite, and Bildad the Shuhite and Zophar
the Naamathite looked at Job’s boils through a
telescope in Uz, and they saw that the grief of
the old crock was very great, though they could
not imagine the cause. The farmer sent for the
circus-man and bade him take the old circus-horse
away, as he did not want him to set a bad example
by dying in the nice grass paddocks. The circus-
man took the dilapidated wreck back to his show,
and the moment the ruin heard the sound of the
band, and smelt the odor of sawdust, it lifted its
under-lip off the ground, took its feed, grew strong
again, and for many years thereafter it carried
the equestrienne lady with the abbreviated gauze
skirts over hurdles and through fire-hoops as gaily
as if it were a mere giddy foal.
MODERN FARMING.
It is time the farmers used the other constituents of
plant food in the wheat-field in addition to the super-
phosphates.—Agricultural Paper.
“ Blame me if a feller isn't called on to create a
new heaven and a new earth for hisself I”
The old, abandoned shafts are not the
only death-traps near mining towns. Now that
barges and dredges are nosing their way over old
workings, races have been cut for miles through
the bush to carry water to dams, for a summer
supply. At the dams a grating is fixed to catch
the flotsam and jetsam carried along by the water.
When the little bush kiddy goes missing, it is at
one of these gratings he is looked for —and often
found. A grating was being cleaned out recently,
and among a miscellaneous collection of branches,
leaves, sticks, and small insects, were found three
young wombats, a calf, two pigs, four dogs, one
’possum (with a rabbit-trap on his front paw),
several rabbits, a porcupine, two hawks, a crow,
and innumerable small birds. If the oldest in-
habitant had been missing he would probably
have turned up there also. The water in some of
these races travels at from three to fifteen miles
an hour.
* * * «
Two stories of early Australian life, re-
markable for their grim Jiumor :
A man was condemned in Goulbuni to be hanged in
Sydney. The mode of transition was by dray, in which
the prisoner was placed, together with the gallows, the
rope, the coffin and a constable. When the extraordinary
collection arrived at the spot where the Town Hall now
stands, a crowd of people, dogs and children accompanied
it. Some with fervid expectancy of a free show, hurried
ahead. “ Don’t hurry,” cried the man who was to be
the star performer in the subsequent entertainment.
“Don’t hurry; the fun doesn’t commence till I get
there.”
Talk about luck. The usual sentence for bushranging
had been passed, and he was to be hung on Gallows Hill.
But he wouldn’t hang. Each time the rope tightened it
broke, and he dropped from the low gallows onto his
feet. Three attempts were made, and then the death
sentence was commuted. It was subsequently dis-
covered that the rope used had been lying for a long
time in the Government store. Above it was a small
hole in the roof, through which the rain had dropped
on it and rotted it. When it was unwound these rotted
portions occurred at regular intervals. The man after-
wards went to Tasmania, where he was appointed—above
all things—hangman.
* * -K *
“The Geyser” : I have read “Silas’”par.
re bulls and bullocks (B. 27/12/’OG). Have been
an ox-conductor for 15 years, and am quite posi
tive that a bullock will decline and die if worked
constantly in the same yoke as a bull. Have
known ordinary bullocks to be worked for years
without any ill-effects alongside gelded bulls—
better known to teamsters as stags, flatbeads, and
tackhammers—which I think proves that the
death of the bullock is due to the very rank breath
of the bull; for, after a year or so of gelded life,
these stags lose that kill-stone-dead odor which
was so characteristic of them when in their
natural state. I can realise the necessity of
changing the bull’s mate regularly, but “Silas”
is mistaken when he says the bullocky works
“billy” in the pole to prevent him carrying
physical contamination to his mate. Any bullock-
puncher will tell you that bulls and stags are
mostly pointers, and for this reason, and because
they are very strong in the neck, he is content to
let them carry the pole, and only stands them up
in earnest when in a tight place, or when bogged.

Some information re Mildura (Vic.) culled
from a person who went there for work, and
didn’t get it: —
All the havenues—they call all the streets runnin’one
way havenues —are plarnted with different trees, and
when you arsk the way anywhere a bloke’ll say : “ Go up
here till you come to a line of blue gum trees, then turn
up till you come to a line of pepper trees ; go on till you
come to a line of cocoanuts, and then look out for a row of
bananas.” Questioned as to what transpired beyond the
bananas, he said: “ Blowed if I know. I never got
beyond the bananas. I expect coral or seaweed or some-
thing, if you go far enough.”

“ Agricola ” : I was lately passing from
Wileannia to Milparinka (N.S.W.), atid struck a
small waybaek school —regulation little galvanised
iron gehenna. The kid-puncher was an old friend
ot mine in the happy days when we
growled at the heat of Sydney, so I
accepted his offer of a rest in the school
till his lunch-time and leisure. He had
a full muster—three blacks, four whites,
and five brindles. Shortly I was seized
with an awful nausea, and feared heat
apoplexy, though it was rather early in
the year for that. I mentioned my
approaching demise to the nigger-
driver, and he laughed. lie said it
was only the smell of the aboriginal
class, and reckoned it acted that way
on him for the three months after he
first struck it. It took two mulga
whiskies to settle my stomach for
dinner. Teachers out-back are told
that they have no ambition —likewise
that they cause the Education Depart-
ment much trouble answering applica-
tions for removal. As to the ambition,
if you put that smell on top of the
early ambition of a Napoleon he would
become a retail greengrocer.
“ L.M.” on mules :
Odd and varied are the loads of the pack mule. In
North Queensland once I met a big hybrid carrying a
bored expression, also two waggon-wheels, a single-furrow
plough, and a 200-gallon square iron tank. I asked the
mule man in charge if the tank was empty. His eyes
bulged with something like contempt. “Empty be
blowed,” he roared ; “ think I do packin’ fer sport ? Why,
man, there’s close on 40 bags er sugar in that tank, besides
terbackc; ... 1 it 1 '— things.” I believe he was a liar.
Knew of a mule, Cooktown way, who once did duty as
a hearse. Two coffined corpses (Chinamen) were slung,
one on each side, and securely strapped. Then, amid
much jabbering and banging of gongs and other music,
the procession started. The sight of a dozen volcanoes
belching forth Celestials wouldn’t surprise most mules ;
but something—the curse of Joss, or snakes in his ear,
perhaps—scared this one. Suddenly he planted his hind
feet in the stomach of the nearest Mongolian, and shot
down the track, like a devil rushing to a public-house.
Some time later a mob of howling heathens were busy
gathering up splinters of coffin and lost funerals distri-
buted over a live-mile course through the scrub. When
last heard of, the mule was racing a railway train towards
the Palmer.
A Normanton mule-conductor told me he once packed
a 12ft. live alligator a 20-mile trip. The reptile was
enclosed in a strong rope net, and fastened frog-fashion
along the mule’s back. Didn’t ask him the length of the
mule, size of the net, or whether he chloroformed the
gator to get him there.
A timber-getter who had been insulted and aspersed
by an extra nimble mule, swore to get even. He caught
the mule, blindfolded him, backed him to the edge of a
bank with a sheer drop of 30ft. to the river, and then
tickled his heels. The mule lashed out, and, feeling
nothing but vagueness beneath his nether part, stretched
his neck and grabbed the nearest support, which happened
to be the timber-getter. They fell together and came
out together—the half-drowned human (who was a poor
swimmer) on the mule’s back. The latter selected his
landing place, scrambled out, allowed his rider to dis-
mount, then promptly kicked him in again. There the
timber-getter stayed, clutching mud and rushes, until
his enemy got tired and went home.

AN ARDUOUS LIFE.
OLD GENT : “ Your wife and daughter keep a laundry I And what do you do? ”
BILL JONES : “I post their correspondence.”
An Irish terrier in S.A. has been going
strong for the canine endurance record. Just
where the suburbs of Adelaide touch the hills,
tnere is an old lead mine, which, as is usual with
old mines, has been left as a man-trap for future
generations. One Weidenbach, living near by,
lost his dog on December 11th. On January 6th
he passed the mine, heard the faithful animal
calling for help, and went down with a rope to
bring up a dog that was very thin and sorry for
itself, but not a corpse. There was no sign of
food or water in the shaft, and, presuming the dog
fell down there the day it was first missed, it put
in 26 days underground.
> * • *
“ W.P.8.” : “ The Prooshan ” (B. 3/l/’O7)
says that the white cockatoo runs the crow a good
second for cunning. I cannot agrde. The cocka-
too is a fool-bird that yells like blue blazes at
lunch or any other time. If he were cunning he’d
shut up, instead of attracting attention like that.
As for not being able to get within 200yds. of
them, I’ve shot dozens, and I hadn't to exercise
any particular wisdom to do it.

‘ ‘ Onawe ” : Among the Maoris it is the
custom, when one of them dies, to burn down the
whare he turned his toes up in. His belongings
suffer the same fate, or are buried with him.
When the men of the tribe see a case is hopeless,
they generally carry the moribund person to the
most dilapidated whare in the kainga —one they
can spare. So Maori policies are not popular
among insurance agents.

“ H.G.” (Maryborough): Appeared re-
cently a fish of the “ tetrodon ” family, which,
by its powerful jaws and equally powerlul teeth,
crunches the oysters in the vicinity of Sandy
Straits and Hervey Bay. One well-known oyster-
man estimates his loss from this fish at not less
than £2OOO. Previous to the last drought they
were unknown in tins quarter, but it seems as if
they have come to stay.

“ Falder ” : Be much recent nonsense
about lining bees to their holes. All of us round
here get part of our living with honey, and we
look lor nests in autumn, when there’s a fair
chance of getting a full one. We don’t worry
about following stray bees. We patrol the likely
timber, watching the tree-tops till we sight a few
bees flying round about one spot; usually a hole
in a dead limb. We then fall the tree, daze the
bees with smoke, and cut out the honey, the big-
gest trees usually yielding the smallest supply.
Ouiier times the nests are found hanging on rocks.

“ Crabhole ” : The usual someone wants
to know how to get rid of meat ants. Let me
supply the funeral. Get enough bran bags (or
any other sort of bags), and spread them on top
of the holes. Then make an embankment of loose
earth all round the edge of the bags. The ants
won’t appreciate this attention, and will promptly
move on to where bran-bags don’t block their
doorways.
Tumut (N.S.W.) has seen the bunyip.
It’s in the local lagoon :
The creature has a cry like a seal, and very much
resembles this well-known amphibious specimen about
the head. The tail is described as being like that o( a
kangaroo, running from a fair" thickness at the root to a
taper at the punt. The bunyip swims rapidly, and as it
glides along keeps its head above water. Its length, from
the tip of the nose to the extremity of the tail, has been
set down at about 4ft., and the color of the creature is
reported to be black.
The yarn conies so circumstantially this time that
Sydney Museum authorities are sending an inves-
tigating expedition. Quite probable that Aus-
tralian inland waters have a creature of the otter
variety. But if the bunyip is to shrink down to
an otter four feet from snout to tail, a big romance
will founder on a very small and prosaic reality.
IN THE SMART SET.
SHE: “ You think it’s funny, but I think it's
foul.”
Dear Bulletin. — Be incongruous names.
'Way up here on the nor’-west (Queensland)
mineral field is a small postal township named
Friezland. Seeing there is only an envelope
between this place and Tophet, it should be
known as Frizzleland. Friezland ! Why, there
isn’t an adult inhabitant of Fryslaud who wouldn’t
give his best Sunday loincloth and a bottle of
lukewarm rum to anyone who could make any-
thing freeze for five minutes. About the only
frosts liable to occur are in connection with some
of the wild-cat mining ventures. Freezeland is
the natural home of the ’cat. So numerous and
voracious is that animal, that anyone is a fool and
a gambler who goes out after dark without a
loaded gun.—Yours on the rim of Sheol, Mallaba-
POONIE.
THE TOWNSHIP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There’s an old one-horse township on Ballarat-road,
Like a derelict hull,
Grown dingy and dull,
On the bank of a stream that tumultuous flowed,
When life in the 'fifties exuberant glowed
On Ballarat-road.
It looks as if burdened with centuries grey ;
Though withered and mean
It once was the scene
Of luminous night, and of roystering day ;
And lusty carousing of men that were gay
From Ballarat way.
Here halted the team with its mountainous load
Of cornsack and bale,
And Her Majesty's mail
At “ The Rest and Be Thankful,” that cheerily glowed,
Changed horses and sipped of the neetar that flowed
By Ballarat-road.
Here tramped it together through thick and through
thin,
With blankets of blue,
Old mates that were true,
Who lustily cheered when, with wheels all a-spin,
With clanking of swords, and importance, and din.
The escort came in.
Now, worn with the winds and the rains that erode,
Its days are outrun ;
The alluvial’s done.
The trains and machinery follow the lode—
It’s long since the tide of prosperity flowed
Down Ballarat-road.
vie.
A.B.C.

“ Mallarapoonie ” : 1 sat smoking in the I
scanty shade of a desert gum. A string of black
ants hurried like absconding cashiers between
two holes, some 40ft. apart, among the bare hot
stones and wilted spmifex. There were other
strings of ants junctioning into the main line.
These are the records : —782 ants passed a given
point, going both ways, within five minutes. Of
these 21 humped grass-seeds, 15 carried manna,
six bore along ant-eggs, five hauled one dead
caterpillar, three struggled with a grasshopper’s
hind leg each, and one staggered along under the
dead weight of a defunct relative. The other 730
carried nothing, but hustled to and fro like 730
life assurance agents. If these ants were union
ants, and worked union hours, the result of a
day’s work would be that 70,176 ants would hustle
like all Gehenna in the hot sun to no purpose ;
2016 would trundle a grass-seed every time ; 1440
would each lump manna ; 576 would daily help
along the birth rate ; and 480 ants would yank 96
dead caterpillars over hot stones; while 288
others were battling the back limbs of 144 grass-
hoppers down a hole. During the day 96 funerals
would pass by (which seems rather a high death
rate!). Now can any Bulletin reader, whose
wisdom is as the wisdom of the snake, inform me
why it was necessary for those other 70,176 ants
to hustle at all, seeing they weren’t required to
handle any merchandise? Does Nature waste
all that ant energy ? And supposing they had
simply got to hustle, mightn’t they just as well
hustle with an umbrella apiece to keep the hot
sun off their bald heads ?

“ See Gee ” writes :
Horsham (Victoria), which held the Y.O. for beer-
drinking, lately attempted a strike against an increase
in the price of the brown substance from 3d. to 6d.
Following the publicans’ announcement, a meeting of
hard drinkers was called, at which oratory poured forth
as from a burst cask. A professor of cookery besought
the meeting “ not to take away his beer.” The chair-
man, in a voice that savored of a reading from the 4th
chapter of Crocodiles, commanded the crowd to stand
shoulder-to-shoulder (possibly to prevent it falling), but
when a temperance orator, taking advant ge of a
heated argument between the chair and a beer-chewer con-
cerning who was going to shout, informed the thirsty
mob that he had never tasted liquor in his life, he
was told that “he did not know what he had missed.”
The cold-tea advocate called upon bis “berruthers” to
take the pledge, and was rewarded with one convert—the
aforesaid chairman. Then 400 thirst-maddened sand-
heaps fled as the fly crows for the nearest public-house.

“H.7.H.”: Bulletin (29/11/’O6) errs in
calling Rose’s trip from Lissadell, Ord River
(W.A.), to Charleville, the record stock-drive. In
1884 M‘Donaid Bros, took out cattle from Goul-
burn (N.S.W.) to stock Fossil Downs, 200 miles
east of Derby (W. A.). Rose’s trip covered 2500
miles, and McDonald’s was well over 3300 miles.
Rose delivered 2100 out of 3750 he started with,
and he was never off settled and well-opened-up
country.

“ Cabby,” in a depraved frame of mind :
The average Australian back-blocks child gets his ideas
strangely mixed. Was at one time teaching in a little
country school near Narandera (N.S.W.). Some of the
pupils attended the Presbyterian Sunday school in that
town, and their parents drew the attention of the minis-
ter to the ungodly state of my flock. One day he called
and asked permission to give a little “ instruction.” He
commenced with prayer, but was rudely interrupted.
“ Please, Pm a Carthlic,” came a reproachful voice. The
“black sheep” was promptly ejected, and he was the
only one who enjoyed that quarter-hour.
The minister discoursed on “ Peace ” to the mystified
children. “And now, my dears,” he asked presently,
“ can anyone tell me who it was that brought Peace into
the world?” Blank-eyed, open-mouthed, the youngsters
stared, till suddenly an inspired whisper went round, and
all hands were waved aloft. “ The King ! ” they yelled.
The minister appealed to one of the Sunday school
pupils. “You ought to know, Gordon.”
A light shone suddenly over Gordon’s mind, and by its
rays he got the lay of the ground. He rose unsteadily to
his feet, and in an uncertain tone faltered, “ God.”
“ Yes,” said the parson briskly, “ or to put it more cor-
rectly, His Son. And because His Son brought Peace
into the world, He is called The Prince of ”
“ Wales 1” came in triumphant chorus from thirty little
heathen Australians.
The minister never called again, much to the regret of
“ the Carthlic.”

“ Quidam ” : Be using native shrubs and
trees for ornamental purposes (“Janor,” B.
3/l/’O7). Let me bring under notice the dead-
horse creeper of Rockhampton (Q.) scrubs. -The
stock joke in its locality is to take an inex-
perienced stranger up to the creeper, which bears
great masses of pretty, white, lilac-spotted flowers,
and watch him fall down on account of the smell.
The dead-horse creeper would be eminently suit-
able for Stinking Fish apostles a'nd Yankee Beef
Trust magnates. Personally, I consider its smell
warrants its use as a fertiliser. By the way, I
once knew an old lady near Thirlmere (N.S. W.),
who tried somebody’s blood and bone manure on
her strawberry beds. The strawberries survived
it, but the family very nearly didn’t, and report
says that even now, after many years, the perfume
of that establishment is such that the fragrance
has to be deducted from the rent. And the dead-
horse creeper beats that cottage hollow.

Th’ wife, she won’t

t’ ’ell! ” said Hos-

Millington. “If
yeh won’ come wi’
me, I’ll drownd
meself in the troft.
Goo’-bye, George.’
He shook hands
with Hoskins, and
the tears streamed down his face. “Goo -
bye Mick. Goo’-bye, Charlie.” He shook hands
with them all. At the door he paused, and
turned a fat, tearful face. “Don’ take th body
’ome t’ th’ wife, boys,” he said, “she don love
me.” Then he passed out. Nobody seemed the
least distressed. They all knew Millington, and
they knew that Scougil’s trough was cracked and
had no water in it for a year. Millington had
“ drownded ” himself in it a score of times. Half
a minute later there was a wild, bovine roar out-
side, and then Millington dashed back into the
bar streaming with water, and made a murderous
rush at Scougil. “ You German swine, 111 tear
yer ears off ! ” he yelled. Scougil had mended
the trough.
“Bilya”: Be “ Falder ” (B. 20/12/’O6)
querying “Cocky’s” statement that sleeper-
hewers cut up to 18 sleepers per day. ihis
would, no doubt, constitute a record, for m the
iarrah country the best tally is about 14 sleepers,
cut in something like 12 hours. A fair average is
about eight in an eight-hours day-usual price
Is. 4Jd. But this question of high tallies leads to
a much more important one. In “Cocky s
tally, what was the proportion of marketable tim-
ber cut in comparison to the waste ? As a rule,
the hewer who is hacking great pieces out of the
landscape to put up a big tally is wasteful to the
last degree. Have known instances where not 40
per cent, of the log was turned out as the finished
sleeper. In the States where royalty is charged,
the measurement of the finished sleeper is taken,
so that the sacrifice of timber doesn t trouble the
cutter to any noticeable extent.
The divining rod is more or less under
State Government patronage now in Queensland.
Such success has followed the efforts of twig-
bearers on the Darling Downs that Minister for
Lands Bell engaged a diviner to prospect tor
moisture in the badly-watered, but extremely rich,
belts of country behind Rockhampton. At
several spots the twig gave way to agitation, and
bores are now being put down.

“ W.G.” : Saw some weeks ago a goanna
v. snake scrimmage. They writhed and twisted
about each other, the snake repeatedly biting the
go., which ultimately proved the victor, almost
tearing the snake’s head off. I caught the goanna
and kept it for some hours. It was none the
worse for the mill. My own opinion is that the
snake was not able to pierce the go’s, leather skin
with its fangs.

“ Mooch-about ” : Henry Lawson’s ladies
aren’t the only ones who go into a bathroom, wet
their towels, splash the water about, and come out
without wetting their toes. A medical acquaint-
ance told me, t’other day, that five out of 10 old
men in our benevolent asylums who’ve been com-
pelled to bathe once a week make a. great watery
noise in the bathrooms, wet their hair, and emerge
as dry as a western plain.
* * * *
About the most ludicrous thing that has
happened to the heathen Chinee in N.S.W.
occurred at a way back vegetable garden lately.
The days being excessively hot, the vegetable
Johns had resorted for coolth to a deep cellar they
had excavated alongside their residence. Here
the bland Celestials would chant their folk songs,
and eat preserved ginger, and think out new
methods of passing decaying fruit on to wily house-
wives. They would also say ‘‘Hoo ki la,” and
Other words of similar import, and smile at each
other in a mysterious, dark fashion, whilst they
rested there, immune from the sun’s rays. But
this day a fire broke out in their residence just
after they had retired to their cellar, and it had
just finished up the last of their valuables when
they emerged from the cellar in the evening.
And the bland heathens then spat on the ashes
of their Joss, and forbore to smile in a dark way
for quite a long period.

“ Janor” : Here’s a wrinkle for “The
Prooshan” next time he cooks a satin bird (B.
27/12/’OG). By using the following mode he’ll
save a lot of time and consume the “ sweet bit ”
all the same. Select a round, smooth pebble of a
size to fill the cavity of the bird, and heat it until
incandescent. Open the bird with a straight cut
down the breast-bone, remove the innards, insert
stone, draw edges of opening together, and bend
neck over joint; wrap tightly in a piece of cloth
or sacking, and bury in a shallow hole in the
ground for one hour. It is then cooked ; the
feathers are easily removed, and you assimilate
the fowl done in its own juices. If one has an
appointment with his best girl what time the bird
is cooking it may be placed in his coat pocket
instead of in the earth. It will there cook while
lie’s hugging the appointee. The boiling of a
satin bird tender knocks a big hole in a week—to
treat a lyre bird a week does not suffice. Tried it
once when snowed-up out Mt. Pinnabar way.
Kept the beggar boiling for 96 hours, but, as he .
was getting harder every hour and was black as
the hob of the other place, shot him out. He
came in very useful later on to sharpen tools with.

“F.H.R.”: Had an old sheep-dog who
could beat Sherlock Holmes at deducing things.
He could tell when I was going to town by the
trousers I wore, and, deducing that he would
most probably be tied up and left at the camp, he
would straightway go into obscurity, refusing to
reveal himself even when tempted with a piece of
meat. I mention the meat just to show you how
fond even a dog is of a trip to town. I lent him
to my mate while I took a run to Sydney, He-
turned Wednesday morning, saw my mate, and
left the dog with him. Got to camp about sun-
down (25 miles from dog). Thursday morning,
when I opened the tent door, the dog poured in
so suddenly that he knocked me back on to the
bunk. Now, how did he know whether I had
gone on to the camp or back to Sydney ? Did he
overhear me borrowing five bob from my mate,
and deduce from that information that I was too
“ stiff ” for Sydney, or did he just go out on spec?
I reckon he deduced things from the borrowing
transaction. He was a dog of such intelligence
that he was capable of borrowing five bob from
another dog himself.
“ Elmore ” : Every dog has his day, does
he ? Well, my dog Laddie—but here’s the yarn.
My three dogs and I were out hunting. Sud-
denly Laddie, with a yard or two of brown snake
coiled round his neck, came towards me at a
terrific pace. I put on a terrific pace, too (not
towards him). When he caught up I grabbed a
lump of tree, and dropped it on the snake s head,
quite forgetting the dog’s head was just under-
neath. I hurried home, leaving him quite dead.
Jones was there, and said he was laying strych-
nine baits for foxes, and advised me to tie up my
dogs. I was asleep next morning before
when Jones yelled out, “ Anyone up ? ” “ I am,
I said. “Well, you’d better come and attend to
your dog. I seen him eat two of my baits.
Scrambling out in my shirkl almost dropped with
astonishment as he threw Laddie over the garden
fence. “Well,” I gasped. “Snake-bitten,
brains knocked out, and now strychnine.
I guessed a gun was the only remedy, and went
inside to get my rifle. I looked about for Laddie
when I came out. He was right among my bee-
hives, where he proceeded to take a fit. Me had
only done his preliminary, so to speak, when he
overturned a bee box. It was tbe quickest fit on
record. He gave a mighty leap and cleared a six-
feet high fence. I got under the bed till the bees
quietened down. An hour afterwards, Laddie on
the edge of a 20-ft. shaft prepared to resume his
interrupted fit. I fired, and with a yelp he dis-
appeared. Next morning when I opened the door
to get my beef, which was hanging under the
verandah, there was Laddie outside the gate (he
was also outside my meat). And he has been
looking well ever since. #
A Dutchman’s moralising over his dog
You vas only a dojr, bub I vish I vas you. Ven you go
mit your bed in, you shust turn round dree times and lay
down. Ven I go mit the bed in, I have to lock up de
blace, and vind up de clock, and put de cat out, and
ondress mineself, and mine frau vakes up and aoolde, den
de baby vakes up and cries, and I haf to walk him mit
the house round ; den maybe ven I gets myself to bed it
is time to get up again. Ven you get up you shust
stretch yourself a leedle and you vas up. I haf to light
de fire, and out on de kettle, scrap some nut mine wife
already and get myself breakfast. You blay around all
day and haf blenty of fun ; I haf to work all day and haf
blenty of drabble. Ven you die you vas dead. Ven 1
die I haf to go to hell yet.

“ Te Ngaehe ” : Great is the tohunga of the
Maori! You needn’t be an archangel of purity
to persuade the M.L. aboriginal that you are a
spiritual leader. One of the principal tohungas
in a certain district was an inveterate thief. He
stole his grandmother’s pig when nothing more
was available, then stoutly denied it. Months
afterwards he gave out that the spirits had told
him to confess, promising in return an endow-
ment of supernatural powers. Other natives
swallowed this yarn, and he has waxed a mighty
prophet and layer on of hands. Among Ins
followers were a Maori M.H.R.. and an M.L.C.

A Sydney artist has these 20 years been
reckoning on a reversionary interest in some valu-
able Kingston, Jamaica, property. And now the
earthquake has wiped out his expectations.
They say that Ashton, whose retirement
from the N.S.W. Ministry is announced, has
made quite a fortune in the land agency business
—not by dubious means, but by reason of the
enormous fees he was able to command.
C. R. Bell, who died recently at Casterton
(Vic.), was one of the oldest school-teachers in the
State. He carried on horseback, from Melbourne
to Ballarat, Gov. Hotham’s despatch to the
miners during the Eureka Stockade trouble.
A man from Kalgoorlie (W.A.) was trying
to tell some Melbourne friends where he had
spent the Sunday afternoon. “ I dropped in at a
big church, where there was a little man saying
‘I, I, I,’ when he wasn’t saying ‘ Me.’ ” There
was a chorus of “Judkins!” “ Well,” said the
visitor, “I fancy I did hear his name wasJud-
something.” Jud-something ! Such is fame !
Visiting sailor-man Frank Bullen, lunching
privately with Alfred Deakin, couldn’t perceive
that the affable Premier, when asked how he pro-
posed to keep out the Japs, courteously put his
questioner off with a non-committal, temporising
reply. Bullen should have known better than to
ask such a question seriously, and he must have
known better than to use the private conversation
as “copy.”
Miner Verran told theS.A. Assembly, the
other day, during a burst of eloquence concerning
the refusal of the Legislative Council to grant the
benefits of Wages Boards to miners, “The Council
Would wreck the devil, to say nothing of the State,
so long as they could Work their wicked will,”
Now, Jack Verran is a Methody local preacher,
and why should he object to the Council wreck-
ing the devil. Perhaps he looks upon the action as
a piece of unfilial conduct deserving of reprobation.
RETIRING POLICE SUPERINTENDENT
LARKINS (N.S.W.).
Photo, by L. W. Appleby, Sydney.
Rev. R. J. Campbell, of City Temple,
London, shakes the world by denying the Fall of
Adam.
They say that Adam did not fall,
And all the scandal we have heard
Is simply false and most absurd—
For Eve was married, after all!
That democratic parson, ‘ ‘ Brother Charles, ”
will probably forsake N.S.W. shortly. His term
with the Bush Brotherhood is up, and he has not
much encouragement to stay. The fashionables
of the Church of England in his district couldn’t
forgive him that he was not Anti-Sosh; they
expected him to preach Sosh on Sunday and
be fervently Anti-Sosh from Monday to Satur-
day. And he had not that convenient sort of
mind, and won much fashionable disfavor in
consequence.
