“ Blister ” writes :
Strange how every capitalist thinks he is a fit and
proper person to represent the electors whenever he feels
inclined to wallow in the cheap glory and advt. of M.P.
Up till a few days ago nobody ever knew that old Ben
Dowling, cattle salesman and investor, of Ballarat, took
the faintest interest in the big- game, but the other night
he spent a shilling in announcing that he intended to
enter the Legislative Assembly. Ben’s only claim to a
vote seems to be that he is “ a fine stamp of a man ”
what the Yankees call “a good looker.” It is alleged
that he has never been heard to orate outside of a cattle
yard, and his feeler has not yet touched any substantial
promises of support.
Melbourne Age seems to have become con-
verted to The Bulletin’s practice of taking away
that bauble. Anyway, it refers to the creator of
Melbourne’s St. George statue as “Mr.” J. E.
Boehm.
“Warrym”: Jarrah Jack Forrest has
decided to be High Commissioner. How do I
know ? He’s had a coat-of-arms painted on his
carriage panels. It includes “supporters”—two
blue lions. Those supporters signify that the
owner of the carriage is a peer of the realm.
There’s nothing to prevent Forrest painting
coronets on his carriage panels if he likes; but
how those who are It in England would laugh at
the coat-of-arms if sported by the High Commis-
sioner of Australia!
A recent “ In Memoriam ” notice in S. M.
Herald recalls the fact that the North Devons,
under Colonel Bloomfield, were the first soldiers
to occupy Paddington Barracks (Sydney). The
11th held the barracks for nine years, and five
veterans, aged from 79 to 90, still survive in the
neighborhood of Sydney. They have reason to
be proud of their period of soldiering. Regiments
that preceded them in Sydney were public
afflictions. But when the 11th left, each soldier
was presented by the citizens with a written
address, and the officers with a silver dinner ser-
vice for the mess. The 11th cleared away the
scrub on the site of what is now the Cricket
Ground.
Thomas Cordingley, who died while visiting
Sydney last week, was the first man to make
meat extract in Australia. He and Alban Gee
were imported for Sydney Meat Works about the
same time —Cordingley ns meat-extract chemist;
Gee as canning expert. Later on Cordingley
went North, established on Alligator Creek, near
Townsville, probably the best-designed works in
Australia, and ran them with great success till
drought thinned out the herds and sent up prices
beyond the point at which there was much profit
left in the potting business.
Adelaide has lost an old identity in
“ King,” the life-saving dog at the Glenelg baths
(he was good enough to be a “ Personal ” and not
an “Abo.”). A heavy, white, short-haired mastiff
crossbreed, he had great power, and a love of the
water, and was trained to rush from the keeper’s
quarters at the sound of a bell, and drag anybody
ashore that needed it. He was overfed by picnic
parties, got pig-fat, and the first time he was
given practice in rescue work he carried it out
faithfully—and died on the steps.
There is one very prominent Adelaide
citizen who must have mixed feelings regard-
ing the destruction by earthquake in Jamaica
of James Fergusson, ex-Governor of S.A.
Way back in the seventies he was a young
and wealthy squatter, about to be married.
Fergusson was at Government House, a soldierly
figure of 40, having just buried his first wife.
And it somehow came to pass that the Adelaide
girl broke off her engagement at the eleventh
hour and married the vice-regal widower. She
died of cholera, 10 years later, in India, and not
till then did the Adelaide lover look for consola-
tion elsewhere. The undaunted Fergusson made
yet a third matrimonial venture in due course.
Police Superintendent Creaghe, of Bathurst
(N.S.W.), who retires after 44 years’ service, is
one of the veterans of the early-day mounted
force, which consisted largely of broken-down
English “ toffs,” ne’er-do-well adventurers, &c.,
who were “soft tack” to the wide-awake bush-
ranger of the time. Creaghe reached Maoriland
in 1861, and had as ship-mates a family named
Scott, one of whom later achieved fame as “Cap-
tain Moonlight.” He arrived in Sydney in ’62,
joined the police, and was stationed at Albury.
He had charge of the northern and southern gold
escorts. He officered the party that shot Clarke’s
confederate, Pat O’Connel, had several “brushes”
with the gang, one of which lasted about six
hours, and made things so brisk generally that his
men were called the “ Bellabula bloodhounds.”

with yer foot !”
“ Bill, I cannot tell a lie. I've hurt it.”
Professor Rutherford, the new occupant
of the Physics chair at Manchester (Eng.) Univer-
sity, is a Maorilander, and a graduate of M.L.
University. He has been for some time Professor
of Physics at the McGill University, Montreal
(Canada), and has made his mark in the ionisation
of gases by the Rontgen and Becqueral rays.
Tom Molloy, who had only climbed as high
as senior-constable in the Vic. police when Death
served him with a warrant lately, had a jealous
regard for his own religious faith. When he was
in charge of the lock-up at Geelong, a drunk was
brought along. The usual particulars had to be
filled in. “ Pfwat’s yer religion ?” asked Molloy.
“Romansh Cather-hic,” said the drunk, and
added: “You’re Tom Molloy-hic, ain’t yer?”
But the drunk, expecting sympathy and a soft
plank, had miscalculated. Molloy eyed him sus-
piciously, then stepped up and commanded :
“ Then make the soign of the Cross.” “ Whash
that?” the drunk wanted to know. “I thought
so, yeblaygard,” said the zealous Thomas; and
in the “ Religion ” column, this entry was made :
“Declined to state.”
The appointment of Street as permanent
N.S.W. Probate and Bankruptcy Judge in place
of Justice Walker (retired through ill-health)
was the result of a series of fortunate circum-
stances. _ When he received the acting appoint-
ment his seniors were unavailable. Gordon,
K.C., was away a-marrying; Lin gen was in
Europe; Dr. Cullen, K.C., was suffering from over-
work ; and Adrian Knox, K.C., would not accept
the acting billet for a mere three months term.
Street stepped in, and proved a success. He
showed a remarkable capacity for dealing rapidly
and accurately—without haste, without rest—with
the cases; and cleared the decks of a whole lot of
Equity and Probate work which had been clogging
the lists for months, and thus putting the public
to great inconvenience. Though the new Judge’s
merits were known to a few, he had never been
in big practice. He reaches the Bench in one
long stride, like the outsider on a racecourse that
gets a lucky opening—the opening that happens
once in 10 years—and goes through. It should
be also a lucky opening for N.S.W., where the
Bench is getting weaker owing to the dead-weight
of time. Street is a young man—comparatively.
Most of the other Judges are old men—and are
showing it. Also, in some cases, their law is even
older than they are.
“ Melb.” writes :
There died in our Brunswick suburb the other day Eli
Summers, one of a quartette of clever brothers : Eli,
Charles, Albert, and Joseph. Charles was the only dis-
tinguished one, but the others are noticeable. In another
way altogether they may remind you a trifle of the
Bronte sisters, with Charlotte sticking out. They were
sons of a Somersetshire stonemason, and toiled in the
quarries, with aspirations like Hugh Miller. Charles,
Eli, and Albert wanted to be sculptors ; Joseph’s aspira-
tions were for music, and I may move him out of the way
first by stating that he pluckily got to Oxford somehow,
became Musical Bachelor there, came to Melbourne, and
was a leading musical professor. Charles was taken in
hand by one Weekes,, art instructor, of London ; was
taught modelling, and carried off, first, the Eoyal
Academy silver medal for sculpture, and then its gold
medal with a fine work called “ Mercy Interceding for
the Vanquished,” reproduced in the Illustrated London
News. Watson, a rather eminent sculptor, became ill
when he had nearly done statues of Lord Eldon and
Lord Stowell, at Oxford. He handed them over
to Summers, who finished them well. In 1853
Charles, Eli, and Albert came to Australia. The
object of the trio was to make enough money, on the
goldfields, to enable them to study in Rome. They went
to Tarnagulla (Victoria), and had the luck to spot the
afterwards famous Poverty Reef. When within 4ft. of
that great find Charles gave up, thinking he would
obtain a job on the Hotham monument in Melbourne.
Eli and Albert struck the gold, and became well off.
Charles married a French lady. Their son is the present
notable sculptor, Charles Summers, now in charge of
statuary at Christchurch Exhibition. The elder Charles,
the genius if ever there was one, had a bad time in Mel-
bourne, crowned, however, by the Burke and Wills
monument. In that he had the competition of one
Sydney Camroux, who wanted to represent the explorers
standing side by side, with Burke pointing to the ocean.
Wills, senior, eagerly supported this. Paltry jobs of
Summers yet in evidence in Melbourne are the Cupids
and garlands on the Bank of N.S.W. frieze, and a figure
of Albion over a Richmond drapery shop. He managed
to get away in 1866, and thenceforth worked in Rome.
James Fergusson, killed in the Jamaica
earthquake, at the age of 74, fought in the Crimean
war as a Grenadier Guards officer. He was
appointed Governor of S. Australia in 1868, and
of Maoriland in 1873. He returned to England
in ’75, and held office in Salisbury’s second
Government. Laborist Clynes ousted him from
his seat at last general elections. His second wife
was a daughter of Mr. John Richman, of S.A.
Recent newspaper mention of “ Rev.
Henry John Noble” recalls the rather belated
slipping of a round peg into a round hole. Noble
(not then “ Rev.”) was once fairly well-known as
N.S.W. Deputy-Registrar-General, and, later, as
a member of the Old Age Pensions Board. His
inclinations were strongly “churchy.” When he
retired on a pension, a few years ago, lie joined
the C.E. Ministry, and was appointed curate at
Bendigo (Vic.). Recently he returned to Sydney,
and now fills a Leichhardt pulpit.
J. F. Hogan has been telling the Cockneys
how Charles Gavan Duffy gave the names of Cob-
den and Carlyle to Victorian townships in the
long ago. Cobden is still a flourishing Western
District township, but Carlyle, an incipient settle-
ment on the Murray, has long ago followed its
namesake to the grave. Duffy told Carlyle
what he had done, and the dour old sage ex-
pressed the hope that the town would have a less
melancholy existence than that of its sponsor.
Died in Brunswick (Vic.), last week, Eli
Summers, one of the famous Summers brothers
whose lives have been so much bound up with the
pioneer and artistic work of Victoria. There were
four brothers, one of whom (Dr. Summers)
went in for music, while the others took up sculp-
ture. All four found their way to Australia to
get gold to go to Rome and study more sculpture
and music. They reached Tarnagulla and pegged
the famous Poverty Reef, which Eli and Albert
stuck to until they dug a fortune out of it.
Charles abandoned his share just before gold was
struck. He rushed off to Melbourne because a
statue of Gov. Hotham was talked about. In
Melbourne Charles did a lot of work. The
Burke and Wills monument is his, and the
four great royal figures in Melbourne Public
Library. He went to Rome, and Eli and Albert
followed him there ; but Eli returned to Aus-
tralia and settled at Newbridge, not far from
where ho made his fortune. On all the baby
rushes during the last five or ten years, Eli,
though nearly blind from a blasting accident
(his reason for deserting sculpture), used to peg
out a claim, just for the sake of old times.
Mackey, the Vic. Minister for Lands, on
the question of the Duke of Connaught’s pro-
posed visit to Australia :
It is a splendid idea. He is a man of the highest military
attainments. Apart altogether from the fact that he is a
royal prince, he is one of the most prominent military
officers of the empire.
In Mackey’s manner of not telling the truth, we
have a fair sample of the methods adopted by
official persons in Australia to reconcile their
professions of democratic faith with an unneces-
sary utterance of grovel. The Duke of Con-
naught, as a military officer, cannot be considered
apart from the accident of birth which has placed
him in the billet of Inspector-General of the
Army. He was born to the position as unques-
tionably as his eldest brother, Albert Edward,
was born the heir-apparent to the throne. His
very christening confirmed the fact. The old
Duke of Wellington impressed upon Queen
Victoria the advisability of keeping the nominal
command of the army in her family, and when
her third male child was born—the second son
having been earmarked for naval “distinction”—
the aged Wellington acted as godfather, and the
baby was named Arthur, after him. Also, the
infant was christened Patrick, with a view to
fitting him for an Irish title when he grew old
enough to be duked. There was no attempt to
disguise the fact that Prince Arthur was intended
to become a “most prominent military officer of
the empire.” His present billet was made for
him by the British Government, and it is only the
Mackey sort of courtier who pretends that the
royal personage has the fullest natural qualifica-
tions for his billet.

The new telephone call with the “ owe’s ”
and “double owe’s” instead of “noughts” can-
not be construed into “ I owe or I double owe
you,” nor into “owe nought,” which would
better suit the Federal policy, and, anyway, it
doesn’t matter how a man calls as long as he
always calls for the right brand—D.C.L. Whisky.
With a proper daily allowance of D.C.L. it is
never necessary to ring off great business or
political enterprises for want of intellectual in-
spiration and support.*

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, January 22, 1907.
My Dear Arini.—
Lady Sarah Wilson, a daughter of the late
Duke of Marlborough, and aunt of the present
one, has been staying at the Australia with
her husband, Major Gordon Wilson. They
left for Maoriland on Wednesday. A brother
(Captain Wilson) of the latter is of the
party. The husband of the Dukes daughter
is an Australian, a son of late Mr. Samuel Wilson,
who had extensive squattages in this sunburnt
land. The distinguished group has been up on
one of the stations for some weeks. Lady Sarah
has not lost the good figure with which she
fetched Melbourne some years ago, soon after her
marriage, when she was staying there in Lord
Hopetoun’s time, and she does look as if she had
developed and perfected the temper of her ter-
rible ancestress, Duchess Sarah, Queen Annes
friend. She has fine eyes, that have a
strike-you-dead expression. She and husband
were in the Boer War, and Lady Sarah was
taken prisoner, and later, in the exchange of
prisoners, swapped for an ordinary Boer woman.
Anyone who thinks that that’s not enough to spoil
the expression of the fine eyes of the daughter of
a Duke must be a glutton.
Sydney is holding its breath and waiting for
the Prime Minister to tell them that the Con-
naughts are coming here with Princess Patricia,
and several gentlerhtn whose uniforms would
leave a good auriferous deposit if put through a
crusher. If the Connaughts come at the same
time as Princess Aribert we’ll get critical and
bored. It will be too much joyness and Highness.
The agility of the Northcote’s staff in evading
queries about Princess Aribert’s visit would do
credit to an oiled lynx.
The Allen Taylors, now free of the padlock of
the mayoralty, go to Tasmania on Wednesday.
Sonny Forster, who has lately returned from
M.L., where he has been relieving at one of the
Bank of Australasia’s branches, is to be left here
to keep house. And just a wee while before the
Taylors took office Forster had crochet round his
pinnies ! Now lie’s trusted to keep house —to
say nothing of a branch bank’s till.
Andrew Black, with the voice like the rolling
deep, is waiting here for the Sing-Song on
Saturday. He waits in a camp at Middle Harbor,
taking in sunlight and food (his attack, crescendo,
on a pile of Irish stew, two octaves high, is a masterly
achievement), and being blood-sucked by mos-
quitoes at night. This Scot is an incorrigible
raconteur and mimic, and to hear his de Richter,
the famous conductor, is better than haggis.
Richter, conducting a rehearsal of the orchestra
in Manchester, says: “Laties and shentlemen,
give me notice, please. When you play the
violins pizzicato ” —holding up the fingers of one
hand and indicating the tips, not the nails—
“play mit der meat, not mit der horn.” And
Richter, telling in reply to someone’s question of
Will Madame Richter come to the matinee?—
“Nein. If my vife do not downright lie after
lunch she schwindle,” meant that the lady’s head
would swim.
According to the basso, we have something in
the way of melody worth living for—the playing
of the queerly-named band, “ Besses o’ the Barn.”
Not to know that the combination takes its name
from a village in Lancashire is of course truly
awful ignorance, but enlightenment will be full a\
few weeks hence. All the players are artisans,
their conductor a young Welshman, who is a
miracle waving a stick, and behind the combina-
tion is a hundred years of heredity in playing
band music.
'Miss Tittell Brune writes to say how greatly she
is interested in the good work of the Child Study
Association, and how deeply she desires to help
it. To materialise her desire, Miss Brune will
henceforth charge two-and-six for her autograph,
and give the money to the association. Every
week Miss Brune autographs dozens of books,
poht-eards and portraits, and if the demand for
the signature (at two-six) keeps up, the worthy
charity will have reason to be grateful to the
tragedienne. (The tragedy will be in the cessa-
tion of autograph requests when seekers find they
have to pay.)
A friend in Melbourne sends a guinea to the
Child Study Association charity. Mrs. Fischer,
of Cranbrook, Burwood, and Mrs. C. M. Regan,
of Tamworth, also help along the good work to
the extent of a guinea each.
The baby daughter of the divorced wife of the
King of Saxony (same monarch who was so
shocked by Calve’s calling the tenor “head of a
sheep,” or some such) is to be brought up
as a nun. So the old man has decreed.
And some of the German press is so dis-
tressed by the “ ultipomatum ” that it is falling
over its irregular verbs. The infant was born
after her mother had fled from her husband’s
drab court escorted by a tutor, and the press
incites the lady to put up a vigorous fight
against the girl being compelled to wear a black
robe and veil when her natural taste might run to
Paris frocks and frisks.
It would be interesting to know how those
twenty-seven Maoriland housewives are getting
on with the imported (English, Irish and Scotch)
serving damsels. Are the imported giving the
importers back answers, breaking the china, and
telling the missus (of the work) “ That’s not the
way we do it at Home ” ? Some time ago a syndi-
cate of fifteen missuses in Napier district got into
communication with the British Women’s Immi-
gration Association, and 27 accredited young
women arrived lately. The lady who took charge
of them coming out tells that she secured them
with great difficulty. They had to be coaxed out,
and adds that there is not much hope of the ex-
periment being repeated on a larger scale., The
demand for good women servants is pretty keen
where those girls came from, and the emigrating
working woman prefers America as not being so
far from Home and friends as Oceania.
A few visitors from New Caledonia are making
holiday in Sydney, and vary the aspect of the
passing show. Both men and women are heavy-
weights. The latter wear one long, loose outer
garment, an overall; it is buttoned down the
front from neck to hem. Some of the men have
enormous moustaches, and one eating in a restaur-
ant had his tucked behind his ears. In one of
Jokai’s novels a robber chief always wore
his moustache so, only to each point was fastened
a heavy gold coin. The bushranger of the past
had a sense of the picturesque.
Some of the correspondence from London about
Australians abroad makes one feel prickly. The
writers have stock phrases that they intend to be
complimentary. “ Ada Crossley still keeps her
slender, graceful figure.” Pity they didn’t add,
“in spite of her age and infirmities.” Why, it’s
only a few years ago that Ada acquired that
graceful slenderness —and it isn’t likely that she’s
going to part with it before she’s quite in her
prime. When she left Australia for study she
was a thick-figured young party, and she stayed
thick for many years, until she practised the
right sort of physical culture. Another “ compli-
mentary ” phrase is, ‘ ‘ Mrs. Flood is looking
younger than ever.” “Ever,” since when? In spite
of the ingenue hat with the wreath of pink roses,
the Widow Flood looked, when she left here, an
honest sixty-five. Does she look the blushing bride
age now ? She is to have a smart, big wedding,
I hear—bridesmaids, a crowd, flowers, breakfast
—and the man-of-war ’groom will no doubt cut
the cake with his sword. In John S. Winter’s
military novels the officer always cuts the cake
with his shining blade, so, of course, it is the pro-
per thing to do. Haw !
A returned Australian tells me that one of the
little side shows to which she and her sisters
treated themselves, when on a fly round in
London, was secured in this wise: They would
see in the society columns of the press that the
Duchess of (say) Londonderry’s reception would
come off on a certain evening. On that
night the girls would have a late supper;
then, in evening dress, at about 12.30,
they would go round to the Londonderry
mansion, stand near the edge of the red carpet
and watch the Somebodies come out. The
watchers always knew who was who through the
policeman bellowing the name. “The Chinese
ambassador’s carriage blocks the way.” “ The
Countess of Warwick’s carriage blocks the way.”
A few deadbeats or late loiterers might be also
gazing, but from a greater distance. Whenever
the evening dress group arrived and stood with
their toes close to the carpet, the policeman
always remonstrated with “ You can’t stand here,
ladies—move on, please.” But he never failed to
wilt under their entreaty—“ O, don’t ask us to
go ; we’ve come all the way from Australia.” It
staggered him and flattered him, for he seemed
convinced that they had come all the way from
Australia for this—to see a peeress or an ambassa-
dor get into the carriage that blocked the way.
Mr. Bischoff van Ryck, whose black horses and
park phaeton have been known to Sydney for half
a year, goes hence for Asia, by the French liner
Nera, on Monday.
I have seen “Six Bachelors,” the gentleman
who has been sobbing into the Daily Telegraph
about his inability to get a suitable wife. There’s
only one of him, although he calls himself five
more—and he is about as anxious to call some
girl his Very Own as a cat is to do its own fishing.
M.L. Premier J. G. Ward, his wife and
daughter, will get to Sydney on the thirtieth of
this month en route by the Mongolia to the con-
ference in London. The enigmatic Wilson, who
used to be private sec. for J.G.W., is now
secretary to the Arbitration Court in M.L., so he
is out of this trip.
A rumor is flying about like a noonday devil
with wings, and saying that a mature couple who
got married a few months ago are about to sepa-
rate. They are not compatible, they explain, and
a long interval of single cussedness between their
first matrimonial ventures has unfitted their hearts
to beat as one. Both of them want the liver wing
of the chicken and the soft side of the bed, and
there is no peace.
Latest explanation for shoplifting is—influenza.
In a fashionable London case, the other day, the
judge accepted this explanation and suspended
sentence on “a widow of independent means,”
discovered prigging from a shop, on the plea, set
up by her lawyers and confirmed by medical
evidence, that she was suffering from the after-
effects of influenza, causing a mental unsettlement
which made her confused about meum and tuum.
It is, perhaps, just as well for the social organism
that only the rich can afford to have the benefit of
such astonishing medical testimony; otherwise
gaols would be empty and honest folk uneasy.
M.L.’s fatted cheque for the Irish Envoys is
£5500. Now, isn’t that past all whooping !

Premier Carruthers’ private sec. has shaved off
his moustache preparatory to getting married.
Most men shear the moss off their upper lip
because She told them to do it. Yet in that
awful Kipling story, “The Gadsbys,” the Girl
says that a kiss from a man without a moustache
is like an egg without salt.
Beauty-culture is a better-paving line than
hoeing turnips. A law case, dragging through
the courts in London, hinged on a Mrs. Pomeroy
—her name has long been associated with skin
foods and such like —who turned herself into a
limited liability co. and became Mrs. Pomeroy,
Limited. She was the co.’s managing director,
and had a salary of £l2OO a year.
From London: —
Major Frank Maxwell, who is to wed Charlotte Osborne
(of the P. H. Osborne clan), at St. Paul’s, Knightsbridge,
on the 22nd, is a soldier with a record that includes the
Y.O. twice over. The Osbornes, now that the chicks are
leaving the paternal nest, find the mansion in Lowndes-
square too large for them, and have taken a smaller but
equally smart house in Wilton-place, where the wedding
reception will be held.
At the Austral Club “ Tuesday,” last week, Miss Doro-
thy Menpes, baby-faced, golden-haired, and frocked in
mauve cloth, was a dainty figure. She writes the letter-
press for her father’s (Mortimer Menpes’) sketches, and is
just now considering the offer of an enterprising Ameri-
can agent who would like to engage her as a lecturer on
Japan. Her years match'her looks in reality, and she will
be quite one of the youngest platform speakers if she
accepts.
Rifle-shooting is by way of being put amongst
the fashionable fixtures for women. Invitations
to receptions may have on the corner Shooting,
instead of music or dancing. “Come to lunch,
dear, and bring your rifle,” will be heard through
the telephone. And dressmakers will have to
study the making of the rifle coat just as they had
to invent the Bridge Blouse. On Thursday night
the new civic chieftainess, Mrs. Thomas Hughes,
will fire the first shot of a Sydney Ladies R™ e
Club. There will be a large audience, and 1 una,
after consulting the latest work on etiquette, that
it will not be the thing to crawl under the seats
when the lady raises the rifle to her shoulder.
Conceal your fear, and reflect that probably the
rifle will be loaded with blank cartridge, and the
bull’s-eye bell will be rung from behind the
scene.
All the elements for a first-class Bland Holter
are in the quarrel between President Ro° s ® v ®)®
and Mr. Storer, who has just been tumbled off his
pinnacle as U.S. Ambassador m Vienna.
Dramatis Personae: The President, the Am-
bassador, the Ambassador’s wife, an Archbishop,
a Roman Cardinal. Synopsis of the drama:
Mrs. Storer (who is known in Europe as the
American Ambassadress) puts a long, pointed and
well-manicured finger into every delicate dip-
lomatic affair that comes within her husbands
radius. Also she corresponds with the Presi-
dent, who addresses her as “Dear Maria,” and she
and her husband write to him as “Dear Theodore.
One of Mrs. Storer’s most cherished wishes is
the getting of a Cardinal’s hat for Archbishop
Ireland. In ’99 she began to make active sorties
after the hat, and asked “Dear Theodore, who
was then vice-president, to send her a telegram,
which she could show (at Rome) as a proof of the
high appreciation in which the Archbishop was
held. Several private letters were written by
Theodore in warm praise of the Archbishop, and,
though the writer forbade the lady to publish
them, he told her she could show them to her
dearest enemies and worst friends —under promise
of secrecy, of course. Secrecy was so well main-
tained that a copy of one of the letters went into
the possession of Cardinal Rampolla.
Theodore wrote ever so many letters entreating
Maria to recover that copy. She failed, and
the seventy-seventh scene jn the drama shows
Storer, prodded by his wife’s costly lace fan,
asking the Pope for that Hat. At this stage the
newspapers got hold of Maria’s desire for a Red
Hat —not for herself —so Roosevelt, instead of
continuing to call the lady “diplomatic,’ de-
nounces her as “intriguing.” The denouement
hurries. Letters of rebuke from the President to
Storer and his wife follow, the _ Ambassador is
hauled back from Vienna, and is_ now sending
Open Letters to the Cabinet at White House.
Think of the scenic effects, the Court robes, the
“interiors” of Austrian, Roman and American
palaces —and Walter Baker as Cardinal Rampolla,
and Bland Holt as an Open Letter ! Why, it’s a
cinch —whatever that may be.

My correspondent at Brisbane tells me these
things:—
How’s this for tact? His Ex. was at the Mayor’s
banquet, in Toowoomba, last week, but left early “ in
order not to interfere with the political discussion,
which I feel sure was the raison d’etre of your coming
together.” He was quite right, too —it was just an
Opposition rally.
Great are the preparations for the. Chelmsford F6te ;
and rumors of much new and original “ doings ” are
being vigorously whispered around. The doll-stall
already has 160 inhabitants; and a story competition
(with a fat money prize), a temple of mystery, and a
children’s play are coming to the boil.
Touching children’s plays, a sad tale is filtering out in
connection with the Girls’ Grammar School performance
last year, and it is to be hoped it has lost its truth in the
process. It seems, according to the yarn in circulation,
that when a “repeat” was proposed—this time in the
Opera House—Govt. House presence was promised on
condition that one item—a dance—was left out of the bill.
As Govt. House presence is a thing no self-respecting
Grammar School shivoo would be without, the pretty
posturing was promptly banished, and a Vice-regal party
shed its lustre on an expurgated programme.
Fact is, Govt. House is nuts on children’s parties:
but as soon as the chick has crossed into that misty
border-land which begins at the 12th birthday, and con-
tinues until she is ready to fluff forth in chiffon and
snowdrops, she is excepted to be kept under lock and key.
Especially her limbs.
To put it nicely, one more Brisbane bud has been
plucked to grace a station homestead. Miss Hilda Hood,
daughter of Mr. W. W. Hood, who occupied the old home
of Tom M'llw’raith, Auchenflower, is the party of the
first part, and the party of the second part is a Scotsman
named G. E. Mitchell, the seasoned owner of much
Warrego cattle. The bride elect is one of the few women
who can ride astride with grace.
Died on Monday last, at Eilden Hill, Barbara Drury,
relect of the late Col. E. K. Drury, for many a year
manager of the Q.N. Bank. The Drurys’ house was one
of the most hospitable in Brisbane, but after the Q.N.
smash and her husband’s mysterious death, Mrs. Drury
lived quietly with her daughters. She was far from well,
and her peaceful death comes as a rather happy release.
Her father, J. Grahame, was a well-known merchant of
Sydney in the old days.
Though Brisbane is pooh-poohing it, New Farm, a
comparatively clean suburb, has a bad outbreak of
plague : 14 cases one clay and seven the next were taken
off to Colmslie. Almost all children—probably the
result of a rat raid. Health Commissioner Ham says the
thing had better be published, so as to scare the suburban
dweller into a general clean-up ; but the property-owning
crowd is trying its best to hush things up.
The Bright-Foxton wedding came off on Thursday. The
bride was in white chiffon taffeta, with wreath and veil.
Speeches followed the knot-tying in the drawing-room,
after which Mr. and Mrs. Bright went to the Tweed
(N.S.W.) for a week before proceeding to Tasmania. The
bride’s travelling gown was of green tweed. The presents
included a set of silver letter-carriers and a cake-dish
from the P.O. clerks.

Governor-General Northcote, at the open-
ing of the Australian Exhibition in Melbourne,
treated his hearers to some straight Australian :
He had to congratulate not only the members of the
A.N.A., but the whole Commonwealth, upon the evi-
dences of abounding prosperity with which last year had
favored the country. Certainly the main factor that
conduced to Australia’s prosperity last year was the ex-
cellent season with which, under the blessing of Provi-
dence, the country was favored, but they m st also con-
sider that every year saw the growth of what he might
term national assets, which were independent of season.
Every year our agriculturists learned more and more as
to the qualities of those fertilizing agents which tended
to increase the products of the soil, and year by year the
march of Science showed increasingly the wealth which
could be obtained from the by-products or tailings of the
great mineral industries. ... He hoped members would
never lose sight of the duty incumbent upon them to
keep public opinion constantly attracted to such
objects as water and forest conservation, as the pro-
motion of agricultural and commercial education, and
many other public questions of a non-party character,
upon which they could bring weight and important in-
fluence to bear. No time could be more propitious than
the present for Australians to use every legitimate
influence to advertise their country to the world.
A short time ago the present Prime Minister announced
as one of the cardinal features of his policy “ Australia
for the Australians.” To his mind the natural
corollary of that sentiment was “ Australians for
Australia.” In visiting Australian factories he had
seen engines and machinery imported from Great
Britain, the United States, or the Continent, and had
sometimes asked if these machines could not be made
here. The answer was invariably, “ Yes, but there
is not sufficient demand to warrant us in putting
down an expensive plant.” When Australia had quad-
rupled its present population, such a reply would not be
given to a future Governor-General. If the country kept
before it the example which had made the United States
great, and which was developing such countries as Canada
and the Argentine, and when it had a native Australian
population, in whose strong right hands the defence of
the country might be safely entrusted, its people
would add to the legitimate weight which they
ought to have in the councils of the Empire—an influence
which Australia’s geographical position and energies en-
titled her to. Then they would speak with a voice of far
greater weight than at present in the councils of that
great Empire, of which Australia was proud to be a com-
ponent part.

“ Janor ” : The very large percentage of
informal votes cast at Federal elections stresses
the need for an improved way of voting. The
cross is not a happy idea by any means. It is
popularly regarded as a “bad mark,” and is
associated with disapproval rather than of appro-
bation in most minds. What’s wrong with a
ballot paper carrying the names of candidates
encircled with perforations and so arranged that
the voter could remove those of candidates he
wasn’t going for ?

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne:, January 22, 1907.
Mi Dkar Yarrie,—
Miss Stuart-Wortley wore, at her marriage
with Captain Cecil, a unique veil lent by
Lady Talbot. The bride was the only soft-eyed
charmer we’ve had at Stonington on a visit.
Mostly the occasional guest at the Malvern man-
sion has been severely Puritan, with a rigid spinal
column and a grave countenance. Miss Wortley s
new mate is the Aide who grew fat and lazy in
the service of Governor Brassey what time that
Ex. was falling off various things.
Alan stands in wonderment round the tobacco
stalls of the A.N.A. show. He is amazed at the
rapid play of female fingers in the process of cigar
and cigarette rolling. To the he-spectator it is as
fascinating as the minuettings of a canary to a
cat. The dexterous play of fingers is interesting.
It never errs, never pauses, never slackens speed.
After being glued to the spot for quite ten
minutes, I asked the “hands” how they were
enjoying themselves. One pallid wench passed me
a laconic stare, looked along the line of males,
expanded her features into a playful grin, and
said “ Orlright; this is play to-day. I asked
another freckled-faced, sharp-nosed youngster of
fourteen how many cigarettes she could roll in a
day. “ Four thousand odd ” was the reply. All
work right on while providing tags of informa-
tion. The fingers flash without a pause. The
work is so mechanical that the eyes can roam any-
where. 1 didn’t notice any of the “ Carmen
type among these Australian tobacco girls.
The White Workers at the Exhibition attract
the second sex. The machines hum in different
keys, but monotonously. The deftness of the
pedallers sets the mind whirling cyclone-fashion.
Every part of the machinist is a-quiver with the
rapid motion of the feet. Collars are being
stitched. The workers are invariably 14 or 40.
The intermediates must be married. Their
Song of a Shirt is ended or suspended till the
“old man” dies, or is invalided, and woman comes
back to the machine. The fourteen-yearer has
her frizzed locks hanging loose round her ears.
She doesn’t bend to the machine. If she did, her
mane would of a certainty be gathered into the
lightning run of stitches. Her machine goes with
a speed that stings the senses. Her eyes the while
scan the crowd with automatic jerks. A corner
of the collar is turned without the help of the
eyes. The fingers act as eyes. They have
unerring sight. All day long they are turning
collar corners. “You get used to it,” said one
red-headed worker with the blasd air of an old,
tired fashionable. All wear ear-rings, or pearl
necklets and rings and other decorative odds and
e ids. But like the cigarette rollers, their faces
are expressionless—or deadly stoical. The con-
centration of mind controlling feet and fingers
locks up the face. Poor little souls ! Not being
toured philosophers, but merely girls with a
tteady job, kept off the edge of slavery by a
Factories Act, they get along happily enough.
The Queensland court makes the most impress-
ive effect. It is showy, big, picturesque. Cotton
in the raw is massed into tall pillars, beams,
balconies. The fruits tower up to a luscious
height. Pines on the point of yellowing bristle
aggressively from their plant-pots. The tobacco
leaf has a special show of its raw and dried and
finished states—its final cigar form. The tropical
profusion makes the pulse beat faster. The fruits,
the grasses, the palms —they set one dreaming of
a hot, languorous rich land, where the big sun
pierces all things and gives a glory of color to its
products. The efforts of all the other States to
look prosperous are eclipsed by tropical Banana-
land.
The eldest son of the late Bryan O’Loghlen
faces the altar shortly. She is a Mount Gambler
girl—Miss Blanche Beazley. Marriage is rare in
the O’Loghlen family. There’s a quiverful of
of whom has just disengaged herself in
the Big Smoke from a Londoner who didn’t prove
interesting or attractive enough. Amother Miss
entered the Loretto Order. The cloistered sister
was actually the beauty of the family.
A fetching mummer, whose spouse is quartered
in another section of the globe, found she had an
anniversary of her marriage to celebrate, a while
ago. She was puzzled whether to observe it, or
to mark the day with a dirge. Some Johnny pals
decided for her. They gathered in force and had
a wild corroboree. Several toasts were listed in
honor of the event, and no one seemed to see the
irony of a semi-detached charmer glorifying a
marriage anniversary with a shoal of other men.
The only toast that approached a cynical view of
the proceedings was “Many Happy Returns of
the Nuptial Day.”
The nautical girl hereabouts is rejoicing over
the Sayonara victory. It appears to her a good
thing for the Sydney craft to be licked, seeing
that the visiting boys make such a close hobby of
the game. Members of the Rahwiti crew were
lodged at Menzies’, and did things ashore in
style; nevertheless the nautical charmer barracked
for the local boy with heavy emphasis.
Colonel Stanley is being warmly welcomed
back to Melbourne. The girl hereabouts has yet
to learn if he polkas as well as of yore, or whether
Sydney has steamed dance-energy out of him. ,
J. C. Williamson signalised his youngest chick’s
arrival at her fourth January by a big kiddies
panto, treat. He got together all the children of
the local orphanage, appointed them front pews
in the gallery at a “Mother Goose' matinee, and
at the interval presented each youngster with a
large tin of candies. The performing lads of the
panto were similarly dealt with. It was the
greatest munching audience Her Majesty s has
produced for a length of years.
Two local widowers have entered a second
martyrdom. Both have contracted the bargain
for better or worse with dames wearing the
willow or the lilac of divorce and owning tat
incomes.
A great discussion arose recently as to the
selection of the bride-helps’ frocks. When a
number of fat and lean, short and long, dimpled
and bony girls are lassoed in for a wedding bevy,
the style of raiment to prevail is a big question.
Generally all submit to the proposal of the
smartest. Recently the helps struck and de-
murred at the selection of garb by a
chubby dazzler. She chose color and style
according to her own dairy complexion scheme
and snug little figure. But one tall girl, lacking
chic in her clothes and color in her cheeks, was
unhappy, dreading her appearance in a style
chosen by Rosy Dimples. She confided delicately
to the bride her fears that she’d look a fright.
The orange-blossom nymph told Pale 1 ace to
cheer up, that the ceremony wouldn t take long,
and the crowd wouldn’t really look at the maids.
“As long as you all make a nice, fussy group,
you’ll do.” So Pale Face had to prepare
merely to be a sort of nail in the frame of the
great picture.
A new engagement is already bumping along
badly because the male party, recently rescued
from appendioitis, refuses to take his fiancee out
in her hospital uniform. He reckons he looks
like a weakling in the company of a strenuous,
protective blue cloak and cap. Or, at least, like
the victim of a pillow-patter. Cap-and-Cloak
says, “ Nonsense,” and ties her cravat with deter*
mination under her saucy chin. I saw them the
other sultry noon, looking unhappy together. He
was quite miserable, and .she was piqued. Put
my sympathy is with the male. Fancy if each
She who captured a brute were constrained to
appear, ever after, with the net or fishing-line
with which the seizure was effected ! And Blue-
Cap-and-Cloak represents just that.

next week at Mrs. W. B. Cummmg’s dance—
Miss G. Keats.

A dame wanting a nice, amiable, agreeable,
snug, young travelling companion to take her
deck chair out of creases, and her appetite out m
the salt air, and her toupee out of its evening
crimpers, sought round in her suburb and circle of
friends for the girl of the right shape, size,
appetite and age. One was thrust upon her,
marked Perfection. The dame took her for a
trial trip to a sea-side resort. In the first jflace,
Perfection lost Madame Dives’ trunks. Then
Perfection lost her own head. She was nice.
The boys gathered at her smiles like the birds at
a shower of crumbs. The dame was deserted.
Her temper got off the chain. Perfection failed
to tie it up again. The week spelt disaster to
the scheme. At present an elderly lady is search-
ing for some girl buck-toothed, freckled, un-

TRYPHENA.
Old Townsend MacDermott, father of the
Victorian Bar, did not remain long to wallow in
the £1 a week granted him by the Bent Ministry.
He died last Monday at Ballarat aged 89. Com-
pared with the centenarians and oldest inhabi-
tants whose records are so off-liandedly quoted m
the press, MacDermott was not a very old man,
but there is a huge difference between the real
indubitable ancient whom everybody knows, and
the telegram stating that John Brown has died
at Brannigan’s Hole, aged 104, and distinctly
remembered the battle of Waterloo in his last
hours; so people regarded the deceased lawyer
as very, very ancient indeed. And if he hacl
lived six years more an astonished community
would probably have made pilgrimages to see
him and yet it would have gone on accepting, as
fuit’e a matter of course, the death at Branmgan s
[ole of Brown, aged 104. Townsend MacDermott
was 66 years at the Bar in Ireland, Victoria and
Maoriland. He dabbled a little in politics, being
Solicitor-General under Premier Kerferd for 12
months some 32 years ago. He left politics, or
rather, thev left him, in 1877; mining specs left
him badly in later years ; and his law practice
deserted him during the last half-decade of his
life. Altogether, the world wasn t too good to
Townsend MacDermott.
Subscriptions to The Lone Hand have
been showering in, by telegraph and post, smce
the publication of last week’s Bulletin, and, to
allow of a fair deal to all Australians and Maori-
landers, the opportunity of securing a place on
the “Founders’” list—entitling to a free copy for
life —is still kept open. Subscriptions—posted,
telegraphed (though there is no need to telegraph)
or delivered at The Bulletin office-by Janu-
ary 31st will range equally for the Founders
subscriptions. They will _ be divided into
batches representing (a) Sydney ■ ; (b) the rest of
N S. Wales ; (c) Melbourne ; (d) the rest of V ic-
toria; (e) Queensland; (f) Maoriland ; (g) S.
Australia; (h) W. Australia;, ft) Tasmania;
and the first happening to be picked out will be
the Founders. Many of the most distinguished
names in the Commonwealth are included already
in the subscribers’lists. ,
“AF.”: Sydney may well bewail the
fact as it does, that its two daily newspapers are
both Conservative. But the question may well be
raised whether Melbourne is much better off in
that respect ? Deakin being the only leader who
could possibly carry out the Age policy, it was
obviously the duty of the Age to support Deakin
to the utmost. Instead of doing so the paper
showed a disposition, whenever it got a chance,
to prefer some other candidate to a Deakinite,
providing they were both Protectionists. The
Age is clearly responsible for the election
of Irvine, whose sympathies are all with
G. Reid, whereas the other candidate would have
been a reliable supporter of Deakin. For weeks
after the campaign commenced the Age took up
the ground that as Irvine and Nichols were both
Protectionists, it was a matter of indifference to
the cause which one was elected, and the choice
could safely be left to the individual preferences
of the electors. A few' days before the polling
the Age determined to oppose Irvine on the
ground that his utterances on Protection were not
sufficiently definite. The mischief, however, was
done. In connection with the Age the criticism
of the late Francis Adams may have more founda-
tion than is generally .supposed. The Age, said he,
is constantly holding in the dog of democracy
while pretending that it encourages his efforts ;
but some day the dog will taste blood and go for
his prey in right earnest, and the .Age will then
try to appear as if it had been sooling the dog on
all the time.

Barraba (N.S.W.) Gazette unearths what
it calls another land scandal, the letting on im-
provement lease in 1902 of the Lindsay holding.
According to the Gazette, the area is suitable for
agricultural settlement.
Sydney Herald last week announced that
a serious effort was being made to get William
MacMillan to consent to be Premier of N.S.W.
But so far, N.S.W. doesn’t seem to have heard of
its own anxiety to have Calico William as its
Premier.
Mr. It. D. Meagher, the leading light of
the Sporting League, claims that he and other
members of the executive earn their livelihood
“as honestly, probably, as Mr. Carruthers him-
self.” The Bulletin has examined the statement
carefully, has looked at it sideways and endways,
and, after mature consideration, decides that it is
probably about true.
£6OOO has been voted for further tinker-
ing at N.S.W. Parliament House, and a much
larger sum will be spent in the future. The
£6OOO goes in a few repairs at the rear of the
building; the larger sum is to spent on remodelling
the Macquarie-street front. N.S.W. Parliament
House is a sink for loan money, and the fewer
members it accommodates, the more money seems
to be wanted.
There is another split in the camp of Bruce
Smith’s “ Hempiah ” League. The women’s
branch revolted some time ago. The present row
is about a proposed exchange of flags by school
children in Great Britain and Australia. Persons
named Edward Smitherst and Coleman Hyman,
and Delicious, or De Lissa, or something, make
kaleidoscopic appearances in this rag row.
HON. ALBERT HINCHCLIFFE, TRADES
HALL, BRISBANE (Q.).
A large-sized blunder has already been
discovered in Victoria’s new Licensing Act, and,
as might have been expected, it is the fault of the
Fat Landlords. They inserted an amendment
prohibiting restaurant-keepers who hold wine-
licenses from sending out for other liquid refresh-
ment that a customer may wish to take with his
meal, though a restaurant-keeper who holds no
wine-license may do so.
Forecasted that if the United States intro-
duce—as is proposed, the House of Heps, has
turned down the Bill by one vote only—a compre-
hensive system of shipping subsidies, to revive
the American mercantile marine, it will be as big
a blow to British shipping interests as were the
British shipping laws to Dutch sea supremacy;
and it is likely that the last vestiges of Cobdenism
in England will vanish with that new develop-
ment.

More Socialistic legislation. Last year,
the S. Australian Government set an expert to
certify the weight and quality of a dozen
shipments of wheat. Results: The buyers took
the Government guarantee without demur, and
merchants and growers got extra-prompt settle-
ments ; and markets which had been hitherto
closed to S. A. were open to produce bearing the
State brand. The experiment cost the State
nothing, and paid the growers and merchants.
Suggested now that Rose Bay, Sydney,
should be improved by, among other things, re-
moving the monstrous wart that the O’Sullivan
placed there and called Lyne Park. The im-
provement should be made, and then Rose Bay
will provide an object lesson in borrowing. First,
money was borrowed to build a wart on Rose Bay.
Next, the wart was used as a stone-breaking yard,
where men were paid with borrowed money to
break stones. It was mainly useless labor, for
machinery could have done the work at a fraction
of the cost. Now the wart will probably be re-
moved with borrowed money, and the puzzle will
be to find the permanent and reproductive asset.
The ruction created by certain ill-regulated
leather exporters at the action of the British
Customs authorities in stopping quantities of
Australian leather because it was over-loaded
with glucose, etc., is further testimony to the
efficacy and advantage of Federal legislation.
Under the Commerce Act a leather exporter must
either get an export certificate with the Com-
monwealth stamp, or he must affix to his
goods an exact trade description of the dressing
contents thereof. If the Commerce Act is
honestly administered it will presently put a stop
to a lot of trade practices that are chiefly
swindles; it will do a good turn to the honest
manufacturer and dealer; and it will get Aus-
tralia a first-class reputation for the genuine
character of its goods.
Thus Premier Price, of S.A., at a lamb-
exporters’ luncheon:—
I am one of those wicked Socialists, those fellows whom
the world fears, before whom people of business and
commercialists tremble. Yet I have had the adminis-
tration of this State in my hands for 18 months, and
everyone seems very comfortable. I remember when I
moved to extend the use of the Export Depdt to other
produce than wine, and so enabled it to be used for the
exportation of frozen lambs, I was looked upon as a wild,
irresponsible Socialist. But I am proud to remember
that to-day, when the export trade in frozen lambs is
such as it is.
The trade has increased in eleven years from 2000
carcases to a quarter of a million, and the ex-
porters of the frozen baa-lamb now invite the
wicked Socialist to lunch as a token of their
gratitude.
As if to point the spiteful folly of the Yic.
Fat Landlords last session in shelving a Factories
Amending Bill that extended Wages Boards to
shop assistants and drivers and carters, a cartage
contractor had the gall to defend a case in Mel-
bourne City Court last week in which he was sued
for a week’s wages in lieu of notice by a dismissed
employee. The lorry-driver in question swore
that his wages were 355. a week, less 6d. a week
for the groom, and that he averaged 73 hours a
week ! Fivepence three farthings an hour ! He
mostly started at 3.30 a.m., though once or twice
he had lined up at 12.30 a.m., and he was seldom
finished before 5 p.m. On the day of his dis-
missal he had started at 3.30 a.m., and got back
to the stables at 5 p.m. (defendant’s manager said
it was 4.45); then he was told to go to the
abattoirs, get a load of fat and take it to Colling-
wood, and return. That meant, the manager
admitted, an additional five hours’ work, without
extra pay. The driver refused, and was sacked.
One of the deputy-returning officers for a
Yic. electorate tells a story which gives a very
vague, uncertain sound to the political Voice of
the Country. Among the utterly uninformed
persons who applied to him for guidance on
election day was a raw Scot with hair in his ears.
In the first place, this free and unenlightened
voter wanted instructions as to which names he
should put a cross against. The officer told him
he could give no advice, but he would read the
names aloud. The Labor candidate for the di-
vision had a name beginning with Mac., so the
voter promptly seized on “Mac.” as the mon of
his choice. The list of names for the Senate had
to be read twice over to him before he could sepa-
rate the Scotch wheat from the chaff of either
nationalities. Then he proclaimed his intentions.
‘ ‘ Simon Fraser ” —weel, he’d gi’ a vote to Simon.
“M'Coll”—yes, he was a guid mon. Finally,
after a further pause, the sturdy patriot thought
he couldn’t go wrong in giving his third vote to
Skene. It was a Scawtch name, ye ken. So he
took his papers into the place of secrecy, and re-
corded a Labor vote for the House of Reps., and
three Tory votes for the Senate. His troubles
about the Fiscal Issue, Socialism, or Sectarianism !
The Caledonian ticket was good enough for him.
BUT WHAT GOULD YOU EXPECT?
If our friends had the same zeal as our enemies we

The Immigration League of Australia has
lately passed a sensible resolution :
That the policy of this League is to assist in settling on
the land the people already living in the country, and
confining any subsidies, assisted passages, etc., intended
to stimulate immigration, to British-born white citizens.
There is no objection whatever to white citizens
who are not British-born ; but the British-born
have first call on the limited assistance that can.
be given.
London Daily Mail’s special in Asia
shows to what extent Japanese insolence and the
white man’s complacency can go. Dr. Allen, the
American Minister at Seoul, offended the Japanese
by attempting to protect the Koreans against the
brutal tyranny ot their new masters. President,
Roosevelt has summarily sacked him “witb
rather less courtesy than one would show to a
disgraced lackey.” The British Minister at
Seoul, Mr. Brown, has also been forced out by
the Japs. The Daily Mail concludes :
Three years ago the trade of Korea was open to every
man. To-day the independent administrator of the
Customs has gone, his place being taken by a Japanese ;
concessions and contracts have been showered upon
Japanese speculators by Japanese officials; and foreign
employees have been reduced in number to make room
for Japanese agents.
The N.S.W. Local Government Act is a
big advance on the previous Acts, which were
based on legislation passed by N.S.W. in the
sixties, and copied from the old English Acts of
the thirties. In the 200 odd sections are con-
tained the broadest powers, capable of applica-
tion in the most conservative and in the most
socialistic municipality or shire. The smallest
civic body in N.S. VV. can at will acquire powers
superior to those of Glasgow or London, and can
acquire them in a handier way. The shire coun-
cils are already at work. The municipal councils,
which were elected on the old plural franchise,
have a year to prepare for the new order of things.
With the new franchise, a progressive municipal
party could be organised in N.S.W. by February,
1908, which would not be afraid to exercise these
powers.
Victoria has just discovered one of those
“permanent and reproductive works” that the
more or less recent pioneers gloried in. It is in
the shape of a stone weir, 20ft. high and
10ft. wide at the top,away up in the Gipps-
land hills where the Mitchell River rises.
Some fool Government spent £23,000 on the
job, and then disremembered all about it—
though probably Cohen recollected the
matter when he drew his interest. It had
just about vanished from memory when
Bairnsdale shire got a new engineer, who
saw it and got the idea that the flow of
water might be made to generate enough
electricity to light Bairnsdale town ; so a
little of that £23,000 may be recovered
after all. This country would be a lot
better off to-day if some of those old
pioneers hadn’t been so beastly fond oi
pioneering at posterity’s expense.
George Wills, head of the old Australian
firm of importers, left an estate of £718,000 for
the British Chancellor of the Exchequer to draw
Probate duty from. “He bequeathed a total
sum of £5OOO to charitable institutions in Ade-
laide a tithe of the amount that goes in Probate
duty to the country where he spent his income, or
most of his income. In death, as in life, “ ship-
per ” Wills was a converter of Australian money to
British uses. His business policy was to snort at
Australian manufactures, and the departed spirit
of that deplorable Australian merchant cries
“ Stinking Fish ! ”
A large shipping deputation has waited on
N.S.W. Premier Carruthers—the local shipping
cos., which were pushed in the front rank; the
British cos., which spoke little, and the foreign
ones, which maintained a discreetly subdued
demeanor, were in the rear. The deputation
clamored for a big reduction in pilotage rates at
Sydney and Newcastle, and Carruthers hastened
to say “Yes.” Now the purely local Austral-
asian cos. don’t pay any pilotage whatever.
Some of the British vessels do, many are exempt,
but 90 per cent, of the foreigners pay. So
the reduction will benefit foreign ships hugely,
British ships somewhat, Australasian ships not at
all.
An obscure humorist named Black, of
Billabong Creek, Parkes (N.S.W.), wrote last
week to the Sydney papers demanding, on behalf
of the Socialistic party, the “nationalisation of
light.” The humorous cuss based his joke on this
basis: —
If it is a fact (which, viewing the almost alarming
strides of astronomical science in the present day, there
is little reason to doubt), that the light from the sun and
moon occupies some thousands of years in travelling to
this earth, it is apparent that the light now in common
use must have been performing its pilgrimage prior to
the time of Julius Caesar. It seems to me, therefore,
that the people of the present period have no moral or
legal right to the light with which they have been making
so free—not paying one penny for the use thereof.
They don’t know everything at Billabong Creek.
The light from the moon reaches the earth in one-
and-a-half seconds, and from the sun in about
eight minutes. Black’s emergence from his
Billabong was a mistake.
An electoral law reform which will make
the Commonwealth and States ballot papers uni-
form is badly wanted. The average elector is a
slow-witted animal and easily flurried on election
day. When a cross on one paper elects a man
and on another rejects him informal voting is
bound to follow.

The economical kink in the popular mind
that suggests it’s a good thing to keep the inmates
of gaols, reformatories, orphanages, hospitals, &c.,
in idleness seems incurable. JLcoke (M.L.)
Orphanage set up a brick-making plant, the other
day, and made all the bricks for its own new
building. Then it went on the public market
with the bricks and competed successfully. But
objections were raised and the Government
stopped the competition.
A political fable :
A Maoriland owl, struck by the enormous pig-raising
possibilities of the island, devoted its moonlight nights
to the reiteration of the cry, “ More-pork ! more-pork!”
considering, with Lord Beaconsfield, that the main thing
in any agitation is to have a party cry, and continually
to give vent to it. After a year’s fruitless effort, he ex-
claimed, “ Any owl can give men a good idea, but even I
cannot endow them with the brains to understand it,”
and expired.
J. Ramsay McDonald, British M.P., has
been writing about us. He describes Australia as
“ a land of vastness, and of solitary and mournful
men,” and speaks of “a funereal solemnity
brooding over masses of scrub, which enters one’s
soul like a chilling mist.” Other visitors call us
a frivolous and pleasure-loving community, with
a gregarious instinct towards racecourses and
football matches and two-up schools. But then
it is impossible to please everybody.
The barracking of the Fairfax newspaper
people for the importation of Englishmen to fill
high Australian positions is an old fad, and the
family has paid away good Australian “splosh”
over it. When Justice Innes, on one occasion,
gave judgment against the Herald for libel, the
Echo, the Herald’s ill-bred young brother, let
go thus in a leading article: “We doubt very
much whether any British Judge would place
before a jury the issues in the manner in which
they were put forward by Mr. Justice Innes.
But, then, his Honor has not had the benefit of
an English training.” So it was always. The
Full Court considered the article a contempt, and
fined the family £250. So the Fairfaxes paid
handsomely for barracking for “English training.”

tality that its council has started a restaurant to
supply, gratis, meals and pertinent advice to
poor mothers for some weeks prior to and after
their confinement. This is a distinctly Socialistic
step, purely in the interests of the babies and the
State generally—but, so far, no Cold Country
anti-Soshers have raised any outcry about “dis-
rupting the marriage tie, or smashing up the
’appy ’ome.”
In Maoriland the administration of the
Department of Toil is once more like it was in the
days of W. P. Reeves. Millar has a good know-
ledge of the workings of most trades, and has
just announced that he is going to settle for him-
self if the scow is a safe animal in Maoriland
waters. It is long years since J.A.M. stood on
the bridge and shouted orders, but he is going to
don the oil-skins and navigate a scow from
Auckland to Bluff. He reckons at the end of
that trip he will be in a position to say whether
the scow ought to be let loose or not.
“Philo”: Be the proposal to plant a
colony of Jews in Australia. The Jew has shown
himself to be eminently successful on the land.
The Jewish colonies of Palestine are all flourishing
and the American Jews are leaving the Ghettos
and going on to the soil in large numbers, and
doing surprisingly well; Canada has two very
successful Jewish agricultural colonies. There
are many Jewish colonies in Argentine, and the
Russian Minister for Agriculture says that the
Jews are the most successful agriculturists in
Russia. [When ? Ed. B.]
