SPORTING
NOTIONS
Lion hunting is a rather unusual sport in Aus-
tralia, but a crowd of shooters at Bateman’s Bay had an
excitina outing Saturday last. A lion and lioness had
escaped from Eroni’s Oircus, but the male animal was
promptly seized by the circus hands before he got out of
the tent, and booted back into a cage with his tail
between his legs. The lioness, like the human female
animal, proved much more ferocious, and after mauling
horses in the tent and scaring the audience nearly to
death, got away. A party hunted her all night,
and twice fired without effect. The Erom party
found her next morning a mile or so out of the
town holding down a cow, and one of the Eronis
wounded her. She backed off and turned round about
200 yards away to fight it out. Two more shots plunked
into her, and she charged, the shooters dodging her
round a tree. But it was close, so close that she tore the
sleeve from one sportsman’s arm. Then she charged
Maloney, and Maloney, finding himself in a tight place,
aimed steadily and killed her. The Eronis have had bad
luck with their animals. A hyaena got loose on the pre-
vious Thursday night, but the circus men, armed with
clubs, surrounded it, and the lion-tamer grabbed the
brute by the back of the neck and hurled it into a cage.
That tamer is a man with whom it is an honor to drink.
* * * *
The atmosphere of the Six Days race at
Madison-square Garden, New York (U.S.A.), had a very
Roman flavor about it. The succession of sensational
smashes, and the tales of the tricks that were being re-
sorted to to “ out ” competitors in the interests of bet-
ting, drew a vast crowd of brutal and degenerate sensa-
tion-hunters. The huge building was crowded to excess,
and crowds were turned away for lack of room. More-
over, it was shockingly unventilated to prevent the riders
getting a chill, and the result was that they stood a very
good chance of being poisoned and suffocated. Hour
after hour, with haggard faces and bloodshot eyes, they
whirled round and round the monotonous track, seeing
nothing, hearing less, through an atmosphere where the
tobacco-smoke hung like a fog, breathing in the exhala-
tions of thousands of human bodies. The result was a
kind of stupor. Many of the spectators never left the
building from the time the race started till the finish,
and ate and slept in their seats. Ruffianism
and robbery were' rampant, and fast harridans
in private boxes drank champagne, whooped the
riders on, handing their favorites dollar bills as they
passed. When this amusement palled the dear creatures
fought and tore each other’s hair and clothes One of
the riders, Vanoni, the Italian, just missed a knife thrown
at him by some drunken hoodlum. On the fifth day the
smash the crowd had been waiting for came. Two riders
fell, and were spreadeagled over the track. A bunch of
eight following them blindly, madly, crashed full tilt
into the struggling mass, and the result from the stand-
point of the wild beasts looking on was satisfying.
Hips were fractured, ribs broken, and Vanoni’s nose was
smashed flat. Twenty-five yards of the wooden, saucer-
shaped track of battens were ripped up, and the race
was suspended till the battens were relaid, when the
weary grind resumed. So far, absence of news to the
contrary indicates that the Australian team, Pye and
Clark, were in the race at the finish. This affair doesn’t
seem to be sport in any way, but merely a degrading
exhibition of endurance, brutality and luck.
* * • *
Judging by his performances at Christchurch
Championships meeting George Sutherland, of Maori-
land, is still as mighty a sprinter as ever. And what a
wonder he has been ! His fearful rush at the finish dates
back to the days when races were held on the high
machines, when all the rest of the present-day flyers
were youths in knickerbockers. It was in the last Five
Miles Championship that Sutherland showed his sterling
qualities. Plunkett, Arnst, and Burton overtook him in
the last lap and entered the straight nearly three lengths
ahead. It was the sudden upheaval of the spectators,
roaring applause, that told them the Old Lion was on
them. They saw him race alongside for a moment, and
his head went down a shade lower, and he shot, like a
stone from a sling, clean away from them, winning by
two lengths clear, which he seemed to have gained in the
last ten yards.
* * • •
Saturday’s round of Sydney First Grade cricket
added to the. rank and dignity of Gordon District Club,
which is now leading the competition with 14 points,
which is no less than seven ahead of the second club,
Paddington. Paddington in its turn is succeeded by Glebe
and Central Cumberland with four points each, and close
behind Burwood and Waverley struggle along with three
each. There was some excitement over the Burwood-
Waverley match, at St. Luke’s Park, on Saturday. The
previous week Waverley had scratched up 254, and Bur-
wood had lost a couple of sticks for 65 runs. With four
wickets to fall only 43 were required to win, but the bats-
men failed miserably, notching only 32, and leaving
Waverley top dog by 11 runs. Central Cumberland’s win
over North Sydney was of the plain slogging variety.
When University closed its innings with 333 for the loss
of seven wickets it took chances. Sydney defied the col-
legiate bowling, and had scored 29S for the loss of seven
wickets also, when the sun went down and time ran out.
The afternoon yielded only one century, that of W.
Bardsley, of Glebe, who smacked up 111 against Balmain.
M. A. Noble reached 94, when Barnes, of Redfern, caught
him off one of Goddard’s. Some of the bowlers did well:
Matthews, of University, took three wickets for 17, Ridge,
of Middle Harbor, took four lots of Glebe’s timber for 49.
Macartney and Pitt, of Gordon, took five for 20 and five
for 37 respectively playing against Balmain, and Whit-
ting, of Balmain, retaliated by knocking down four of
Gordon’s for 37.
* * • *
Syd. Gregory—for whom a benefit cricket
match is proposed—had a long innings in N.S.W. cricket.
In 1888 Gregory first “ represented ’’ against Queensland ;
the next year against Victoria ; and in 1890 against South
Australia. Gregory had a place in six Australian XI.
teams to England (which is a record); and in “rep.”
cricket scored 1738 runs in 73 innings, an average of
25-94 runs per innings. Gregory saved more runs in the
field than he scored; and, although he was dubbed the
finest fieldsman in Australia, he was the smallest.
* • * *
The Victorian cricket team to play against
N. in the last match of the season will contain some
new blood not yet seen in Sydney. T. Horan (a son of
“ Tommy ” Horan—a once brilliant Vic. player), Vernon
(a bowler from whom great things are expected), Hazlitt
and Vaughan are strangers to inter-State cricket in
Sydney.
* * * *
Although Alfred Shaw had reached the respect-
able age of 64, the news of his death, following close on
the announcement of W. W. Read’s departure, is another
reminder that first-class cricketers in England are very
second-class “ lives ” from an insurance company’s point
of view. The name of Shaw is associated with Shrews-
bury, his partner in the management of two or three
visiting English teams for Australian tours. Shrewsbury’
died at 53, and of the men whom they managed many
failed to reach the prime of life. Briggs, Lohmann,
Barnes, Scotton, Ulyett, Maurice Read—all of them gone
long ago. Briggs and Scotton died insane. The cable
probably has recorded, or hasn’t recorded, the deaths of
other and less notable cricketers who visited Australia in
the 80’s. Those aforementioned, with the exception of
W. W. Read, were professionals, and it was the strain of
playing in two matches a week for five months of the
year that undermined their constitutions. When they
weren’t playing, in all sorts of weather, they were standing
about in the pavilion waiting for their innings or looking
at the rain. The toilers of the cricket field take many
risks, apart from the beers and whiskies, incidental to
their calling. It is a damp and biting-windy game, in-
volving occasional touches of sun-stroke, and bodily in-
juries for the players. No other class of professional
out-door athletes are so persistently engaged in wearing
themselves out. Australian “ crack ” cricketers have a
comparatively easy time, on the average, notwithstanding
their trips to England. And they don’t swallow nearly
so much beer, man for man, as their harder-working
rivals in the Cold Country. Beer, probably, has a lot to
do with the death-rate among English cricketers of note,
in which regard they are the “flanneled fools” that
Kipling called them. # ,
Cricketer Cotter asked N.S.W. Cricket Asso-
ciation for a bonus in return for his services against S.A.
and Victoria, on the grounds that, being out of
work, he could not claim for loss of time,
and he did not wish to ask for anything under
rule 19 and thus become a professional. The
chairman ruled a motion to grant the bonus out of
order. But it was stated authoritatively that it was
through playing for N.S.W. against S.A. and Victoria
that Cotter had lost his employment. If this can be
shown to be true, Cotter is certainly entitled to some
consideration.
* * * *
“The Captain ” : “Sunny Jim” Mackav, who
sailed away to Afric’s golden banket beds, will not find
himself in a bed of roses as far as cricket is concerned.
There are a few nasty snags there among the bowlers.
R. O. Schwarz, the Jewburg crack “ Bosanquet ” bowler,
is undoubtedly the best in Africa, and equal to, if not
better than, any I have seen in Australia. When
S’Africa sent a team to England in 1904, Schwarz headed
the first-class bowling averages of the English season,
being far ahead of all the cracks—Hirst, Rhodes, Lee and
others. Mackay will also have to face Kptze, the Cape
Colony man, who has the reputation of being the fastest
bowler in the world. Walter Niemeyer, Pretoria’s crack,
is also a good man, and there are others behind them.
Lots of sportsmen in Australia look on S’Africa as a
second or third-rate cricketing power—well, in the only
test match played during its English tour, the S’African
XI. defeated All England by an innings and 123 runs.
When Pelham Warner took his team out there last year,
he was soundly beaten in four tests of five played. Two
of these were lost, I think, by a whole innings. _ It was
the Australian visit in 1903 that put the S’Africans on
their metal, and they have improved out of all recogni-
tion since then. It would be an excellent idea if the
Australian Board of Control invited a Springbok team to
Australia; even N.S.W. would find the S’Africans worthy
opponents. * » *
N.S.W. Cricket Association, having discovered
that Sydney Cricket Ground Trustees had recently given
the use of the ground free of charge to another body,
suddenly bethought it of the fact that its overdraft now
amounts to £477 16s. 6d. So it decided to ask the Trus-
tees to remit the usual 15 per cent, ground charges for
the inter-State matches played this year. If it can get
this rebate, the amount will about square-up the over-
draft.
• • * •
The automobile reliability test from Parramatta
to Bathurst and back will start on January 25th, and
should be concluded by the evening of the 26th. Twenty
contestants have entered no less than 11 makes of cars :
Four Clement Talbots, three de Dions, two C. Humbers,
two Darracqs, two Innes make, two Minervas, and one
each of Vulcan, Argyle, Rover, and Reo cars. The dis-
tance is 223 miles, and the cars will start at 8 a.m.
* * * *
| (The race on Saturday for the combined cham-
pionship of Sydney Sailing Club and the Flying Squadron
was robbed of much of its interest, first, by the absence
of Australian and Christian Webb, who are on their way
westward; and, second, by the collision between Arawa
and Donnelly, which brought the latter’s sails tumbling
round the ears of the crew. The impact was due to the
carelessness of the Donnelly crew, and the referee imme-
diately hoisted the signal of disqualification. Later on
he hauled it down, and Donnelly started about 3min. late.
Arawa had to retire. Then Mona’s throat-halyards gave
way, and, her mainsail coming down, she dropped out.
Zena (skipper Firth) led home by 17sec., with Viking,
Vision and Scot in that order behind.
* * * *
There is something like ill-feeling over the La
Carabine Cup contests. The Port Melbourne yacht S.J.S.
not having been measured before the race, her rating was
only approximate. The first heat was won by Rawhiti,
with Heather second and S.J.S. third. The second heat
fell to S.J.S., with Heather again second and Rawhiti
third. This left all three yachts level, with four points
each. In the final, Heather sailed into first place, with
Rawhiti second and S.J.S. third. Then the club had
S.J.S. measured, and it was discovered that she was
entitled to more time allowance than she got. It made
no difference in the first and second heats, but did in
the final. With the extra allowance to which it
was entitled, S.J.S. would have dead-heated with
Heather, and the Royal Yacht Club ordered the
yachts to sail another final to dispose of the ownership
of the Cup. Mr. Stewart, of the Heather, refused to
re-sail unless the race was held at Sydney, and the
Rawhiti, which was out of it in the original final,
excluded. The Club refused to exclude Rawhiti, and
Stewart declined to sail. So the race was ordered to be
sailed off on Tuesday afternoon, when Rawhiti and S.J.S.
would be the only competitors.
Judging by her showing in the race for the
Sayonara Cup, it was the desperate attempt to reduce
rating by taking out her ballast that lost the Sayonara
yacht the La Carabine Cup. In the Sayonara contest the
yacht had been restored to her normal state, and in the
final, in spite of a bad start, during which the Sydney
yacht gained nearly a quarter of a mile, the Victorian
boat overhauled the challenger and won by 6m. 17s.
m •* 4 m
The Motor Boat Club of N.S.W. will hold its
reliability test on February 2nd, weather permitting.
Boats over 30ft. long and 6ft. 6in. beam will start from
Berry’s Bay, round Shark Island in Port Jackson,
through the Heads to South Reef, and round a boat
anchored 15 miles to windward, back to Manly buoy,
round Shark Island, then away up Parramatta River to
Ryde, and back to Berry’s Bay. Boats under that
measurement will start from Berry’s Bay to Ryde, thence
back to Shark Island, across to Manly Buoy, and away to
Ryde, and back to Berry’s Bay. The race is open to all
boats that like to compete, whether owners belong to
the club or not. It is to be a genuine test to discover
which is the most all-round reliable type of craft. Marks
will be given for relative speed, general condition of hull
after trial, security of arrangement of ignition installa-
tion, arrangement of fuel supply, economy of fuel on h.p.
basis, efficiency of reversing, ease of control in working
to marks, silence and smoothness of running, starting
motor, relative flexibility of motor speed with relation to
normal speed, and accessibility of installation. Each
i boat can carry as many passengers as the owner pleases,
' but must carry an official observer appointed by the
judge from gentlemen nominated by club members, each
of whom may nominate one. Contrary to general ex-
pectations, none of the speed boats has a beam of
6ft. 6in., and none therefore will be seen outside.
Among the boats whose progress will be watched with
keen interest is Papa Relph’s Van Auken, which the
owner is straining every nerve to have ready in time for
the fixture. This will be the smallest twin-screwed boat
in Australia. Mr. Relph’s Invincible will be there, and so
will Mr. Davies’ new Fairbanks racer, and motorboat men
are trying to persuade Mr. Don McKay to allow his boat
to be brought up from Port Hacking to take part, but as
Mr. Mac will be up country at the time it is doubtful if
he will trust his flyer to alien hands. All the same, he is
anxious on his return to meet Mr. Davies’ Fairbanks
boat in a match for £5O.
Only 14 entries were received for the Motor
Boat Club’s reliability test on February 2nd, which, con-
sidering the number of boats owned in Sydney, is remark-
ably small. It is more than doubtful whether any of
them will be called upon to run the outside course.
• * * *
N.S.W. Anniversary Regatta, coming Satur-
day, promises to be a huge affair. For sailing races 190
entries have been received, while 10 rowing races have
drawn 44 entries, and seven motor boats will compete in
two events.
• • * *
N.S.W. Rowing Association is considering
ways and means of encouraging country rowing. The
Hunter River, the Clarence River, and the Shoalhaven
ought to be haunts of amateur oarsmen, each supporting
its own clubs, and sending representatives to the big
regattas in Sydney. Now the Association has asked all
existing clubs to affiliate with it, and proposes to take
steps to form clubs where none already exists.
it * * *
The inter-State dinghy racing at Sydney on
January 30th, promises to be fairly representative, as
both South Australia and Queensland will be there.
This is the first time the wine and wheat State has sent
a boat to compete, and the innovation is due to the
energy of Mr. C. G. Tolley, who is bringing over his
champion sailer, Vigilant. There will be four Queensland
representatives. The absence of Victoria is to be re-
gretted.
• • * *
Rev. Dill Macky appeared in a new r6le on
Saturday. Hitherto no one has suspected him of
any sporting tastes ; but on Saturday last he figured as
owner of a boat named, of all names, Colleen Bawn, which
won £3 10s. in a general handicap at Manly regatta.
* * * *
In spite of a heavy sea rolling in through the
Heads on Saturday, the Manly regatta was an unqualified
success. The yacht race provided a splendid finish.
Kukuburra led the fleet away to Shark Island and hack
again to Manly, and won by a few feet from Fleetwing,
with Oooya only inches behind her. A decent-sized sail
would have covered the whole fleet. In the rowing
races, the Fire Brigade again wiped the floor of the
water with the Navals. The ladies double-sculls cham-
pionship brought out five crews, two of married ladies,
and the others of spinsters. The spinsters won. The
Misses Lewis, sisters Gertie and Kitty, representing
Middle Harbor, went to the front with a bound at the
start, and made the water fly at such a rate that they
were soon leading by two lengths. The Grainger girls
hung on to them, but never managed to draw abreast,
and then their strength gave out, and Mesdames Wool-
bridge and Hyde rushed into second place.
• ■» * *
Sporting men in Maoriland are deploring the
death of John Kirkwood, a famous old Auckland sport.
Far back in the days of the Maori wars he was in the
softgoods business, but finding there was more profit in
the wet goods, he took to the wine and spirit trade.
About 10 years ago he retired from that, a rich and genial
old man. He was one of the founders of the Auckland
Racing Club.
* •* * <
Despite the loud whoop with which Judkins
ushered in Bent’s Gambling Act, it doesn’t seem to be
effecting much. The Collingwood Tote has not by any
means suspended operations. The masked and mysteri-
ously-robed gentlemen who in the glad old days acted as
clerks are gone. “ Aunt Sally’s ’’skittle alleys are there
instead —and the telephone. With the help of these the
tote flourishes almost as of yore About a thousand men
visit the tote on a race afternoon, and shy balls at Aunt
Sally. All the most confirmed gamblers have taken a
sudden dislike to Sally, and can’t be happy unless they
are throwing things at her. At City Tattersall’s Club, of
which John Wren is only a member (he has said so him-
self, so it must be true), betting at starting price, or any
other price, or on the tote, can still be had as freely and
as ungrudgingly as of yore.
* * * •
Horse-racing on the modern system seems to
have originated with the Greeks, who started with chariot
races, and later on introduced the mounted contest. The
Romans copied it (as they did most other things) from
them, and introduced it into Britain. But, previous to
the coming of the Romans, the Britons not only seem to
have had horses, but to have raced them for prizes. They
had the craze pretty badly, and among the Germanic
tribes it was an absolute vice. When the Romans left
Britain horse-racing stayed on, and Jutes, Angles and
Saxons all took to it when they settled down on their
stolen property. St. Augustine was very much opposed
to the sport, and cursed it with bell, book and candle.
People who attended the meetings and put on their little
bit he excluded from attending church, and he laid a
heavy curse on the others who actually ran the n eddies.
The first Council of Arles, in its fourth canon, declared
that members of the Church who drove chariots at the
public games should be denied communion. But as time
went on horse races became a recognised feature at
church festivals. The Saxons, however, lost interest in
horse-racing—flat contests with the Danes took up all
their spare time, and also a lot of it they could not afford,
and the horse sank in public respect and affection till the
Normans came along. Edward 111. ran a stud, and charged
its upkeep to the country. The Tudors looked coldly on
the sport, but all the same, in the year 1512, the city of
Chester held public horse-races. When the Stuarts came
in, horse-racing revived. James the first held meetings
at Croydon and Enfield, and ran an Arab himself.
In 1609 Robert Ambyre, sheriff of the city of Chester,
devised the sweepstake, and set racing on a financial
basis. Professional trainers appeared, and a proper study
was made of the preparation of horses for the meetings,
Charles I. gave a gold cup for races held in Hyde Park,
London. Cromwell was mildly interested in the sport,
but regulated it with parental care, forbidding, it for
about eight months in the year. However, it still
flourished, and Epsom course-seems to have originated
during the Commonwealth. Charlie 11. was very horsey.
Round courses were introduced in 1666, and he gave
plates of precious metal as prizes, and after that most
races were either Cups or Plates. Charles 11. was the
first monarch who entered and ran horses in his own
name. Anne lost much money on races. In George ll.’s
time an Act was passed prohibiting the running of ponies
and weak horses, and competition for plates, cups, or
other trophies of less than £5O value. Ascot course
seems to have been laid down in 1727, for the recreation
of royalty at Windsor, and Goodwood came into existence
in 1802. These facts come in the main from the “ British
Encyclopaedia.”
* * * *
The visit of swimmer Billington to these parts
promises to shake up some of the State Governments to a
S’Australian sense of duty. Billington’s manager, Chas.
Bastard, runs the baths at Adelaide, with the assistance
of a Government subsidy for teaching local noviceshow to
swim. In the character of a public servant, he bore a
letter of recommendation from the S’Australian Premier
to brother Bent when he brought his “ star ” artist to
Melbourne last week. Bent, under the circumstances,
can’t refuse official patronage to the Billington show at
Melbourne City Baths. In giving it, he practically
admits that swimming is a uniquely valuable athletic
accomplishment, whilst he calls attention to the fact
that his Government does nothing towards spreading
the accomplishment. Vic. State school education should
begin with lessons in the easy art whereof Billington is a
boss exponent. And there are other States where this
gate-money show can claim the character of a missionary
enterprise. For that matter, the object of most mis-
sionary enterprises is boodle.
• • • •
Sydney Board of Health proposes to inquire
into the presence of swimming baths in close proximity
to sewers in Sydney harbor. And it isn’t too soon, either.
While it is about it, perhaps the Board will have a
look at the pontoons and structures which support hun-
dreds of screaming enthusiasts on “ carnival ” nights.
The collapse of a staging can be just as unhealthy as the
intrusion of typhoid germs.
The Deakin Government having promised
£lOOO towards the visit of an Australian rifle team to
Bisley, the riflemen looked upon the thing as settled.
But, with only a few weeks to go, the council of the Rifle
Association has suddenly found that nobody seemed
anxious to make up the sum of £355 which must be
raised in N.S.W. before Ma can be represented in the
team. It is still doubtful if Ma can find tne £355.

The cricket bat as a deadly weapon is some-
thing new. A Staffordshire (Eng.) man tried to murder
his wife with one last month. He had a knife (with
which he afterwards cut his own throat), but to settle
the lady he laid on the willow—as the sporting reporters
say.
* • * 0
A new generation of pugs seems to be rising up
in Melbourne. Last week a 9st. 21b. star appeared in
Hughie Meaghan, one of the “novices” discovered by
Jack Wren’s tournament. Meaghan w'as matched against
Ranji Moody, the smart little half-caste, and though
Ranji fought a clever, brainy battle for seven rounds,
Meaghan never gave him a chance, and belted him to the
floor for seven, eight, and nine seconds every round or
two. Moody got the very mother of a hiding, and after
knocking up both his hands in vain attempts to make
some impression on the other fellow, he just saved a
knock-out by chucking the fight in the seventh round,
when Meaghan was climbing all over him looking for a
mortal spot. Meaghan is a big, lumpy sort of a lad, with
a peculiar smother, and a determined way of putting his
whole 1281 b. of weight behind every hit. He makes
every punch a knock-out—it it gets on the right place.
He does not know much about the ring yet—is a novice
beside such a tactician as Moody—but that will come,
and then Meaghan will be a star of the first magnitude.
• • • •
“Suss”: John Wren goes to every fight in
Melbourne Cyclorama these days. So do I. John goes
to look at the fight. Igo to look at John. It costs me
ss. every time, but I get about 395. worth ; so it’s cheap.
John sits in the “ stalls,” right up against the ring side.
Round him ranges his court. Before the fight starts John
sits bolt upright like a prince of the blood honoring some
village festival. As soon as the boxers enter the ropes,
he becomes obviously interested. Each of the men
in turn leans over the ropes and “w’ords” him.
John is visibly proud that every pug makes obeis-
ance to him before going to battle. Bashi-
turi te salutant 1 As the men shake, John takes
his hat off and reveals his noble brow, wearing a look of
grim expectancy. The gong sounds. They’re off. So is
John. As the men spar (I don’t look at the men ; I see
it all in John), John wriggles violently, his eyes bulge,
his feet struggle, his hands clench, 'lhen a blow is
struck. John’s mouth —he has a prehensile mouth—takes
up a position in direct Echelon under his ear. One
eye closes. More blows are being delivered ; the Wren
face convulses ; his fingers claw the air; his tongue
appears through a hole that seems to have erupted up
between his chest-bone and ear. “ Break ! ” cries the
referee, and John’s face springs back to its normal cari-
cature of Judkins, just like an india-rubber face that has
been stretched in different directions. John fights the
mill from start to finish. Every nerve of him jumps ;he
is partly like St. Vitus’ dance, partly like a 3d. mechanical
toy. If I could catch John and tame him, I’d make a for-
tune showing him on a stage against a dark background
watching a fight that I’d arrange in the wings.

Stimulated thereto, no doubt, by the sudden
eclipse of Mike Wil iams, Frederic Buckland, lightweight
champion of S’Africa, is coming to Australia on the
chance of finding a grave for his reputation here. He
will arrive in Sydney early in February.
• • • •
Although Thorn, of Westralia, won the feather-
weight championship of the continent at Sydney on a
foul, there is but little doubt had the fight gone to its
natural conclusion, he would have won it all the same.
Frost, the Sydney representative, was worsted from the
very start. The Westralian made a fight of it, and the
Waler a boxing exhibition, and the result was that most
of the time the Sydney boy was on his back, trying to
discern the roof through the clouds of tobacco smoke.
It speaks well for his stamina that he managed to endure
the walloping Thorn gave him for nine dreadful rounds.
Apropos, it is time the people who run the Gaiety Club
were made to shift to some better hall. The Gaiety is
a dreadful place, and unfit for a dog fight.
« » * *
Followers of stoush looked forward to the scrap
between Cocker Tweedie and Clark at Sydney Gaiety on
Saturday night to provide some skilful slogging. They
were disappointed. Clark got home on the jaw with the
“ Jeffries lunge ” so successfully that Cocker was counted
out before the first round had properly begun.
* * * *
John Johnson, the Amurkan heavyweight who
is said by the papers of his own country to equal Peter
Jackson at his best, arrived in Sydney this week. Jeffries
and other top-notchers have drawn the color-line against
Johnson, and it has been printed that their objection
was not so much to his color as to his dirty right. _ He
comes under engagement to James Brennan, who, it is
understood, will open another boxing hall in Sydney
that will vie with the National Amphitheatre. It is
just possible that Johnson may be overrated—Amurkan
papers have been known occasionally to exaggerate ; one
described Louis Riel as a combination of Caesar and
Hannibal a few weeks before Louis went under to a mob
of Canadian militia, led by Garnet Wolseley, who was far
from being a military genius. Hopes are entertained
that Mr. Johnson and Mr. Squires will shake hands in
the ring before the latter leaves for Amurka.
• . * * •
“Skull”: A convincing demonstration of the
speed with which athletes develop when prosperity
shines on their particular game, is gives by the sudden
rise of new boxers in Melbourne. On two successive
Mondays, at the Olympic Club’s Cyclorama, reputable
experts have been whipped by novices. It’s true that in
one case the referee could not see the plain fact; but,. in
the second instance, young Meaghan gave no opening
for doubt by administering a handsome licking to cham-
pion Ranji Moody in seven rounds. Ranji is a half-
colored pug who came from Sydney with the ban-
tam championship, a few months back, and Meaghan
is the winner of one of Wren’s novice tournaments. Up
to meeting Moody he had only fought tenth-raters. His
improvement has been rapid, and he fought the cham-
pion like a finished craftsman, hisstopping being excel-
lent. There was always a glove or an elbow there when
Ranji landed a fat punch. Meaghan has a good shooting
left, and follows very quickly with a strong right. Moody
showed the effects of much punishment,,and although he
claimed to have broken his hand in the 7th, he was
beaten all along the line, and never for a moment looked
like mastering the new arrival. J. Stokesbury refereed
the fight to please the boys, and got a rousing reception.
It is hoped by patrons that the club will make up its
difference with Joe, as he is the most satisfactory referee
it has had.

‘ ‘ Philo ” : Be The Bulletin (3/l/’O7),
the disabilities to which the Jewish children
were subjected in Montreal (Canada) were fully
abrogated by a Bill passed in 1903. Here are the
full facts of the matter: By the Provincial
Education Act, Protestant and Catholic school
commissioners maintain separate schools, and
previous to 1903, the Jews were given the option
of contributing their taxes to either the Protestant
or Catholic panel. Mostly they chose the
Protestant schools. The taxes were paid by the
landlords, but as the number of Jewish landlords
was very small, the Protestant Board protested
that the taxes paid by the Jews were by no means
in proportion to the number of Jewish pupils attend-
ing their schools, and declined to acknowledge any
obligation to educate children of the Jewish faith,
and claimed the right to refuse to receive Jewish
pupils. A court case, instituted by a Jewish
parent, whose child had been refused a scholarship
which it had rightly won, upheld the Protestant
side. Immediately steps were taken to obtain
fair play for the Jewish children, and in April,
1903, a law was passed to the effect that all Jews
were to pay their taxes into the Protestant panel
and their children enjoy equal rights with the
rest of the pupils in the Protestant schools, and
given freedom in the matter of religious obser-
vance.
Nothing much ever happens in Australasia
according to “Whitaker’s Almanac.” In the
new issue, among the elaborate list of “Remark-
able Occurrences” of the last year, the following
sample extraordinary events —outside Australasia
—are noted :
The mission conveying the Order of the Garter for the
Mikado left London for Japan. The Prince and Princess
of Wales reached Rangoon. The Duke and Duchess of
Connaught arrived at Kimberley. A great fall of rocks
from the cliffs took place at Cheddar. A petition was
presented against the return of Mr. Fell for Yarmouth.
Prince Arthur of Connaught and the members of the
Garter mission were greeted by the Mikado in person at
Tokio. Japanese sailors from the Iyo Maru visited St.
Paul’s Cathedral and Westminster Abbey. The King,
travelling incognito, arrived in Paris. The King and
Queen received at Windsor a party of officers from two
Japanese warships that had visited Portsmouth. The
collection of wild animals presented by the Maharajah of
Nepal to the Prince of Wales arrived at the Zoological
Gardens. Mr. Winston Churchill, M.P., presided at a
festival dinner in honor of Miss Ellen Terry, held at the
Hotel Cecil. The King presented new colors to the 3rd
Battalion Grenadier Guards. The Report of the Royal
Commission on Ecclesiastical Disorders was issued. In a
collision between a motor-omnibus and a tramcar near
the Elephant and Castle several persons were injured.
The Bishop of London consecrated the enlarged chancel
of St. Margaret’s, Westminster. A fire occurred in a shop
in Seven Dials, by which three persons lost their lives.
Princess Louise, Duchess of Argyle, opened a new High
School for Girls at Headingly, Leeds.
And while these stirring episodes were taking
place, what was happening in Australasia?
According to “Whitaker” only five remarkable
events happened in regard to Maoriland —the
M. football team beat England; the M.L.
elections gave Mr. Seddon an overwhelming
majority; Mr. Seddon died, was. buried, and a
memorial service was held for him in St. Paul 3
Cathedral. And Australia had even a blanker
year. Only three events happened—according to
“Whitaker” :—A great fire broke out at Sydney,
N. causing damage estimated at £150,000.
The jubilee of Responsible Government in New
South Wales was celebrated at Sydney. The
Legislative Assembly of Westralia, by 19 votes
to 13, decided that the union of that State with
the Commonwealth was detrimental to the in-
terests of Westralia. That’s all.

Memories of the old Quetta, wrecked
off Cape York, in Torres Straits, some 17
years ago, were revived the other day by
the arrival at Sydney, by the Japanese
steamer Kuraano Maru, of several tons or
copper, steam tubes, and a lot of tin, portion ot
the luckless vessel and its cargo. Salving is still
going on, and the wreckers anticipate a good haul.
The Quetta’s bell, by the way, is still in service
at Quetta church, which was erected at ihujsday
Island in memory of the 133 victims of the
disaster.
“ The Mussel ” Wky does a plaintive,
dreary, monotonous drone appeal to the person
whose life is a fierce battle with all social organisa-
tions ? Just now in our street the band played a
plaintive monotonous melody—about lo verses,
and never a variation. At the 14th verse I had
figured out in my mind the whole thing, this
was about it—
. Never again shall sainted mother
Bash the head of dear old dad.
Over both the green sward clusters—
Heaven help an orphan lad !
While the band was playing this dirge, the
special brand of people I am talking about
sauntered out of the alleys and pubs, and occupied
more or less conspicuous positions according to
their then present financial conditions. Blimey
spat heavily on the footpath “Say, boxy,
that Weedin’ toon alius gets on me nerves
alius makes me feel like a baby wan tin to suck
one o’ those injer-rubber dummies,” This is no
exaggeration. I want to know why pathos
appeals more to the animal than to the man.
“ Snell ” : A thoroughly disreputable poet
once took me to his den over a very odorous
Chinese cook-shop in Carpenter’s Alley, and while
we sat luxuriously on the kerosene box settees, and
drank three pen’orth of beer out of lemonade
bottles, a fight arose in the alley below. The
contestants were two blousy females of about 40,
and they were disputing over beer. (Rarely is
there a dispute over anything else in Carpenter s
Alley.) The rest of the inhabitants took no
interest in the fight, as the fat women rolled and
tumbled on the garbage-covered road, and tore
each other’s hair and faces, and squealed like pigs
at dinner. Presently the smaller of the two
women came uppermost, and made such excellent
use of her advantage that she soon had the other
helpless and incapable of retaliation. At this
juncture, a thin, sandy man lounged to thedoor of
the fly-specked greengrocery opposite, removed
his pipe from his mouth, and said, slowly,
“That’ll do, Ceelier. You’ve give her quite
enough. Don’t forget yer a lady. Ceelier
then withdrew from the scrap, leaving her victim
groaning on the road.

“Speak, my darling'! Speak before you leave us!”
(Gasping'.) “Where’s my CAPSTANS?” (Gasp.)


“ W.” : In the course of a windy war on
the question “Is there an Australian dialect?”
lately raging in the West Australian, a corres-
pondent ventured the opinion that “one reason
Australians are disliked in England is their vile
distortion of the King’s English.” It is news that
Australians are disliked in England. But there are
types who pose as representative Australians who
would be hated anywhere for their blatancy, for
their vulgar ostentation, and for their base ingrati-
tude to the country that gave them all they possess.
One such returning from Europe, some years ago,
in the same boat as the wife of the then Governor-
General, sought desperately to be included in the
circle of which she was the hub. ‘ ‘Your ladyship
must hate to leave Home and have to live a nexile.
No society, no goin’ to court, no theayters worth
mentioning ” —he began to freeze as he met the
lady’s supercilious glance—“ But all the same,
yer ladyship, if yer ever in Perth you must come
to my little place.” “I never intend to visit
Perth ” was the reply, in accents that had been a
week in the refrigerating-chamber; and she never
did. Throughout the voyage the blatant little
snob was assiduous in his desire to “shout a
small bottle” for the lady, with a result that may
be guessed. Before the departure of the vessel
from Fremantle he made one huge effort to gain
the lady’s favor, and sent her a case of champagne
from his own cellar, with a note begging her
acceptance thereof, and the assurance that she
wouldn’t get better in her own house, for it cost
him “fifteen pound a dozen, and a bargain at
that.” And of such are “the representative
Australians.” But the worst are those who make
all they own here and then take it away to the
“deah old land,” where they struggle to enter
county society by disclaiming themselves as Aus-
tralians in any shape or form. Some day some of
these foolish people will learn that a true
Britisher does not despise them for being attached
to their country as he is to his, but has only
scorn for those who deny their patriotism, and
affect a love for a country in which they are, in
fact, little else than aliens.
“The Prooshan” : I am of opinion that
snorers are a curse to any country, and should be
heavily taxed. I would load them with a license
of at least £lO per annum, and compel them to
wear the license or snoring badge on some con-
spicuous part of their person. I would, more-
over, compel hotelkeepers to furnish special
snorers’ sleeping apartments, where these human
hogs could toot their foghorns as oft in the stilly
night as they chose without discomfort to other
tired flesh. To occupy a bedroom with a man
who owns a snore is to go to hell before you die.
I have rushed to bed at five minutes to nine,
hoping to get off to sleep before the arrival of a
snore lodger with his heavy artillery, but the
very dread of those awful runs and sudden barks
and yaps has kept me awake till the wretch
bowled into the room and said, “ ’Ope yer don’t
mind snorin’. I sometimes lies on me back.”
Well, I have lain and listened to the “piff, piff ”
prelude, then the “n’gare, n’gare, piff, n’gare”—
listened with black murder in my brain. I have
hearkened to that awful blast with cramped
nerves and a lump in my throat. I have shied
my left boot, my right boot, my trousers, my
curses at that infernal snore—in vain. Right up
till midnight has the storm raged, and upstairs
into the morrow. Cursed be the snorer !
“Poet-Scorner : ” Stood on the Gr.P.O.
steps one recent afternoon for a quarter
of an hour. In that time 150 persons of both
sexes passed, and 32 of them wore glasses. Twenty
of the wearers were women. Are Australians
becoming a short-sighted race like the Germans?
Or is it just affectation—especially on the part of
women and medical men ? I recently attended a
soiree at a well-known suburban doctor’s. It was a
long narrow room ; in it were twenty-two people of
both sexes, and only four of them without glasses.
Was asked to sing, and as I rose to comply all
glasses were focussed on me. Remarked to the
girl next to me that I felt (juite like a hot-house
plant. Come to the conclusion that if you haven’t
got brains you want dignity. Dignity gives the
appearance of brains. Glasses give dignity. So
if you want brains get glasses.

ISSUE OF 300,000 NEW SHARES, MAKING THE BHARE
CAPITAL £500,000 IN 1,000,000 SHARES OF 103.
IN accordance with the resolution of Share-
holders authorising the issue, the new shares
are offered at a premium of Is. to Shareholders,
pro rata to their holdings, on the following
terms :
Is. on application 3s. at three months
2s. on allotment 4s. at six months
making the shares paid up to 95., and leaving Is.
per share to be called up if and when required.
It is intended to devote not less than £150,000
of the proceeds of the issue to the purposes of the
Etheridge Railway, including all preliminary ex-
penditure already incurred, and the costs of the
issue.
In order to fully cover the cost of the railway,
it is proposed to supplement this issue of shares
by an issue of debentures, secured on the
Company’s interest in the railway, and, if re-
quired, by an increase of the existing debenture
debt to the extent of £75,000. The consent of
the Company’s debenture-holders to this increase
has been given.
The entire issue of 300,000 shares is under-
written, and any shares not applied for on or be-
fore 7th February, 1907, may then be allotted to
the underwriters.
C. L. HEWITT, Secretary.
39 Queen Street, Melbourne,
17th January , 1907.
THE CHILLAGOE COMPANY LTD.
ETHERIDGE RAILWAY.
THE proposed Etheridge Railway is authorised
by the “Etheridge Railway Act of 1906,”
described as “an Act to validate an Agreement
entered into between the Secretary for Railways,
for and on behalf of the Government of Queens-
land, and the Chillagoe Railway and Mines
Limited, for the construction of a line of railway
from Chillagoe to Georgetown, and to authorise
the construction of the said railway, and to make
provision for certain matters incidental to the
construction, maintenance, management, and
working thereof.”
In the Agreement specified it is provided, inter
alia /
CLAUSE 2.—The Etheridge Railway shall commence
at or near Chillagoe, and shall proceed thence by a route
to he mutually agreed upon, and to be approved by the
Commissioner, to a point at or near Georgetown, on the
Etheridge goldfield, to be selected and approved of by
the Commissioner and the Company upon considerations
of probable traffic and construction cost.
CLAUSE 6.—The cost of construction, including the
actual cost of material, duty, and charges made for
freight, carriage, and insurance, and of labor, including
supervision and inspection, and of survey, and of broker-
age, and of underwriting charges incurred in providing
the money necessary to cover such cost, and of law costs,
shall not exceed £450,000, and the amount thereof shall
be ascertained and decided as follows :
All payments shall be duly vouched for, and
vouchers'for such payments shall be audited by the
Commissioner, and if in his opinion such payments
are fit and proper charges against the Railway
Construction Account, shall be passed by him as
such.
Upon the completion of the railway, the total of
such vouchered expenditure, duly certified by the
Commissioner, shall be the cost of construction of
the railway.
CLAUSE 7.—The Etheridge Railway, when completed,
shall be worked, manned, and maintained by the Com-
missioner with his own staff.
CLAUSE B.—The rates of tolls, fares, freights, and
charges for the carriage of goods, live stock, parcels, and
passengers, to be charged by the Commissioner on the
Etheridge Railway shall be calculated on a mileage
basis, and shall be the same as the rates authorised on
the Chillagoe Railway under the schedule of rates, fares,
and freights, dated 12th September, 1900, and authorised
by the Governor-in-Council under “The Mareeba to
Chillagoe Railway Act of 1897.’-’
CLAUSE 9.—For all the purposes of this Agreement,
the cost of the management and working expenses of the
Etheridge Railway shall be ascertained on the basis of
the train mileage' and the cost of maintenance on the
basis of mileage length; but the total working and
maintenance expenses charged by the Commissioner shall
not be higher than one-and-one-quarter times the average
annual total cost of working and maintaining the Chil-
lagoe Railway during the three years preceding the date
of this Agreement.
CLAUSE 10.—From the commencement and until the
Etheridge Railway is actually acquired by the Govern-
ment of Queensland in the manner hereinafter provided,
half-yearly balances shall be made, and any profit made
from working the railway in any half-year over and
above the bonus or subsidy hereinafter mentioned, shall
go and belong to the Company.
CLAUSE 11.—The total cost of the Etheridge Railway,
as specified in Clause 6 hereof, shall be provided by the
Company with the assistance of the Government, such
assistance to be as follows :
(a) The payment by the Government during the cur-
rency of the Agreement of a sum equal and amount-
ing to 2£ per cent, per annum on such cost
actually incurred (and not exceeding £450,000),
payable half-yearly; but in the computation of
these half-yearly payments, the moneys expended
by the Company on this work during the currency
of any half-year shall only be taken into account
as from the end of such half-year, and the total
expenditure for the half-year shall be treated as .
if it had all been incurred on the last day of that
half-year whereon interest shall accrue during,
and be payable on the day after the end of, the
next succeeding half-year;
(b) by the purchase by the Government of the Ethe-
ridge Railway at the termination of fifteen years
from the date hereof. The price to be paid by
the Government shall be twenty-eight and four-
sevenths times the amount of the average
annual net earnings (that is to say, the excess of
receipts over management, maintenance, and
working expenses) of the line during the period
of five years immediately preceding the date of
the purchase of the line.
CLAUSE 12.—The payment by the Government of the
amount due to the Company for the purchase of the
Etheridge Railway shall be made in London to the Com-
pany or its nominee, and may, at the option of the
Government, be made either in cash or in inscribed stock,
bearing interest at 4 per cent, per annum.
CLAUSE 15.—1 f there be any difference between the
amount of the price of the Etheridge Railway—to be
ascertained as herein provided—and the certified cost of
its construction, the amount of such difference shall be
divided equally between the Government and the Com-
pany. If such price is less than such cost, one-half of the
deficiency shall be added to and be payable with the
amount of the price. If, on the other hand, such price is
greater than such cost, one-half of the excess shall be
deducted from such price.
The length of the Etheridge Railway, according
to the surveys conducted jointly by the Govern-
ment and the Company, will be about 147 miles.
The cost of construction is estimated by the
Engineer-in-Chief of Queensland at £348,368 15s.
9d., and by the Company’s Engineer at £325,059
18s. 6d.
The territory—mineral and pastoral—within
the sphere of influence of the proposed railway is
very extensive, and apparently possesses great
natural resources, the development of which has
been retarded or made impossible through the
absence of a railway.
Within the territory is embraced the Etheridge
Goldfield.
In 1905 Mr. E. A. Weinberg, M.I.M. & M.
(London), was engaged to ascertain and report on
the economic value of the mineral resources of the
field in direct relation to the proposed railway.
Mr. Weinberg devoted some months to the
work, and his report is conclusively in favor of
the railway. He gives the area of the field as
about 4000 square miles; the number of known
reefs, 140; and the value of gold won, £2,000,000.
Over a large portion of the field contained in
the “Granite” area, Mr. Weinberg states, the
reefs are more or less heavily mineralised below
the water level with iron pyrites and galena,
accompanied by blende and copper pyrites, all in
combination with gold and silver, but that such
ores, owing to their complex character, as well as
through the existing isolation of the field, cannot
be worked at present to any advantage.
He likewise calls attention to the great possi-
bilities contained in the “ Sedimentary ” area of
the field, which shows abundant evidence of the
presence of many rich silver-lead and copper
lodes, hitherto remaining practically untouched
for want of proper means of communication with
the outside world.
As all these ores require to be smelted, it is safe
to assume that the most natural outlet for them
will be found eventually at the Chillagoe Works,
which, with their modern installation, will be in
a position to offer to the mine-owner the best
facilities for the economical treatment of all
classes of smelting ores.
Mr. Weinberg’s report, in concluding, states :
“ It would be impossible for anybody to express
in absolute figures the possiblities of expansion,
but taking the field as it is to-day, without allow-
ing for the chances of some future discoveries,
which, in the enormous area affected by the new
railway line, may reasonably he expected. I
believe, the field to be capable of supporting a
settled mining population of 5000 to 6000 people.
Furthermore, it must not be overlooked that the
Etheridge Railway is to be regarded above all as a
great pioneering enterprise, whose object it is to
open a large tract of country to capital and labor.
One thing is certain to lead to another, and as the
line will eventually be the main outlet for the
whole Gulf country, the elements of speculation
are reduced to a minimum.”
In a subsequent report, Mr. Weinberg states :
“In my opinion, by the time the Etheridge
Railway is completed, a mineral traffic of about 80
tons per day may be depended upon. It is reason-
ably certain that this will increase from year to
year, so that one may be justified in saying that
within three years of the completion of the line
the probable quantity will have increased to about
150 tons, or say 45,000 tons per annum. This
does not take into consideration any ore reserves
which are certain to be discovered between the
time actual construction work on the line has
commenced and its completion.”
The mineral traffic indicated should chiefly or
wholly consist of ore for treatment at the Com-
pany’s metallurgical works at Chillagoe. Small
trial parcels of ore have been carted to the works,
at a reported cost of £9 per ton, the journey
occupying about fifteen days. One lot is reported
to have contained over 20 per cent, lead, o'9 per
cent, copper, 3oz. 14dwt. gold, 3qz. Bdwt.
silver per ton ; another lot 36 2 per cent, lead, 1
per cent, copper, 16dwt. gold, 6oz. silver per
ton ; another lot 39’2 per cent, lead, IT per cent,
copper, 14dwt. gold, 6‘9oz. silver per ton. The
Directors have been advised that other parcels of
silver-lead ore, containing over 60 per cent, lead,
and from 60 to 90oz. of silver per ton, have found
their way to the coast via Croydon-Nor man ton
for shipment to southern smelting works, and
that there appear to be large quantities of ore
similar in character available, which can jinly be
profitably dealt with by smelting, and with rail
connection to Chillagoe should eventually all
come to the Company’s Works.
As to the probable effect of the proposed rail-
way on the traffic returns of the Company’s
existing line. The latter is practically an exten-
sion of the Government railway from Cairns, as
the new railway will be an extension of the
Chillagoe line to the Etheridge. The total annual
receipts on the Cairns Railway prior to the open-
ing of the Chillagoe Railway were considerably
less than the working expenses, whereas for the
last official year the net earnings were officially
reported to have been £37,523.
The influence of the Etheridge Railway on the
Chillagoe traffic receipts would be felt over a con-
siderably greater mileage, and the advantage
should therefore be proportionately greater.
The Company’s Superintendent, Mr. F. Back,
has givGn this matter careful consideration, and
he has furnished the following opinion -.—“Ethe-
ridge Railway.— Within six months of com-
mencement the revenue of the Chillagoe Railway
should be increased by 15 per cent. ; on comple-
tion, 20 per cent. —without including any mineral
traffic. With one additional smelter furnace, 40
per cent.; with two additional furnaces, 60 per
cent. These figures are conservative. As the
fixed charges on the cost of maintenance of the
Chillagoe Railway will remain practically as at
present, the profit on the additional business will
considerably exceed the present ratio of profit.
Expert reports point to the conclusion that as
soon as the Etheridge Railway is completed,
sufficient ore will be available to render an addi-
tional smelter furnace immediately necessary.”
The gross revenue of the Company’s Chillagoe
Railway for the past three years was as follows: —
1904 £49,128
1905 £63,871
1906 £81,009
The approximate gross value of metals pro-
duced at the Company’s Works for the same
period amounted to :
1904 £142,625
1905 £125,217
1906 £229,200
C. L. HEWITT, Secretary.
17th January, 1907.

Legal Notice.
Re J AMES STEWART OR JAMES HUNTER
STEWART DECEASED.
THE persons claiming to be next-of-kin accord-
ing to the Statutes for the distribution of in-
testates’ estates of JAMES STEWART or
JAMES HUNTER STEWART late of Childers
in the State of Queensland Sugar Planter who was
born at Prestwick Ayrshire Scotland who died at
Childers aforesaid on the eleventh day of Novem-
ber 1904 living at the time of his death or to be the
legal personal representatives of such of the said
next-of-kin as are now dead are hereby required on
or before the eighteenth day of March 1907 to
send in particulars of their claims to Queensland
Trustees Limited 41 Queen Street Brisbane the
Administrators of the said deceased. And notice
is hereby given that after that day the said
Queensland Trustees Limited will proceed to dis-
tribute the assets of the said deceased amongst
the parties entitled thereto having regard only to
the claims of which they shall then have had
notice.
DATED this fifteenth day of January 1907.
CHAMBERS & McNAB,
Adelaide St., Brisbane,
Agents for
STAFFORD, CORSER & HARRINGTON,
Maryborough,
Solicitors for the said Administrators.

The Capture of Harry Groat.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
AFTER a night of rain, the warm sun
shone on a clean country road from a sky
of the richest blue. There was a lilt in
the piping of the birds, and a keen freshness in
the washed air that made Harry Groat sing with
all the light-heartedness of 21. In the distance
he saw the white flash of a sun-bonnet, worn by a
girl on a restless horse. He quickened his own
pace. When the canter of his own horse reached
the ears of the animal under the girl, it began to
curvet and toss its head ; then it began to buck.
He galloped to her assistance. The girl was a
good rider, but he saw she was getting nervous.
Reining in and dismounting, he ran to seize the
horse’s bridle. It plunged and swerved swiftly ;
she lost her balance, and was pitched almost on
top of him, and they rolled on the damp ground
together; while the freed horse galloped with
jingling bit, mane and tail streaming, along the
road. Groat scrambled to his feet, and went to
the girl.
“ Hurt ?” he said, anxiously.
“ I don’t think so—l’d like ter kill the brute.”
“ You shouldn’t ride a half-broken animal like
that.”
“ I broke it meself. You frightened it,” she
said, indignantly. He felt relieved. Then he saw
she was a rather pretty girl, none the less so from
being dishevelled by her fall. He laughed
lightly. She glanced up, then shyly down again,
and pulled down her skirt. His eyes began to
light.
“ What shall Ido ? The ’orse ’as gone, ’an I’ve
ter go to ther township.”
“It’s only another mile. I’ll walk with you. I
expect somebody will find the horse there.” He
helped her to her feet. Placing the bridle of his
own horse over his arm, he stepped out beside her.
She would not look at him directly, feeling his
eager eyes scanning her face. He was tantalised.
Her eyes were very blue too, and her skin was
clear, aglow with health.
“ Have you been long in the district ? ” he asked.
“ I wus born ’ere. 1 know you, Mr. Groat.”
“ It’s curious that I haven’t met you before. I
was born here, too.”
“But you’s a squatter, ’an I’m on’y cocky
Waddel’s daughter.” There was a touch of
attractive defiance in her voice.
“You’re a neighbor then. I often ride'past
your place.”
“ That’s ’ow I know yer.”
“ I’ve a keen eye for a pretty face, and wonder
that I missed seeing you before. You are pretcv,
you know.” She tried not to wriggle with
pleasure, which relieved him from the embarrass-
ment of his crudity.
“You gentlemen is all alike; besides, I’ve ’eard
that before.”
“ You’re the first who has heard those words
from me.” He gazed at her half admiringly, half
quizzingly.
“Do you really think I’m pretty?” She was
wholly serious.
“Yes,” he answered, slowly. “Pretty and
strong. Your hair is auburn and peeps from
under that sun-bonnet in the prettiest little curls,
and your dress has the simplicity of the highest
art.”
“ You’re laffln’ at me,” she pouted.
“Beauty has never been laughed at seriously
yet.” She looked puzzled. “ I mean that—that
—perhaps I ought not to tell you.”
“ Oh do, please,” she asked, eagerly.
“I mean, then, that beauty is always laughed
with, not at. ’ She was stjll puzzled. Suddenly
her eyes lighted merrily.
“ You mean that yer likes me,” she said.
She was a simple-minded maiden.
Every Saturday afternoon, for two months
past, Tom Jones and Dick Smith had come
to see Mr. Waddel. They were both slightly
over six feet, proportionately built,, and
powerful men. Their occupation was post-and-
rail-splitting or fencing contracts. At present
they were on a contract, fencing for old Mr.
Groat. When the work was finished they pro-
posed to buy farms. They were rather silent men,
and listened to Waddel’s instructions on the art
of farming with admirable patience. They were
rivals and still friends. To say they worshipped
Judy was expressing it mildly. To them she was
the embodiment of all the womanly virtues ; some-
thing to live and work for sincerely. The
search for the Holy Grail was nothing in
poetic intensity with their desire for Judy.
Judy utterly failed to understand them. She
knew why they talked farm to her father, and
listened so patiently to his droning ; but she was
always on the verge of laughing at them. If they
had told her she was pretty she would have
understood them, but their silent adoration was
ridiculous. Of late, Tom and Dick noticed that
she had begun to avoid them. When she did
speak it was abstractedly. They listened more
and more silently to her father, and the old man’s
remarks on farming grew more and more dreary.
Then they discovered that a sleek-headed, imina-
culately-dressed stranger was coming to the
house; but were immensely relieved to see that
Judy just tolerated him, apparently through awe
of his clothes. He was a clerk in a store, and
on £2 a week dressed in the latest English
fashion, or what he believed to be such. He never
deigned to speak to Tom and Dick, though he
condescended to nod if he saw them in the street.
But J udy grew more distant towards them, and
when they missed one Saturday afternoon she did
not seem to mind. The following Saturday night
they rode home in silent melancholy. Judy had
left them early, and they believed she was becom-
ing indifferent. They were passing a thick belt
of scrub in a creek flat, when they were roused
from their depressing thoughts by hearing a
woman sobbing. They pulled up and listened,
ihe sobs grew louder. Dismounting, they
hitched their horses to the fence and crept
quietly into the scrub. They had recognised
Judy’s voice.
* * * *
Judy had met Harry Groat by appointment.
She was weepy. He was anxious and angry too,
with himself and her, and inwardly swearing at
himself for a fool.
“This nonsense will have to stop,” he began.
“What nonsense?” she asked, gazing at him
tearfully, and holding up her face. He kissed it
mechanically. SHe felt the coldness, and clung to
him warmly.
“ This nonsense of our love making.”
“ But yer said yer loved me.” She was getting
a little angry. “ I didn’t a§k yer. Nobuddy ast
yer. She released him and flopped on her knees.
“I could kill j'er, and meself too.” He shivered
with disgust, thinking of his sisters, their English
accent and falsetto voices; and comparing their
vocabulary with Judy’s lack of grammar.
“We’re not playing at romance from a penny
novelette,” he said, contemptuously. And it will
have to stop, that’s all about it.”
But you began it,” she insisted.
“It takes two for the business.”
“ Yer saved me life.”
“I’d do it again, and do it again for any other
woman on an unbroken horse. Confound the
brute ! I wish it had broken y —my neck.”
“It can’t stop. I’ll tell yer father.”
“What do you mean, Judy?” he asked
quickly, his heart contracting. She bent her
head on her knees, and sobbed brokenly. “ Don’t
cry, Judy,” he said, more gently. “I’m really
verv sorry. I didn’t quite mean all I said. But,
really, it must go no further.” She seized and
clung to his hand.
“ Oh, I can’t let yer go. You won’t ask me,
will yer, ’Arry ? I’m on’y a girl, an’ trusted yer
with all I ’ave. Oh, Harry, you can’t never give
me back all I gave yer.” He stooped and raised
her to her feet.. She hung to him passionately.
He submitted in rigid unresponsiveness.
“ I—l—have to go away.” She looked up
quickly. “I’m to leave for Queensland to-
morrow.”
“ Can’t yer take me with yer ? ”
“ It’s impossible.”
“ Won’t yer see me agen ? ”
“ I’ll try. Now, don’t make a fuss, Judy.” He
put his arm round her. She flung it away and
threw herself face downwards on the ground,
sobbing loudly. Before he could stoop again,
Tom and Dick broke through the bushes. The
night was brilliant with stars and a low moon.
Dick rushed at Groat, who hit him between the
eyes and sent him staggering backwards ; and got
a blow on the chest from Tom, which made him
grunt. Judy sat up with a scream. She saw the
three men fighting savagely, their blood being
fully up, and saw them steadily overpower Groat.
Grabbing a heavy stick, she sprang to her
feet. She gave Torn a crack on the head
that dropped him like a log; then, aiming at
Dick, she cut a gash in his cheek with the jagged
end as he dodged back. Before she could strike
again he wrenched the stick from her hand. She
shrieked for help. Her screams carried far in the
still night air, and reached the ears of her father
as he was tumbling into bed. He tore across the
paddock, half-dressed. When he arrived Judy
was lying on the ground again, face downwards.
Tom was near her. Groat had crawled away.
Ihe situation called for a lot of explaining, and
in the midst of the explanation Waddel knocked
Dick out with a sudden blow on the point.
» * * *
It was a week before Harry Groat was able to
leave his bed ; then he slipped unostentatiously
to the train. He spent a fortnight in Svdney and
Brisbane before taking the train for Charleville.
Arriving there, he sent a telegram to the manager
of the station 150 miles ahead, and having thus
announced his coming, he started on his ride.
The day the telegram reached the manager, two
large men rode up and asked him for a job at
scrub-cutting or fencing. He told them that the
boss would arrive in a couple of days, and that
they had better ask him. The pair went away
with stolid faces.
Harry Groat rode across a great level plain, hot
and sun-scorched, following a wire fence as guide,
till the end of the third day. He was in a genial
mood, and enjoyed the vast solitude. The fence
was like a human companion, and he even con-
fided to it his trouble of 1500 miles away. He was
equally divided between pity for Judy and con-
tempt for himself. Also, on second thoughts, he
felt sorry for himself and a little contemptuous to-
wards Judy. He resolved to profit by his
experience and keep away from local beauty for
the rest of his life.
The sun began to set, the warm blue shadows
to lengthen, and a faint chill was coming into the
afternoon air. Once he thought he saw two
horsemen moving parallel to him in the distance;
but when he cooeed they disappeared behind a
patch of timber. He looked round for indica-
tions of their hut, in the hope of a shake-down and
company for the night; but not seeing them, he
resolved to camp under a tree, as before. Reach-
ing a shallow creek bed just after the sun had set,
he hobbled the horses, and building a fire, he had
some tea ; then rolled into his blanket.
He was awakened by hearing a voice speaking
in a low tone. The grey tint of coming day was
in the air, and the twitter of an early bird. He
sat up quickly, and saw two shadowy figures
seated by his fire, which was blazing brightly
under a couple of black billies. Beyond them
were six horses, including his own two.
“ Who are you fellows ? ” he demanded.
“We’ve come for you, Mr. Groat,”answered
one of them.
“ Anything wrong at Danagla ? What do you
want with me?”
“We’ve come ter take yer back ter Judy
Waddel.” One of them took a sip from the lid
of his billy, while the other poured some tea into
the lid of his.
“ Good God, men, are you mad ? ”
Not a bit of it. You’re a blackguard, and
were tryin’ ter make yer less o’ one, if possible;
an you’ve got ter marry Judy as yer promised.”
Groat laughed in astonished incredulity, but
with a touch of dread creeping into his voice.
You can t make a fellow marry against his
will,” he argued.
“ True enough; but we can take yer to ’er
father, an’ yer can settle it with ’im an’ Judy.”
“ Take me to her ? ” gasped Groat.
“ Yers. You’ve got to start now. It’s no use,
Groat. There’s two on us, an’ yer’d better go
quiet to save us damagin’ yer. We’re goin’ ter
act square as long’s yer quiet.” They had risen
and placed themselves on each side of him. One
look at their wiry bodies showed that physical
resistance was out of the question. He silently
rose, torn between a desire to laugh and be angry
that sent him to the verge of hysteria. Then he
quietly mounted his horse. After riding for some
minutes, he felt more command of himself.
“The manager is expecting me the day after
to-morrow. I sent a telegram. You’ll have the
police searching for me in a few days.”
We thought o’ that, and, usin’ yer name, we
sent ’im another, sayin’ yer’d gone ter Bourke fer
a month.”
“ That’s forgery, man.”
“An’ promisin’ Judy Waddel ver’d marry’er
was lyin’.”
Day after day they rode, avoiding all settle-
ments, camping out under trees. Tom and Dick
took it in turns to get provisions. They never
passed within 100 yards of a stranger if they
could avoid it. If someone wanted to talk, either
Tom or Dick gratified him, while the other rode
quickly on with Groat. He found them friendly
and obliging, and at last took quite a liking for
his quaint captors; marvelling at their expert
bushmanship as they found their way across coun-
try which he would never have attempted with-
out direction.
Their difficulties increased when they crossed
the Murray, which they swam at break of day.
But the people in more thickly-populated Vic-
toria were less curious, more accustomed to
travellers.
It was about nine o’clock of one fine evening
when the horses were hitched to Waddel’s fence.
A bright light guided them to the kitchen, which
they unceremoniously entered, their captive
between them. Waddel was seated at the head
of the table, and stared at them with astonish-
ment. Judy was on one side of the hearth, her head
screwed round, and amazement written large on
her face. Opposite her was an oily-headed youth,
sleek and neatly-dressed in tweed, with a very
high-starched collar, and an air of disturbed
though still smug satisfaction about him.
“We’ve brought Mr. Groat back ter you,
Judy,” announced Dick, completely ignoring the
sleek stranger. Judy blushed vividly, and the
stranger stared at their rough and travel-stained
clothes contemptuously.
“What ther blazes d’yer mean marcliin’inter
me ’ouse like that?” demanded Mr. Waddel,
angrily.
“ Yer said yer wanted ter make Mr. Groat act
fair by Judy,” answered Dick. “We went an’
brought ’im.”
“Oo ther blazes ast yer?” That was a poser.
Judy glanced apprehensively at the dapper
young man, who was staring uncomfortably into
the fire.
“I did’n’ ask yer,” she said, looking back at
Dick and Tom. Groat’s eyes began to glisten.
“It seems to me there’s some mistake,” said
the young man, apprehensively. Tom and Dick
looked foolishly puzzled.
“What are you doin’ ’ere?” demanded Tom,
stepping menacingly towards him.
“I —ah—really doan’t think lii’ve enny reason
to explain that to you.” The tremor of dread in
his tone spoilt his grammar. Mr. Waddel rose
with a jump that upset his chair. Judy screeched
“Father, 1 ain’t’avin’ them idjits interferin’in me
affairs.”
“Oo’s that feller, Judy?” asked Dick, indi-
cating the stranger.
. “ ’E-’e-’e’s me fiancy,” replied Judy, looking at
him with affectionate pride. Groat leaned help-
lessly against the door-post, while Waddel
grabbed Tom and Dick by the arms and led them
to the door. They were too dazed to resist.
VIC.
W. BRENTA.


"N.L.”: Meaty similes seem to be
popular in Melbourne nowadays. A few recently
heard in the street: “By closing time Perce
was floundering about like a leg of mutton, and
we got him down to the station he was as
helpless as a paralysed pork chop.” Of a limp
one’s salutation: “It was like shaking hands with
a pound of sausages.” A four-year-old, who was
being dragged down Collins-street hill by a
hurrying parent, wailed : “ Mother, you’re
pulling me along like a dead sausage !”
The other day a Ballarat senior-constable
was hauled up on a charge of “ misconduct.”
He had advised a young man, a gas-inspector, to
keep away from tote-shops, etc. ! It looked like
a highly desirable piece of advice; but it seems
that this gas-inspector, as well as another young
man (a miner), had for some time, unknown to
the constable, been engaged as secret police to
ferret out the doings of the totes, while still fol-
lowing their regular occupations. So, as the
fatherly senior-constable’s advice to the young
man to avoid tote-shops was in conflict with the
law’s advice to cultivate them all he knew, the
fatherly officer was punished by being transferred
to another district. Under the system that em-
ploys private citizens, regularly following private
occupations, as police spies, an officer can’t be too
careful with his advice. He never knows when
the man he warns against crime isn’t paid by his
bosses to commit it.

the wickedness of the world, and explaining what a good
r!loiVhm d 0 ha mi. lf *; ake his advice and be miserable
right here. The other two stood by meekly, with rueful
countenances and psalm books open, ready for immediate
ac ,!f n - he entire audience consisted of a dog, that sat
with bent head and drooping ears, and an infant that
off a distance, and seemed greatly fascinated by
the gyrations of the speaker’s arms. People passed to
and fro, but all seemed wholly oblivious of the orator’s
presence. Yet he kept on talking, and appeared to be so
interested in what he was saying that he didn’t have |
time to notice that no one was listening to him. Sud-
denly the trio lifted their voices in a stirring psalm. It
The doff got up and walked off
husriedly, and the parent of the child came and took it
away. Then the meeting gathered itself together with
great deliberation and departed.
Alba : Have asked dozens of wharf
laborers and cargo shifters to name the particular
load they prefer dodging. One said his worst
experience was the unloading of a damaged cargo
of wet sait m hot weather. At the end of the
third day his back and shoulders looked as though
a cat-o -nine tails had cut into them. Guano
Jilting on shelterless reefs and islands is a worse
job than clearing up a Hindoo plague soot. In
October last I saw a dead black whale being hauled
t0 /Mc m, the trying-out works. Twofold
xiay (IN.o. W.) The men at thecapstan said they’d
sooner ship a cargo of dead Chows. It was a
sixty-ton whale and the gas whistled through the
harpoon holes like a Highlander’s bagpipes.
Generally speaking, the most trying load for the
male animal to carry is a restless baby. Have
met timber-getters and lorrymen who could pile
up railway stock ana beer casks for years without
turning a hair, but when it came to nursing a
380 oz. baby for anything over 1000yds., they
usually caved .in with apologies. Toughest
problem m my life was the carrying of my own
sick child, aged 18 months, to a doctor, it was
over a boulder-strewn gully road on a hot January
morning. When I arrived at the doctor’s he pro-
nounced my case to be one of complete shock and
exhaustion. The baby was well within a week,
but it was months before I pulled round.
T. O’Fipp ”: A Bulletin writer re-
marked lately that many valiant drinkers see
other things' than snakes, when one of their
periods for seeing things eventuates. The obser-
vation is borne out by the case of a suburban
draper, who has occasionally rum shrub in his
hair. This gentleman designs many of the
“creations” that are sold in his establishment,
and it is a solemn fact that dresses and hats
described as “the latest Paris models” invariably
turn up in his shop a week or two after he has
been in the horrors. They are weirdly original
productions, and are very popular among the
girls of the neighborhood. In private, the draper
alleges that they are copies of his fantods as far
as he can recollect them, with a feather stuck in
the rim of each fantod, and a big, artificial flower
in the jim-jam.

Managing Director - - - J. E. SCANTLEBURY.
Bert Levy, the well-known artist, formerly
of Sydney, is making a splash of no small dimen-
sions in the U.S. In fact, he is making three
distinct splashes—as a stage artist and enter-
tainer ; as the inventor of an apparatus which,
while he makes his lightning sketches, reflects
them in gigantic proportions on the wall; and as
a preacher to Hebrew congregations. One
Hebrew congregation stood Levy for an hour and
three-quarters, so he seems to be a decidedly
eloquent preacher.
“ Mac ” writes :
There is some reason for the yell from Melbourne
municipal councils against Bent’s intention (either this
session or next) of putting the Metropolitan Board of
Works, along with the moth-eaten Harbor Trust and
Marine Board, under the control of Commissioners. The
Board of Works is a taxing body, and might become a
very magnificent and disastrous taxing body under the
control of an autocratic Commissioner. But the coun-
cils, and the electors behind them, have themselves to
blame. The Board of Works, composed of councils’
nominees, has been a shocking clique of Bumbles, which
wasted a great amount of money and a still more
deplorable amount of time. It didn’t waste intelligence,
for there was practically none. A smaller Board elected
directly by the ratepayers, and paid to do its work
efficiently and economically, might have managed things
better.
“F.H.” : I was in a country court, the
other day, when a man was sentenced for per-
jury. He pleaded that he had only been in court
twice in his life, that he was ignorant of the
gravity of the offence, and that when he was
sworn he did not understand what the constable
said. And I don’t wonder at it. For instance,
John Hodge is called. He hurries in, nervous
and frightened, is hustled into the witness-box, a
book is jammed into his hand, and he hears —
“ Th’ ev’dence y’ sh’ll giv’ ’n th’s cas’now’fore
co’rt sh’ll be trut’ th’ ’ole trut’ ’n’ nothin’ but th’
trut’ s’welp y’ Gawd kiss a buk.” The constable
does his best to make it appear one long word.
And before the bewildered yokel collects his wits
the magistrate sternly tells him that if he does
not kiss the Book at once, after being repeatedly
told to do so, he will commit him for contempt.
By this time John Hodge is confused enough to
swear anything. I would respectfully suggest
that in future tne following be said slowly to each
witness: “You shall speak the whole truth in
this case, and nothing but the truth, or you are
liable to a term of imprisonment, the length of
which will be governed by the state of the Judge’s
liver ; and may the Lord have mercy on your
soul.” Then a witness would know what he had
to expect.
