“F.H.R.” : A very unusual thing hap-
pened at a mine where I was working once—it
was an inspector. Not that inspectors never
came round, but this one actually inspected. He
condemned the wire-rope in use, and caused
much embarrassment by asking to see a tamping-
rod. After feverish search, the shift boss had to
admit there wasn’t one in the mine. The con-
demnation of the wire-rope gave the whip horses
a well-deserved spell, but put the additional
burden on us of climbing 400ft. of perpendicular
ladders after each heavy shift. The first evening
there was a lot of grumbling. When about an
hour after knock-off, Big Joe (who weighed I6st.)
reached the surface he made remarks that would
not bear print. Next evening he rushed the
ladders, thinking to get a fair start ; but several
miners were soon at his heels cursing his slow-
ness. Suddenly he stopped altogether, caught
fast in a man-hole. Fourteen men below him
were cursing and shouting advice up to him. The
next man to Joe called loudly for “half a plug
and a primer, we’ve struck a horse.” Someone
below wanted to know if a pick would be of any
use. Joe’s crib-can was fast to his belt and
effectually stopped his progress upwards, while he
couldn’t for the world get his arms down to
release it. A young Cornishman had his shoulder
under a convenient portion of Joe’s anatomy by
this time, and was doing his best to shift man,
timber and all. Then someone saw what the
trouble was, and cut Joe’s strap. Joe forged
ahead once more, and as his white limbs (his
trousers came right off) gleamed above us in
the candle-light, the man below playfully singed
the fluff off. Joe took ill next day, and didn’t
appear again until a new rope was installed.
A once promising young man who chose
the worse part is Westby Perceval, who now
walks round London with a handle to his name
and the brand of the head station prominently
displayed. He is a Tasmanian by birth, but early
in his life drifted over to Maoriland and became
one of the democratic members for Christchurch.
He got his opportunity the day that Ballance
elimbed to the Premiership. He might have been
associated with Reeves, Ballance and M'Kenzie
in the memorable burst of legislation that arrested
the attention of the world. But he chose to go to
London to be Agent-General. Then when a title
was offered to Ballance, the great democrat
shrugged his shoulders. “Oh, give it to Per-
ceval, he rather fancies that sort of thing, I
believe! ” said the Maoriland Strong Man.
And given to Perceval it was. And Perceval’s
services to humanity stopped right there.
One has only to look at the portraits of
Victorian Governors in Melbourne Picture Gallery
to recognise what a very small circumstance the
imported State Excellency has become. The early
Governors, such as Hotliam and La Trobe, had
huge canvases devoted to them. And in addition,
Hotham had 15 street!, lanes, and alleys, and one
suburb (now disguised under another designation)
named after him, and La Trobe gave his name to 12
highways and one river. Now, mere photographs
are considered good enough for “ their Excel-
lencies.” These range from fairly large, in the
case of Loch, to very small, in the case of Tal-
bottle; and no one names even his dog after a
Governor these times. There does not appear to
be room for any more portraits of Excellencies in
the Gallery either, and the gubernatorial institu-
tion is falling off, like Brassey.

membrane of the nose and adjoining
passages. If this inflammation is not ar-
rested it invades the passages which lead
from the nose to the head, ears, throat and
lungs. It injures the sight and hearing,
destroys the sense of taste and smell, ren-
ders the breath offensive, breaks down
the affected tissues, and consumes the
nasal cartilages. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and stomach, causes
dyspepsia, also consumption. Do you
want relief and cure? If so, try our
great remedy.

Says She Was a Total Wrecß.
Mrs. C. COOPER, of MarricKville, tells of Remarkable
Salvage of Her Wrecked System made by
Clements Tonic.
[BY A SPECIAL REPORTER.]
In an interview with our representative Mrs.
Caroline Cooper, of No. 3 Fitzroy-street, Marrick-
ville, gives some information to the afflicted which
should help them considerably. Her statement is
as follows:
“My health is altogether different now to what
it was some years ago, as there was a time when I
was nothing but a total wreck, and now people are
always telling me how well and strong I look.
Yes, I am not like the same person at all, for now
I am always contented and happy, instead of being
intensely miserable like I used to be. But Heaven
knows I had enough to make me so, for the pains
in my head were so great that many a day I hardly
knew what I was doing. In each temple there
was a nasty beating sensation, which caused me
so much agony that often I thought I would,
surely go mad.”
“You would welcome the arrival of bed-time,
so that you could sleep and forget your afflictions
for a while ? ” suggested the writer.
“One would naturally think so, but I didn’t,
for the simple reason that I could seldom get any i
sleep, and oh! how those hours of wakefulness
used to wear me out! Truly, when I got up of a
morning I felt worse than if I had not been in bed
at all, and I always had a bitter taste to make me !
feel sick for the rest of the daj\ Sometimes I got
so thoroughly exhausted that it would have been j
impossible to walk had I tried, to, but knowing i
how weak I was I used to stay indoors to be on |
the safe side. And another thing, I was aecus- ;
tomed to taking fits of giddiness, and often I went j
right off and did not know anything that was going j
on, so you see I had to be careful. And wasn’t I
nervous ? Goodness ! I only had to hear a little-
shout or a whistle and I’d actually jump again,
and it would be a long time afterwards before my
nerves would steady down a bit. And I used to
have the same shakiness of my nerves when I woke
up from whatever little sleep I chanced to get,
which, by-the-way, was always punctuated with
the most awful dreams.”
“Have you any idea what caused your head.
pains and other ailments ? ”
“ I think it was all through receiving a sOnstrolce
when a girl at my home in Goulbum, for the-
doctor said I would always suffer with nay head,
and the older I got the worse it would be. Other
doctors treated me afterwards, and they all. said
what a dreadful state my nerves were in, and also-
that my digestion was as bad as it could be ; and
the unfortunate part of the whole affair was that
their physics could not help me any. Powders,
medicines, pills galore, and no beneficial result
whatever— not, I can say with truth, up to the-
period when I purchased some Clements Tonic
through a friend’s advice, and then, fo;c the first
time during my long illness, I learned what it was
to get some substantial ease from my torments.
At first I thought the relief would be only short-
lived. It seemed too much to expect anything
else after the disappointments of the past; but
Clements Tonic soon put an end to that pessimis-
tic idea by making me feel better and stronger
every day, and when I realised that my improve-
ment was making such regular strides, my heart
was overflowing with gratitude. It is only right
you should know that besides the medicines my
doctors had ordered I had taken a large variety of
other mixtures bought from chemists, and when
Clements Tonic was the only one amongst the lot
that could do me good it is but just and proper
that the truth should be admitted. Nothing I had
ever tried before could make me eat like Clements
Tonic did, and I assure you my appetite had been
very poor for months, and the ease that came to
my head after a few bottles’ use of that remedy
was something to make me thankful for the rest
of my days. The pains in that quarter eventually
subsided altogether, and the difference in my life
then, compared to what it had been, was just the
difference existing between night and day. Con-
tinuing with Clements Topic ! lost the, burden-
some feelings that affected me after taking food,
and soon the windy spasms that had often doubled
me up were quite cured. It had been my unhappy
lot to have a feeling in the chest as if I had
swallowed something hard and immovable —some-
thing that kept the breath back' and almost
smothered me, but Clements Tonic did away with
that, too, and also gave me permanent relief from
the pains that used to locate themselves between
my shoulders, and about the small of my back.
My eyes had always felt dull and swollen, and I
used to be irritable and anxious from morning till
night, fearing that something was going to happen;
but, strange as it may seem, after using a few
bottles of Clements Tonic my mind became as
free from anxious thought as a babe’s. Jilt gave
me a sense af lightheartedness that had peen lost
to me for years, but such could only be Expected
to follow after so much good had been realised.
I am glad to say that Clements Tonic left
nothing undone, and it gave me a digestion that
has remained sound for many years past, and in
regard to nervousness—well, I hardly know how
to express myself. At all events, I can say that
my nervous weaknesses were soon overcome, and
if anybody suffers from sleeplessness there’s noth-
ing in this world they need take but Clements
Tonic to make their nights peaceful and re-
freshing. That remedy succeeded in giving me
perfect health, and I would rather have a bottle
of Clements Tonic in the house than anything
else ; so would my husband.”

Business, Robbery, etc.
A professional bailer-out turned up in a recent
Melbourne court case. His fee was 10s. for a £2O bail
until the sessions. As the bailee had broken the stipula-
tion to call on him personally once a day, and had also
changed her address, he asked to have' his bond can-
celled, and was accommodated. Considering' the risks
he seems to have taken the job at cut rates.
•. • • •
In a recent shipping case at Newcastle (N.S. W.)
it was given in evidence that the stuff served out as
butter to the crew was a specimen of colored tallow.
The biscuits were full of maggots,.and mouldy. The cap-
tain, evidently a man of ideas, suggested putting them in
the oven and frying the maggots to death. The rice was
more weevils than rice, and was said to have been
spread out on the hen coop in the hope that the
chickens would pick Out the live stock. It was then
bagged up and served out to the crew. Most of the
butter was given to the pigs. The beans were horse
beans, and likewise went to the pigs mostly. The pigs
had a good time. Little wonder that the names of most
of the witnesses had a foreign twang. The Bull Dog can’t
breed on that sort of fare.
9 1 * *.
The Yankees have heard that Maoriland has
got an exhibition in progress, and some of them are on
the warpath to make a few dishonest dollars. There
arrived in Christchurch not long since an American per-
son who said he’d come to start work, please, and could
they show him his particular place of employment. His
letters set out that he was being drafted to look after a few
acres of exhibits at a monthly salary of one hundred dollars.
He would draw his return fare to the States, get free board,
and otherwise have a gorgeous time. Inquiry elicited
the fact that an alleged agent in New York had been lift-
ing many dollars from persons aching for billets in Christ-
church, and that the man with the promise of the
hundred-dollar billet was the forerunner of a crowd on the
water. He reckons by the time he gets back to New York
to have just enough cash to buy a box of cartridges.
“Philo”: Said the boot merchant: “Protec-
tion has done wonders for the boot trade. Within the
last five years Australian-made boots have improved re-
markably. Why, they now make boots in Victoria that
will beat most of the English and Canadian stuff out of
sight. But the Australians will have to pay more atten-
tion to the tanning of the leather. That’s the only fault
with the iocal stuff, the tanning. Once they get that
right, Australia will produce boots equal to anything in
the world. Still, under present circumstances, the
local boots are superior to most of the imported
stuff. It’s not the shopkeeper who’s against the
locally-made article. It’s a certain foolish, unpatriotic,
easily-gulled portion of the public. They seem to
have an idea that because a boot is Australian-
made it can’t; possibly be good, and if it’s stamped
‘Made in America’ it’s impossible for it to be bad
whereas, in reality, some of the shoddiest stuff manu-
factured comes from America. And you can’t even hint
to this kind of fool that he’s mistaken. They make me
tired. The funny part of it is that they can’t tell the
imported boot from the Australian when they see it,
unless it’s stamped. Often I’ve shown an Australian and
an American boot together, and have misrepresented the
Australian boot as an American, and vice versa. They
are always quite satisfied to buy the Australian boot
because I tell them it was made in America, in prefer-
ence to the American boot because they think it is made
in Australia, and they pay a higher price for it, too. There
is a certain Victorian firm that makes a very good boot
and conspicuously labels its boxes with the letters
‘ U.S.A.’ A while ago I was trying to persuade a
cantankerous anti-Australian customer to buy an Aus-
tralian boot in preference to an American one, assuring
him that in style, finish and durability the former was
quite as good as the imported article, while it had the
additional advantage of being cheaper. There were a
number of boxes strewn about the floor each containing
one boot; the other had been taken out for the
customer’s inspection. They were partly American and
partly Australian boots. The importing crank listened
impatiently while I held up the cause of Australia.
Suddenly he snatched the Australian boot from me with
one hand and dived into the Victorian firm’s box (with
the U.S.A. label) with the other, and holding both boots
by the heels in front of me, so that I could get a good
look at them, said vehemently. ‘Do you want to make
me believe that that boot can be compared in style and
quality with this one ? ’ And he shook one Australian
boot in my face with derision and held the other Aus-
tralian boot proudly at arm’s length. The boot he held in
the air was the fellow to the one he was shaking in my
face but he didn’t know it. He didn’t know anything. I
was ahead of him there; I knew he was a fool.”
AN ERA OF PROSPERITY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“An era of prosperity is now about to dawn ”
Shall be Australia’s watchword whilst she’s got a thing to
pawn;
“ Our Natural Resources have increased beyond a doubt ’■
Shall be quoted when we’re shoving those resources up
the spout;
And though the Rate of Interest must always be
deplored,
It’s wise to make a reference to the Confidence Re-
stored.
We must wax enthusiastic re the gold mines that we own
When approaching. Uncle Cohen for another little loan ;
And we’ll focus his attention on the fact that “ we possess
A rich and fertile continent,” though mostly in distress ;
And then we must remind him that the cash he lent
before
Was “ judiciously expended ” —but we need a little more-
Then the Backbone of the Country should be trotted
into view—
As a feature of the landscape he is not exactly new ;
But now we’ll introduce him with a waving of the hand
As a Primary Producer who is settled on the land.
We can praise his “ thrift ” and “ courage,” and the love
of “ honest toil,”
And the “ sturdy independence ” of the tiller of the soil.
Then statistics may be quoted (we can get them ready
made
From the Hbraud or the Argus) re the Volume of Our
Trade;
And perhaps we ought to whisper when he’s totting up
the score
That, although the cupboard's empty, we have- still the
Open Door ;
And if we do not own .a fleet, yet surely we can boast
Of the Home and Foreign Shipping to r be seen upon
our coast.
Should Uncle not reciprocate but answer with a frown,
Do not in well-doing; do not haul the standard
down.
Remember we are struggling for the land that gave us
birth—
For Australia, Great and Golden and the Greatest
Debt on Earth !
Shall this proud country weep because the callous Uncle
shirks
When asked to lend the money for her Reproductive
Works?
Nay, comrades, statesmen, patriots all, in humble prayer
combine
To requisition Cohen for the wherewithal to dine ;
For he’s an ever-present help—a never-failing friend,
And willing on our helpless kids his precious quids to
lend.
Ye patriotic parents, place your infants on the scale—
And cultivate another crop in time to catch the sale.
And loving mothers, think upon the happy times ahead
The hypothecated babies will enjoy when you are dead 1
For an Era of Prosperity shall dissipate the gloom
When Uncle Cohen sends along an Everlasting Boom.
Yea! the Period of Depression shall be permanently sunk
In a howling, raging sea of beer—Until the Beer is Drunk !
W.A.
TOM BEASLEY.

Nine-tenths of the ills which humanity endures, with more or less patience, are unnecessary i 4
For instance: —
Rheumatism
Gout
Neuralgia
Lumbago
Sciatica
Blood Disorders
Ancemia
Indigestion
Biliousness
Jaundice
Bright's Disease
Gravel
Stone
Bladder Troubles
General Debility
Sick Headache
are readily curable. One and all arise from the failure of the
KIDNEYS AND LIVER
t o efficiently perform their functions. These important organs, when acting healthily, deal with and
Remove from the system the poisonous matter which causes the disorders.
The Kidneys filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine every day. In this
quantity of urine are dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains in weight of uric acid, to-
gether with other animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce.
When the kidneys are in health, all this solid matter is in solution and is invisible. Directly the
kidneys, through either weakness or disease, become unfit to do their duty properly, a proportion of
this solid matter remains in the blood, becomes actively poisonous and causes us to suffer from uric
disorders such as Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Gravel,
Stone, Bladder Troubles, and Bright’s Disease. A simple test to make as to the condition of
the kidneys is to place some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass, and let it
stand until next morning. If it is then cloudy, or there is a brick-dust like sediment, or if particles
float about in it, or it is of an unnatural color, the kidneys are not healthy and no time must be lost iti
adopting remedial measures, or Bright’s' Disease, Diabetes, or some less serious but more painful ill-
ness will result.
The Liver. —In the liver various substances are actually made from the blood. Two or three
pounds of bile are thus made from the blood every day. The liver takes sugar from the blood, con-
verts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it to the blood, gradually,
as the latter requires enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is insoluble, into urea, which is
completely soluble, and the liver also deals with blood corpuscles which have lived_ their life and are
useful no longer. When the liver is inactive or diseased we suffer from Indigestion, Biliousness,
Anaemia, Sick Headache, and Blood Disorders.
The health of the liver and of the kidneys is so closely connected that it is almost impossible for
the kidneys to be affected, and the liver to remain healthy, or vice versa.
It is nearly thirty years since scientific research directed specially to diseases, of the Kidneys and
Liver was rewarded by the discovery of the medicine now known throughout the world as
Warner’s Safe Cure.
It was realised, at the outset of the investigation, that is was necessary to find a curative agent which
would act equally upon the kidneys and upon the liver, these organs being so immediately associated
in the work of dealing with the body’s waste material, and, after many disappointments, the medicine
which possessed the required action in the fullest degree was at length discovered. Warner’s Safe
Cure cures all diseases of the kidneys and liver, and, by restoring their activity, these
vital organs are enabled to rid the body, through the natural channels, of the urinary and biliary
poisons, the presence of which, in the system, are the cause of Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia,
Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Blood Disorders, Anaemia, Indigestion, Biliousness, Jaundice, Sick
Headache, Gravel, Stone, Bladder Troublesand General Debility. Warner’s Safe Cure cures all these
disorders simply by removing the cause of the disorder. This is the reason why cures effected by
Warner’s Safe Cure are permanent cures. [1
Adieu to Nature.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At the annual sessions of the Women’s Christian Tem-
perance Union a report was read from the Purity Depart-
ment, in which was condemned, amongst other things,
high art pictures, so called, from models of fallen sisters,
as possessing a bad influence over the minds of young
people.— Melbourne Argus.
aO, Rose, and dress thyself ! Alas ! No more
Shall artists gaze upon thy splendid curves;
The dreary raven croaks without the door;
The searching gimlet bores the wall —nor swerves.
Thy pink-tipped breast—a rosebud on the snow—
Doth envious make these breastless ones and lean;
Cover thy nakedness, dear Rose, and go
Unto a laundry, and be no more seen !
The frigid Virgins of the Earth make moan
That artists gaze upon thy loveliness ;
Go. get thee, Florence, to thy corset zone
And blush all over as you hook your dress.
Cano van Venus, or of Milo, hide
Thy legs in stockings and mysterious frills ;
Let wicked man but himself sit beside,
And to the desert take his amorous thrills.
The beauty of thy white columnar neck
Alone shall tell, dear Grace, of what I’ve seen;
That wondrous bosom, guiltless of a speck ;
That skin of marble, white and silken sheen ;
The swelling hips that do distinguish thee,
Make hatred of thee, dear, in hearts of snow.
No more clean nudity for Art can be ;
So blush, dear Grace, and dress thee quick, and go !
Oft have T seen in Europe’s galleries,
Where all the nasty prurient tourists roam.
And spend their venom and their salaries,
And teach the French the morals made “at
Home” —
The pictures draped, to ease their agitations ;
The statues clothed, and doing as they’re bid,
And all the Venuses in combinations ;
And bright tin fig-leaves that no salience hid.
Go, Phyllis ! put thy shapeless bloomers on,
And with their hideous foldings hide thy shape;
For line of beauty—substitute chiffon !
For wine and roses take cold tea and crape !
No more shalt thou in nature’s nudeness pose,
For form and flesh tints —to a Bougereau —
Encase thy wondrous legs in cotton hose—
Go, get thee to a laundry, Phyllis, go !
And when Art’s beauties leave our saddened lives,
Sweet love shall go with them, and not return ;
We’ll buy our staid and unromantic wives,
They’ll never be a passion that shall burn ;
Romance of literature shall perish so —
(He’s sinful who the tender passion feels)
And marriages be made the earth below—
Hot as the wedding of two conger-eels.
The lyrics of the Holy Book must die.
Forgotten be the Solomonic song—
He sings of necks like towers of ivory.
Of breasts like young twin roes; and such are
wrong—
Of jewel-jointed limbs —of eyes like pools
In Heshbon ; honeyed lips, and skins of silk.
Oh ! listen to the words of these dull fools,
And spill your wine, and drench yourself with
milk.
Helen ! I dreamed of thine historic charms !
The hues and tints that now I may not see !
The curve of jutting breasts and outstretched
arms
That now must always hidden be from me.
Forget the starlit river ’neath the hill,
Where thine hair did my face and heart enmesh —
The night and thee I do remember still,
The wine-like air about thy golden flesh !
But, they decree ! And now the last is said !
What artists may not paint I may not dream !
Beauty and'life and laughter all are dead,
The starlit river is a Stygian stream.
Florence, and Rose, and Grace, and Phyllis—all!
Helen, whose flesh-tints are of Bougereau—
Dead must ye be, so let us drop the pall!
Go get ye to a laundry—dress and go !
VIC.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
A Traveller’s Tale.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ "V » 7 HAT’S yours?” lasked my friends.
\ \ The Commercial Traveller, who had
been looking at my cards over my
shoulder, availed himself of this heaven-sent
opportunity. “ Mine’s a whisky and soda,” he
informed the barmaid. She nodded and waited
for the legitimate orders.
“You ought to get on in your business,” said I
to the Commercial Traveller.
“I intend to,” he replied. “I think I’ve found
my vocation this shot.”
“Why, I should have thought you’d been bred
up to it from infancy,” broke in Reach, as the
girl disappeared. “You’ve such an abnormal
hi—er —readiness. You know what you want,
and ask for it. If you will permit me to say it,
sir—you beat the band.”
“You bet !” answered the Commercial Travel-
ler, smiling. “But I was not brought up to this
game. Look at me. What d’ye think I was
doing a year ago ? Me! ” His superb gesture in-
cluded his American boots, his gold-filled watch-
chain, and his brazen face.
“ Breaking stones,” hazarded Reach.
The Commercial Traveller laughed. “You have
a pretty wit, old chappie,” he declared. “But
seriously. No ! you’d never guess. I was a
showman, my dear fellow. I —was —a—show-
man. Think of that. And a showman of what,
you will ask.”
“ We won’t,” snapped Reach. “ It’s your deal,
Jones.”
The Commercial Traveller leaned over and
whispered loudly in my ear. “Fleas,” he said.
“Performing fleas! Mel . . .” He deserted
melodrama and spoke aloud. “I’ve taken a
liking to you, dear boy, and——no, no, trump it;
the unpleasant-tempered person to your right has
none—remember he played his king three tricks
back. Ha ! you wouldn't take my tip
“ But about those fleas —funny beggars, fleas—
you wouldn’t believe their intelligence. I read in
the paper, the other day, about the brain of an
ant. I tell you, the brain of an ant is a second-
class, brummy affair against a flea’s. Can you
catch a flea in the dark ? I guess not. Can you
catch an ant ? Every time. Well then ! ”
Reach threw down his cards with threefold
words. “Is it whist, or is it entomology?” he
inquired.
“It appears I have no choice,” I answered
meekly. „
“Your friend has a shocking bad temper, said
the Commercial Traveller as Reach banged the
door. We sat fascinated by his colossal impu-
dence. He smiled affably, and began on the flea
subject again.
“Fleas,” he remarked, “are like fire, or
whisky, or women, or horses —good servants, but
bad masters. They’re good shut up in a glass-
topped pill-box, but loose on the country-side
they’re just infernal nuisances. I saw this directly
I studied the subject. When I was a boy I used
to train the young fleas in the way they should go.
I found that after they’d been under a glass roof
for a few days they gave up jumping. When they
discovered that jumping brought ’em sore heads
they’d quit. I know men with less sense than
that. I got those fleas so they reckoned the
universe had a glass lid, and you could pick ’em
up without them trying to jump. ‘ This is the
beginning of wisdom,’ thought I. ‘l’ll see if I
can’t continue their education.’ I did, and so you
must behold me in the first flush of youth—the
shave-twice-a-week stage—touring with a small
but select company of a dozen or so fleas—shining
fat chaps, as big as linseed. I had little
hansoms and mill-wheels and cannons made of
fine cardboard, and the biz. paid pretty well.
I took ’em round the Agricultural Shows, and the
rustic youth would gaze upon them at a penny a
time. Often I’ve thought, when I saw the gaping
mouths of said youth, what a fortunate thing my
company hud forgotten the art of jumping. Some
people would take a bob’s-wortli at a time.
“ My star performance was the drill of a gun-
crew of seven fleas. The captain was a clever
little beggar. He had a little wooden sword, and
used to wave it to give the others the office what
they were to do. When they’d gone through the
loading drill, he’d drop his sword and pull the
lanyard. He got so smart at last that I used to
let him run the show when I was called away,
and, as soon as he heard a brown fall into the
money-box above the ring, he’d start the per-
formance on his own. He used to know the
different sounds of buttons and card-counters too,
and the hayseeds couldn’t even beat him by drop-
ping a halfpenny in the box.
“ I got real fond of him. He got to answer to
his name, Willie, and to cock his eye up at me
like a poll-parrot when I spoke to him. When I
got other fleas he’d train ’em for me. The ones
that showed promise he’d draft off to their different
quarters; the ones that didn’t he’d whack with
his sword for two or three days and then, looking
up from his recruit drill, shake his head. He
trained a dozen of ’em to hunt whales in a little
cardboard boat—the whales were caterpillars, and
it was a lively performance. He taught some to
do the toreador act in the bull-ring. The bulls
were ants, and they always got the worst of it.
He got up a cricket team—he was a regular
Trumper himself ; and he used to play full-back
in Rugby as well as ever I’ve seen the Maori-
landers do it. Gentlemen, he was a pearl. I tell
vou I loved that flea. Yes, you may laugh, but
I did. .
“ I had some queer experiences with my show.
Once 20 of the new recruits mutinied, and it took
all Willie’s firmness to keep ’em from deserting.
And once we were stopped by a fool Customs
officer in Maoriland, and the fleas spent a night
in quarantine while he looked up authorities.
Wasn’t sure whether they were reptiles or tools-
of-trade, he said. I told him they were tools-of-
trade since I made my living by them; but he
held that, even though they were tools of trade,
they didn’t cease to be reptiles, and as such were
liable to a 200 per cent, duty ad valorem. Then,
when I explained the difference between reptiles
and insects, he fell back on the contract-labor
clause. I had a deal of trouble convincing him
that, as my company were paid higher wages than
the native Union rates, they couldn’t possibly
compete with it. But when he proposed to apply
the education test, I gave him half a thick-un,
and we got through. Willie felt the humiliation
of being classed as a reptile very keenly.
“ And once when an epidemic struck the show,
Willie dosed it with homopterous medicine
and”
“ Homoeopathic ? ” interjected Jones.
‘ ‘ Ah, yes—slip o’ the tongue. Little pills and
things, you know. Well, anyway, h 9 cured the
company. For three years I toured Maoriland,
until at last I lost Willie, a martyr to duty.
“ I was showing before a Maori chief, Pakeha
te Wakatipu—you’ve probably heard of him. He
was the chief, or pah, who, when asked by the
British Commander during the war whether he
had a mouth on him, cried out, * Ake, ake, ke
ake / ’ It’s a sort of catch-word now, and the All
Blacks yell it when they take the field. But I
suppose these Maori legends don’t interest you—
you want to have lived amongst ’em like I have,
and then they appeal to you.
“Well, old Te Wakatipu’s chief whare, or wife,
' who was so important she had a carved wahine—
a hut, you know—to herself, came with the other
whores to see the show. She was a fine lump of
a woman, weighing about 25 stone, and the old
chief was real proud of her.
“ Now, while Hinemoa —her name, you know—
was leaning over the little ring, one of the young
fleas, a dreadful chap for the fair sex, spotted a
young Maori she-flea hopping about on her
tohimga, or necklet of moa plumes. Fleas fall in
love very quick, like Dagos, and in one bound he
was beside the object of his affections. Willie
saw him, and jumped to secure him. They all
disappeared under the tohunga, and Hinemoa
started scratching her back like fury. I was in
agony, gentlemen—agony. I told Hinemoa to
quit scratching, and she replied, ‘To Helle te
stop, te prea too much tiggle.’ Then I told old
Wakatipu that I’d have to search Hinemoa,
that the flea was worth pounds to me; but he got
shirty, and waved his greenstone kea, and yelled
‘ Tutanekai te akinehi, wairarapa ke sercha te
plurry fat ol’ kau.’ Which means, gentlemen,
‘ He who dareth to search my queen had better
mind his eye.’ I saw it was all up, and left the
presence of Te Wakatipu with what dignity I
could command; but, once outside, I am not
ashamed to admit that' I wept. Yes, dear boys,
I am not an emotional man, but I wept.
“ That is not all. That night I was awakened
by a tickling on my left eyelid. I caught the
marauder, and, expecting to see one of my
company, turned up the gas. It was Willie, but,
alas, dying. He had hung on to the deserter,
and brought him back two miles. Hinemoa had
scratched off three of his legs and broken his
spine.”
Jones interjected.
“Insects haven’t any spine? Well, anyway,
she’d broken the place where his spine would
have been if he’d had one. He died at two
o’clock, and I handed the body over to the grief-
stricken company to bury with military honors.
They gave him a gorgeous funeral, and sentenced
the deserter to death by court-martial. It takes
a long while to hang a flea —this one lived for
three weeks, but at last died, defiant to the end.
“The show, without Willie’s guiding hand,
went to ruin. The cricket team made blobs and
muffed catch’es; the gun-drill got slack; the
caterpillars capsized the whale-boat; and the
ants killed the toreadors in the bull-ring. And so
now you see me travelling. I’m travelling, by
the way, for a new Automatic Cradle Company
What? None of you married. Well, take my
advice and get married, and buy one of our
cradles. They’re the thing. Eh? Yes don t
mind if I do.” .
He brooded sadly over his drink ; then, with a
deep sigh, rose and made sail for the bar.
Jones spoke. “What a frightful liar! he
said. , ,
“ Yes,” agreed Craig. “ But he seems to know
the Maori lingo pretty well.”

Indigestion is one of the most frequent, painful and disease-
producing complaints which afflict mankind to-day. It consists
of an inability of the stomach to digest the food given to it.
The symptoms are very numerous, the most noticeable being a
feeling of heaviness in the chest, a disagreeable taste in the
mouth, a dull pain in the head, loss of appetite, and a pale and
sallow complexion. Bile Beans cure all forms of Indigestion.
They aid the stomach to properly digest the food, and expel all
waste matter from the system.
12 YEARS’ SUFFERING ENDED .
“ About nineteen years ago,” says Mr. Henry
Speer, a blacksmith, of Lynch-street, Young,
N.S.W., “ I first felt the symptoms of Indi-
The pains I experienced were terrible,
and would completely double me up.
I consulted doctors. They all agreed
it was an aggravated form of Indi-
gestion, and one told me that I would
not live four hours unless I got ease
from the pain. Another doctor ad-
vised operating. All the doctors
could do for me was to administer
morphia and opium to deaden the pain. I was under the
doctors’ hands for many years, attending them regularly
until my patience was exhausted. About seven years ago,
hearing that Bile Beans had cured many cases of Indi-
gestion, I decided to give them a fair trial. After having
taken them regularly for a month or six weeks, I noticed a
great improvement in my condition. Thus en-
couraged, I persevered with the Beans, and was
effectively and thoroughly cured. It is now five
years since I had an attack of Indigestion, which I
think can be taken as a guarantee that the cure is
permanent. Where life was once a burden, it is now
a pleasure, and altogether I feel a different man. I
have now been a resident of Young for seven years,
and am consequently well known.”

Johnson’s Wonder.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I’D been right round by overlands to see the
world and life,
And on the boat at Plymouth I met Johnson
and his wife;
He was a man who knew the world and wore the
know-all smile—
Hio wife a silky pussy cat—the soft, obedient style.
His constant source of comfort was his life was
all serene,
His ceaseless source of wonder was that “men
could be so green.”
There were two women of the world whom John-
son knew by sight,
The one as fair as Southern dawn ; the other dark
as night—
They played each other off, you see, as many
sisters do—
They travelled with their “ uncle dear ” (their
“manager ” —a Jew);
To us the make-up and the game were plainly to
be seen,
And Johnson wondered how on earth most men
could be so green.
At Naples, as at Genoa, they left no room for
doubt;
They took down the chief officer and cleaned the
captain out.
We saw it as outsiders do—those seamen looked
so mad—,
’Twas managed so that neither knew the other
had been had.
Such games are known to all who sail for gain or
change of scene,
And Johnson wondered how in air such men
could be so green.
You know Port Said, by Suez Lane, and no doubt
you’re aware
What sort of carnivals are held ashore on Sunday
there.
And “Uncle,” being far from well, and voting
ports a bore,
The doctor and a Johnnie took the lovely pair
ashore.
They had a glorious, gay old time (you know which
pair I mean),
And Johnson wondered how the deuce an ass
could be so green.
There was a third-class passenger who acted like
an earl,
Spent something over thirty pounds upon a
Southern girl.
They’d met aboard, and soon the news went round
from mouth to mouth,
They were engaged to marry when they reached
the Sunny South.
She chucked him at Fremantle, and she didn’t
think it mean,
And J ohnson wondered how in life a boy could be
so green.
There was another married pair whom Johnson
did despise ;
She carried on most scand’lously before her hus-
band’s eyes.
He fetched and carried all day long as to the
manner born,
And plain to all on board but him, was her
conceited “scorn.”
And Johnson bossed his wife to show the difference
between
Some husbands—wondering how the smoke a man
could be so green.
Now, Mrs. Johnson, through it all, was anything
but slack;
I'd seen her sleepy-meaning smile behind her hus-
bands back,
I’d heard the whispered covered sneer, the stress
on “ him ” and “ he.”
She spread her charms for all men, from the
captain down to me.
She used her drooping eyes pn all while Johnson
smoked serene, ,
And chuckled secretly because the world was very
green.
He got a wire from Adelaide—on business, under-
stand—
And left his wife to follow round, and journeyed
overland.
That very night, while musing in a quiet spot on
deck,
I caught her with her arms about a first-class
Johnnie’s neck.
The Johnsons live at Manly now, their lives are
all serene,
And J ohnson still is wondering how the world can
be so green.
N.s.w.
HENRY LAWSON.
A Bye-Product of the Cow.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE trouble started on the Monday
morning. Hendry’s boy had managed,
after a desperate race, to get in before
Matthew’s boy. They were the last comers at
the creamery that morning, and, being so, of
course came in for a very large supply of froth
and water, and a correspondingly small supply of
legitimate skim milk. The final comer of all was
the unfortunate who always scored freely with
those undesirable commodities, and there was
naturally great rivalry for second last place. To
return home with a load of dirty water, in which
floated several lumps of refuse from the separator
bowl, brought down on the unfortunate a tirade
of abuse, if nothing worse.
Matthew’s boy, with commendable good nature,
offered' to pump Hendry’s boy’s milk for him,’
while he (Hendry’s boy) backed up his cart!
Hendry’s boy, far from being grateful for the
proffered assistance, inquired if Matthew’s boy
“sore enny green in me heye.” Matthew’s boy,
his face suffused with blushes at the other’s
ingratitude, and his red hair bristling aggres-
sively under his tattered cap, replied that there’d
be black in them before long if a certain party
didn’t be a bit more civil. Hendry’s boy shrilly
accepted the implied challenge, and advised Mat-
thew’s boy to say his prayers and make his will. But
he continued pumping, and Matthew’s boy noted
with dismay, that the skim milk in the reservoir
was gradually getting lower. In a voice full of
emotion he requested Hendry’s boy to knock off
and leave him a drop. But Hendry’s boy had
become absorbed in the beauty of the landscape
and pumped away, apparently unaware of the
other’s request.
He suddenly came back from his dreamy con-
templation of Bandbury’s haystacks. Matthew’s
boy, with a wild cry, had grabbed the pump-
handle and clung tenaciously to it. Hendry’s
boy, with herculean efforts, endeavored to move
it up and down. The pump, for the greater con-
venience of suppliers, was fixed on a stage, some
four feet from the ground, and with Matthew’s
boy and Hendry’s boy the staging was somewhat
crowded.
A gurgling noise inside announced that the last
of the milk had gone through the separator, and
would immediately be followed by two or three
gallons of water to cleanse the bowl. It was a
time for haste. With a determination worthy of
a greater cause, Matthew’s boy struggled for the
possession of the pump. With one hand he held
the handle, and, with the other clenched tight,
rubbed his knuckles over Hendry’s boy’s fingers
where they grasped the tail of the implement.
Hendry’s boy grabbed Matthew’s boy’s hair and
tugged vigorously at that adornment. Then,
with one accord, they let go the pump-handle,
and, uttering shrill cries, embraced each other.
For a moment or two they swayed in each other’s
arms, and then Matthew’s boy tripped and fell up
against one of Hendry’s cans full of skim-milk,
and upset it. It rolled over the side of the stage
and landed top-foremost on the ground below,
bashing in that part so much that it looked like a
concertina. The crash brought out the creamery
manager, who first dragged the combatants apart,
and then bumped them together, and finally threw
them away. With a rueful countenance, Hendry’s
boy picked up his dead milk-can and, in a de-
jected manner, proceeded to load up his cart;
while Matthew’s boy, with a worried look, pumped
a thin stream of froth and water into his receptacle.
Next morning, two angry and wild-looking
fathers arrived at the creamery in com-
pany with their boys. They were some-
what earlier than usual. The unenviable
position of absolute last was held by Dad Smith.
It was clear to everybody that there was battle in
the air. Matthew and Hendry burst through the
gate almost side by side, and, in the rush to get
their milk off and weighed first, they nearly came
to blows. Matthew’s horse, jerking to one side,
knocked the end of the shaft into Hendry’s horse’s
ribs. Hendry swore Matthew did it on purpose,
and, but for the timely arrival of Dad on the
scene, they would very likely have got into
“holts.’’ Dad’s presence had a deterrent effect,
and they made haste to get their milk delivered
before the patriarch backed in. Nobody took
any chances when Dad was about
Dad looked disappointed when he found he was
the last. Also he looked thoughtful. But he sat
on the edge of the stage, and talked to Denis
O’Grady about the potato crop. Matthew’s boy
and Hendry’s boy came along. Old Hendry sat
in his cart, and glared savagely from his battered
milk-can to Matthew and back again to the can.
Matthew returned the look with interest. It just
required a match to start an explosion. Dad
gazed benignly at the two youngsters.
“Which of those lads do you think’s the
strongest, O’Grady?” he remarked, in a loud
voice. The boys cocked up their ears.
“Faith, I dunno,” answered O’Grady.
“Well, I’m not sure myself,” said Dad, “but I
rather fancy young Hendry is. There’s the
makings of a fine lad in young Hendry.”
Hendry’s boy swelled out his chest and gazed
proudly at Matthew’s boy.
“Here, you young shaver,” cried Dad, patron-
isingly to Hendry’s boy, “ which of you youngsters
is the strongest?”
“Me,” answered Hendry’s boy, with a look of
superiority.
“You ain’t,” retorted Matthew’s boy.
“I ham,” replied Hendry’s boy, grandly, “I
ken fight yer one hand kneelin’.”
Dad glanced admiringly at Hendry’s boy.
“ Well,” he remarked loudly to O’Grady, “he’s
got plenty of grit in him, anyhow. I like a boy
like that.”
Hendry’s boy drank in these honeyed words.
“I’ll fight yer now, I will,” he said to Matthew’s
boy, with a side glance at Dad, as the fount of
admiration.
“Game lad, that,” said Dad in a loud aside to
O’Grady.
Matthew’s boy did not at first accept the chal-
lenge, and the head of Hendry’s boy swelled
visibly. He was drunk with old Dad’s apprecia-
tion, and rushed in where angels fear to tread.
He said “Yah,” and shook his head, and told
Matthew’s boy that he was red-headed. Then,
seeing the light of appreciation brighten in Dad’s
eye, he smote Matthew’s boy on the nose, and
said “Coward’s blow.” And again he said
“Yah.”
Next moment Hendry’s boy was down, and
Matthew’s boy was sitting on him and pounding
him ferociously. Hendry’s boy had gone under
without striking a blow. He was calling out
wildly, “ Lemme go.” Matthew’s boy only jogged
up and down on Hendry’s boy, and smote him
again. Hendry’s boy shouted, “Fa-ther, he’s
killin’ me.”
Hendry lea,ped from his cart, seized Matthew’s
boy, cuffed his ears, and propelled him into space.
The flying form of his offspring struck Matthew
just as he was dismounting from his cart. Mat-
thew threw his offspring aside, and lashed out
furiously at Hendry.
If there is one good thing recorded to Dad’s
credit, it is that, at this crisis in his life, he strove
to act the part of peacemaker. True, he hastened
to the skim-milk pump with his cans, but at the
same time he lifted up his voice (not very high),
and urged Hendry and Matthew to cease
their disturbance and come and get their
milk. He lifted up his voice again (not very
high) after each can was filled. Finally he
backed up old Blossom, and loaded his six cans
full of pure skim milk, and remarked to Pat
Horan, in a sorrowful voice, as he drove out of
the creamery yard, that it was a pity men like
Hendry and Matthew couldn’t get on without
quarrelling.
Then the fire died out of the disturbance, and
the crowd dwindled and drove away, and two
surprised men proceeded to share the froth and
water and the lump of foreign matter that always
fall to the last comer.
